# APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS — Complete Sales Training Content

## About This Document

This document contains the complete training curriculum for Apex Foundation Specialists sales representatives, covering foundation repair, waterproofing, and related services in the Florida Panhandle and South Alabama region.

The content is extracted from presentations and field reference materials and is organized to serve as a comprehensive training resource for:
- New representative onboarding and orientation
- Sales process methodology (7 core modules)
- Technical job type education (5 job types)
- Reference tools and objection handling
- The existing HTML-based training application

All content is designed to be imported into an HTML-based sales training web application for interactive learning and field reference.

---

## SECTION 1: CORE SALES PROCESS

#### Slide 1

MODULE 01


SALES PROCESS

FLORIDA PANHANDLE  ·  SOUTH ALABAMA

For Internal Training Use Only

### Notes:
COVER SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESThis is Module 1 of the Apex Foundation Specialists Sales Process training curriculum. It covers the three steps that happen before the rep ever rings the doorbell — and makes the case that these steps are where close rates are won or lost.Frame this module with a direct challenge to the team: most reps think the sale begins when they walk in the door. Top closers know the sale begins the moment they confirm the appointment. Every decision made in the 48 hours before an appointment — whether to confirm decision-makers, how to frame the logistics call, how to open the warm-up — determines more about the outcome than anything that happens in the last 30 minutes.The three phases in this module each have a specific job. The 2-Day Out Call locks in the people and the time commitment before anyone wastes a drive. The 30-Minute Out Call controls the physical environment and pre-commits the customer to a structured conversation. The Warm-Up and Agenda establishes who is running the appointment — and the answer needs to be the rep, not the homeowner.Remind the team that this module is the foundation the rest of the curriculum builds on. A rep who skips these steps and goes straight to the inspection is playing a harder game than they need to.

#### Slide 2

The Pre-Inspection Phase
Three steps that set up every successful appointment
Everything before you ring the doorbell determines whether you close. These three touchpoints build trust, establish control, and ensure you're walking into the right room with the right people.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  MODULE 1 — PHASE OVERVIEW

### Notes:
PHASE OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESUse this slide to orient reps before diving into each step. The core message to deliver: most reps think of pre-appointment activity as logistics — confirming a time, getting an address. That framing undersells what these three steps actually accomplish.Each of these calls does real selling work before the rep is even in the room. The 2-Day Out Call qualifies the opportunity — it ensures the rep is driving to an appointment worth making. The 30-Minute Out Call controls the opening dynamic of the visit — it decides whether the rep starts from a position of structure or scrambles to establish one after walking into a chaotic situation. The Warm-Up and Agenda establishes the rep as a professional running a professional process, not a salesperson trying to work a pitch.The research callout at the bottom is worth spending time on. Decision-maker confirmation is not just a nice-to-have — it is the single most predictive variable in whether a high-ticket home services appointment closes. A rep who consistently confirms all decision-makers will close a significantly higher percentage of their appointments than one who doesn't, all other things being equal. That fact alone justifies making the 2-Day Out Call a non-negotiable standard.

#### Slide 3

STEP  01

2-Day Out Call


Confirm · Qualify · Protect Your Time

Purpose: This is not a courtesy call — it is a qualification checkpoint. A two-minute conversation now prevents a wasted 3-hour appointment later. This call is where you protect your time and the customer's.

All DMs Present
Verify Scope




Confirm every decision-maker will be there. If a spouse, partner, or co-owner can't make it, offer to reschedule rather than proceed without them.
Confirm you can address their issue. Get enough detail to know you're the right company for this call before anyone drives anywhere.

Read the Customer
Time Commitment




Listen for urgency, skepticism, or distraction. Adjust your expectations and preparation accordingly before you arrive.
Ensure they've blocked enough time. Foundation inspections take 2–3 hours. A customer expecting 45 minutes is a customer who will rush your close.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 01 — 2-DAY OUT CALL

### Notes:
2-DAY OUT CALL — OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESTell reps plainly: this is the most undervalued call in the entire sales process. Most foundation companies don't make it at all. By making it consistently, Apex immediately signals a different level of professionalism to the customer before the visit has even started.On ALL DECISION-MAKERS — this is the single biggest close-rate lever in the pre-inspection phase. If a spouse or co-owner isn't home, the rep will almost certainly hear 'I need to talk to my husband/wife' at the close. That is not an objection — it is a structural problem created by not confirming earlier. The position to take with reps is clear: we would rather move the appointment than lose the job. Offer the reschedule with warmth — 'I want to make sure you have everything you need to make a good decision for your family — let's find a time when everyone can be there.'On VERIFY SCOPE — this protects everyone's time. Ask enough to know whether it's a crawl space or slab issue, whether it's moisture or structural movement, whether you have the right products for what they're describing. If it genuinely sounds outside your scope, say so and refer out. That honesty compounds into referral reputation over time.On TIME COMMITMENT — this is especially important for higher-ticket repairs like piers or full encapsulation systems. A customer who expects a 45-minute visit is mentally priced at a few hundred dollars. The 2–3 hour expectation shifts their mental frame before you arrive, which matters at the proposal stage.

#### Slide 4

STEP  01

2-Day Out — Call Script
Use this as a guide, not a script. Adapt tone to match the customer.
SCRIPT

YOU →
"Hi [Name], this is [Your Name] with Apex Foundation Specialists. I have you down for an inspection on [Day] — I wanted to touch base and make sure everything still works on your end."

GOAL →
Confirm the appointment is still on and they haven't forgotten or double-booked.

YOU →
"Will everyone who is part of making decisions about the home be able to be there? I want to make sure we can answer everyone's questions in one visit."

GOAL →
Get a direct yes or no on decision-makers. If someone can't be there: 'I want to make sure you get full value — should we find a time when [they] can join too?'

YOU →
"Can you tell me a little more about what you're seeing? I want to make sure I bring the right tools and that this is something we can genuinely help with."

GOAL →
Validate scope. Listen for crawl space vs. slab, water vs. structural, new vs. old issue. Note anything unusual for your prep.

YOU →
"Plan on setting aside about 2 to 3 hours — I'll do a full inspection and then sit down with you to go over everything in detail. Does that work with your schedule?"

GOAL →
Plant the time expectation. A customer who expects a 45-minute pop-in is mentally priced at $500, not $12,000.

YOU →
"Also — we do work with some great financing options if that ever comes in handy. Just something to keep in mind."



GOAL →
Plant the financing seed early and casually. This normalizes it long before the proposal, which reduces sticker shock at the close.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 01 — CALL SCRIPT

### Notes:
2-DAY OUT CALL — SCRIPT — SPEAKER NOTESDelivery: this call should feel warm and professional, not robotic. The rep is calling as a peer getting organized before a professional visit — not as an order-taker confirming a service window.On the decision-maker question: many reps skip this because it feels awkward. Have them practice it out loud until it sounds natural. The framing — 'I want to make sure we can answer everyone's questions in one visit' — turns a qualifying question into a customer-service statement. That reframe makes it easy to ask every time.On the time commitment: the specific point to drive home is that a rushed homeowner is a non-buyer. When a customer is watching the clock from the moment you arrive, everything is harder — the inspection, the education, the proposal, the close. The time expectation set here is not about logistics. It is about creating the mental space for a real decision to be made.On the financing seed: the reason to plant it here rather than waiting for the proposal is simple — when financing is introduced at the close in response to a price concern, it feels like a concession. When it's mentioned casually during the confirmation call as just a feature of doing business with Apex, it lands as a standard professional service. Same information, completely different psychological framing. Reps should drop this line naturally at the end of the call, not make a production of it.

#### Slide 5

STEP  02

30-Minute Out Call


Set the stage before you arrive

Purpose: Under 3 minutes. This call controls where you enter and pre-commits the customer to a structured sit-down — two things a cold arrival can never accomplish.

Ask Where to Park

Request a Sit-Down First




▸  Signals you are organized and considerate before you've said hello
▸  Ask if you can sit down together before beginning the inspection
▸  Starts the interaction with you asking a question — a subtle but real dynamic shift
▸  Pre-commits the customer to a structured conversation format
▸  Prevents an awkward first impression if you block a driveway or park on the wrong side
▸  Prevents the 'just go look at it' dynamic — inspecting cold with no rapport
▸  Gives you a natural opener on arrival: 'I parked out front like you suggested'
▸  Frame it: 'I like to hear your concerns first so I know exactly what to look for'



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 02 — 30-MINUTE OUT CALL

### Notes:
30-MINUTE OUT CALL — SPEAKER NOTESWhy reps skip this and why they shouldn't: this call feels trivial to most reps. 'I know how to park my truck.' That's entirely missing the point. This call is not about parking — it is about making your first physical interaction feel expected and orchestrated, not random.On asking where to park: this question does something subtle but powerful. It puts the customer in a helper role before you've even arrived. They're already invested in your visit going well. When you arrive and reference 'like you said,' it creates a small social proof signal that you listen and follow through. That signal compounds across the appointment.On the sit-down request: this is the more important piece of the two. Without it, many homeowners open the door and say 'it's out back, I'll show you.' And now the rep is in the crawl space before they've built any rapport, gathered the customer's story, or set an agenda. They're on the customer's terms rather than their own.When the rep asks 'can we sit down for a few minutes before I start so I can understand your concerns?' — almost every homeowner says yes. That yes is a micro-commitment to a structured conversation. It is much easier to control the appointment flow from that point forward.Suggested script: 'Hey [Name], it's [Your Name] with Apex — I'm about 30 minutes out. Quick question: where's the best place for me to park? And when I get there, would it be alright if we sit down for just a few minutes before I start? I like to understand your concerns first so I know exactly what to focus on when I'm in there.'

#### Slide 6

STEP  03

Warm-Up & Agenda


Build trust · Establish authority · Take the lead

Purpose: The warm-up converts a stranger into a trusted advisor in under 10 minutes. The agenda gives the customer a roadmap — which eliminates anxiety and puts you in control of the entire visit.

1
Introduce Yourself & Apex
2
Ask How They Found You




Name, role, how long you've been doing this work. Brief company credibility — years in business, local focus, service area. 20 seconds max.
Surfaces referral source and warms the conversation. If it's a referral, use that connection immediately. If Google, that's social proof.

3
Lay Out the Agenda
4
Set Benchmarks for Normal




Walk them through exactly what today will look like, step by step. Reduces anxiety and establishes you as the professional running the appointment.
Before you inspect anything, tell them what normal looks like — so they have a reference point when you show them what isn't normal.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 03 — WARM-UP & AGENDA

### Notes:
WARM-UP & AGENDA — OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESSet the context: by the time the rep sits down at the kitchen table, they are in the most important 10 minutes of the entire appointment. Everything that follows — the walk-around, the inspection, the education, the proposal — will be easier or harder based on how this moment lands.On introducing yourself and Apex: don't just say your name and ask where the crawl space is. Give them 20 seconds of credibility. 'I've been doing foundation work in the Panhandle for [X years]. Apex has been serving this area since [year] — we're local, we do our own work, and we stand behind everything we do.' Brief, but it lays the trust foundation that everything else builds on.On asking how they found you: this is not small talk — it's a rapport tool and a referral source tracker. If they were referred by a neighbor, use that connection. 'Oh you know the Johnsons on Maple? We did their crawl space last spring — great family.' That single sentence shifts the dynamic of the visit. The customer now experiences the rep as part of a trusted community, not a stranger knocking on the door.On laying out the agenda: this is where the rep takes the wheel. When you tell a customer exactly what today will look like before it happens, you're not just being organized — you're signaling that you've done this many times before and that they're in capable hands. The micro-yes at the end ('does that work for you?') locks them into the structure before the appointment formally begins.On setting benchmarks for normal: this is a trust move most reps skip entirely. When you tell a customer what normal looks like before you inspect anything, you are showing your hand — signaling that you're not going to invent problems. That honesty pre-empts suspicion at the close.

#### Slide 7

STEP  03

The Agenda — Word for Word
Delivered at the kitchen table before the inspection begins.

WHY IT WORKS
SCRIPT
"Thanks for having me in. Before I get started, let me tell you what today is going to look like.

First, I'd love to hear from you — you know this house better than anyone. Tell me what you've been noticing and how long it's been going on.

Then I'm going to do a complete inspection — interior, exterior, and the crawl space. I'll take detailed photos and measurements so we have documentation of everything.

When I'm done, I'll let you know and I'll need about 15 minutes to clean up and put some numbers together. Then I'd like to sit back down and go through everything I found.

I'll show you the photos, explain what's normal and what isn't, and if there's something that needs to be addressed, we'll talk about the best options for your situation.

The whole process usually runs about 2 to 3 hours. Does that work for you today?"
1.  Asking them first makes them feel heard — and gives you diagnostic intel before you inspect
2.  Listing interior, exterior, and crawl space raises perceived value before you've started
3.  The '15 minutes to clean up' framing previews the door knock so it never feels like a stall
4.  Mentioning photos builds evidence credibility before you've taken a single one
5.  'Does that work?' is a micro-yes that locks in their commitment to the full process



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 03 — THE AGENDA SCRIPT

### Notes:
THE AGENDA SCRIPT — SPEAKER NOTESPractice requirement: new reps should practice this script out loud — in the car, at home, wherever — until it sounds completely natural. The goal is not word-for-word memorization. The goal is that every beat lands as though the rep says it every day. Because soon they will.What this script accomplishes structurally:'Tell me what you've been noticing' — this is not small talk. It is the rep's best diagnostic data source. Every detail the customer shares is intelligence for the proposal conversation. The rep who listens well here has a significant advantage over the rep who rushes to the inspection.'Interior, exterior, and crawl space' — listing these explicitly raises perceived value before a single step has been taken. The customer now expects a thorough professional inspection, not a walk-around.'15 minutes to clean up and get numbers' — critical framing. Because the rep mentioned this now, the post-inspection door knock later won't feel like a delay. It will feel exactly like they promised. This is a trust brick laid at the beginning of the visit.'What's normal and what isn't' — this positions the rep as an educator before the first photo is shown. It signals an honest, calibrated assessment is coming, not a scare tactic.'Does that work for you today?' — this is a low-stakes yes, but it is a yes. The customer has committed to a structured process. They are far less likely to short-circuit it mid-appointment. Never skip this question.

#### Slide 8

STEP  03

The Trust Unlocking Moment
One sentence that disarms skepticism before it forms
After laying out the agenda, before asking 'does that work for you?' — add this. It is the single highest-trust move in the entire warm-up.
“

I'll be upfront with you — if I get in there and it turns out this isn't something we can help with, or the timing just isn't right for your situation, I'll tell you that honestly. My job is to give you an accurate picture of what's going on, not to sell you something you don't need.






Disarms Skepticism
Increases Credibility
Separates Apex from the Field
Homeowners expect a pitch. This sentence tells them they won't get one. Their guard drops before the inspection even begins.
Honesty about not overselling makes every finding you share afterward more believable. Real problems land with full weight.
Almost no competitor says this. You instantly become a trusted advisor — not another commission-driven rep.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 03 — THE TRUST UNLOCK

### Notes:
THE TRUST UNLOCKING MOMENT — SPEAKER NOTESWhy this is counterintuitive: many reps think that suggesting you might not be able to help is a sales-killer. The opposite is true. Here is the psychology behind it.Homeowners in 2025 are not naive. They have Google, Reddit, and friends who got burned by contractors. By the time the rep walks in the door, a significant portion of customers are on guard — expecting to be found to have a serious problem, quoted a large number, and pressured to sign today. This one sentence short-circuits all of that. It signals three things simultaneously: the rep is honest, the rep is confident, and the rep puts the customer's interests first.Delivery is everything: this should be delivered calmly and naturally — almost in passing, like it's just how the rep operates. If it sounds rehearsed or performative, the effect is lost. It needs to feel like a natural extension of the agenda-setting, not a formal announcement. A rep who says this with genuine conviction disarms skepticism completely. A rep who says it robotically just adds another line to a script.When to say it: this fits best right after describing what today will look like and before the final 'does that work for you?' It flows as the last piece of trust-building before the appointment formally begins.Field note: reps who add this line consistently report that customers visibly relax when they hear it. Watch for posture changes — shoulders dropping, leaning back slightly. That physical shift is the trust barrier going down. Everything that follows will land differently because of it. The close rate improvement from this single sentence is real and measurable.

A skipped step is a gift to the competitor who doesn't skip it. Consistency is the difference.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  MODULE 1 — PRE-INSPECTION CHECKLIST

### Notes:
PRE-INSPECTION CHECKLIST — SPEAKER NOTESUse this as a self-audit tool after every appointment — especially ones that didn't close. The pattern of skipped items almost always explains the outcome.The most common skips and their exact consequences:'All decision-makers committed' skipped → The rep reaches the close and hears 'I need to talk to my husband/wife.' The job is now a coin flip and the rep has zero control over the second conversation that happens without them.'Financing mentioned casually' skipped → Financing surfaces for the first time at the proposal stage in response to a price concern. It now feels like a concession rather than a standard service offering. The psychological framing is completely different.'Sit-down conversation pre-committed' skipped → Customer leads the rep straight to the crawl space. The rep inspects without rapport and writes a proposal for a stranger who never warmed up to them.'Trust-unlocking moment' skipped → Customer remains guarded the entire visit. Every finding is processed as a potential sales tactic rather than an honest professional assessment.'Benchmarks for normal established' skipped → When the rep shows problems during the photo review, the customer has no reference point. Everything looks alarming, which produces either panic or disbelief — neither of which helps close.Team exercise: have each rep score their last three appointments against this checklist and identify which items were incomplete on any job they didn't close. The pattern will almost always be immediately visible.

#### Slide 10

The close is won
before you arrive.



The pre-inspection phase is not a formality. It is the system that protects your time, builds trust before you walk in, and sets every appointment up to close.

Next Module: The Inspection Phase — Customer Walk-Around, Full Inspection & Door Knock

### Notes:
CLOSING SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESClose with the core message: the work done in the 48 hours before an appointment determines more about whether it closes than anything that happens in the last 30 minutes. Reps who master this phase have a structural advantage — they are playing a different game than the rep who just shows up and inspects.Leave the team with this thought: every decision made during the pre-inspection phase is a deposit into a trust account. The 2-Day Out Call deposits credibility and preparation. The 30-Minute Out Call deposits professionalism and control. The warm-up, the agenda, the trust unlocking moment — each one is another deposit. By the time the rep sits down to present the proposal, that account should be full. A full trust account makes the close feel like a natural conclusion rather than a negotiation.Team takeaway exercise: ask each rep to identify one item from the pre-inspection checklist they don't do consistently on every appointment and commit to building that single habit over the next five appointments. Behavior change happens one habit at a time. Don't overwhelm — focus.Preview of Module 2: the Inspection Phase picks up exactly where this module ends. The customer has been confirmed, the agenda has been set, trust has been established. Now the rep needs to do the most important work of the appointment — gather evidence that is thorough enough, documented well enough, and presented professionally enough to make the proposal undeniable.

#### Slide 1

MODULE 02


SALES PROCESS
The Inspection

FLORIDA PANHANDLE  ·  SOUTH ALABAMA

### Notes:
COVER SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESModule 2 picks up exactly where Module 1 left off. By the time a rep reaches this phase, they should have confirmed all decision-makers, delivered the full agenda, set benchmarks for normal, and delivered the trust-unlocking moment. If any of those steps were skipped, this phase will be harder — each module builds on the last.The three steps here — customer walk-around, full independent inspection, and post-inspection door knock — each have a specific psychological job beyond the obvious data-collection purpose. The walk-around makes the customer feel heard. The full inspection builds the evidence base. The door knock is a physical proof of effort that almost no competitor delivers. Taken together, they transform the rep from 'the guy who came to give a quote' into 'the professional who actually inspected our home.'Key framing for the team: most reps treat the inspection as the necessary work they do before the real sales conversation begins. That's backwards. The inspection IS the sales conversation. Every photo, every measurement, every minute the customer watches you work is building the case your proposal will rest on.

#### Slide 2

The Inspection Phase
Three steps that turn data collection into trust building
Most reps treat the inspection as a technical exercise. Top reps treat it as the most powerful sales tool in their kit. The way you gather information says everything about who you are and how much the customer can trust your findings.

01
02
03






Customer Walk-Around
Full Inspection
Post-Inspection Door Knock
Listen · Gather · Build rapport
Document · Measure · Photograph
Prove effort · Set the table



Key insight: The inspection is not just about finding problems — it is about building the evidence base that makes your proposal undeniable.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  MODULE 2 — PHASE OVERVIEW

### Notes:
PHASE OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESUse this slide to orient reps to the purpose of the module before diving into each step.The reframe to deliver here is critical: most reps think of the inspection as necessary work they do before the real sales conversation starts. That's backwards. The inspection IS part of the sales conversation. Every photo you take, every measurement you record, every piece of evidence you document is building the case that your proposal will rest on.A customer who watched you spend 90 minutes in their crawl space, emerge dirty, and sit down with organized photos and measurements is a fundamentally different customer than one who watched a rep glance around for 20 minutes and hand them a number. Evidence creates credibility. Credibility creates trust. Trust closes.Also worth noting: the inspection phase is where your competitors typically lose. Most foundation companies do cursory inspections. When Apex does a thorough one, customers feel the difference — even if they can't articulate exactly why.

#### Slide 3

STEP  01

Customer Walk-Around


Let them lead · Listen first · Begin gathering

Purpose: Before you form a single opinion, let the customer show you their home through their eyes. Their observations are diagnostic data you cannot get any other way — and feeling heard is the first step toward trusting you.

Let Them Lead
Start Documenting Now




Ask them to show you what they've noticed. Walk behind them. Don't jump ahead to areas you want to see — follow their sequence first.
Begin photographing during the walk-around, in front of the customer. This signals thoroughness immediately and starts building the evidence library.

Take Measurements
Listen More Than Talk




Begin gathering key readings as you go — moisture levels, floor slope, crack widths. Recording data in front of the customer builds credibility in real time.
Every detail they give you — how long it's been happening, whether it's gotten worse, what they've tried — is intelligence for your proposal.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 01 — CUSTOMER WALK-AROUND

### Notes:
CUSTOMER WALK-AROUND — OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESThe most important thing to communicate: this step is not passive. The rep is not just being polite by letting the customer lead — they are actively gathering intelligence while building rapport simultaneously.On letting them lead: The instinct for an experienced rep is to immediately go check the things they know matter — crawl space access, visible cracks, grade around the foundation. Resist that instinct. Let the customer take you where they want to go first. Two things happen: you learn what the customer is most worried about (which often drives their decision), and the customer feels respected and heard. That emotional state is worth as much as any measurement you take.On starting documentation during the walk-around: Many reps wait until the independent inspection to start their camera. Starting during the walk-around, in front of the customer, sends an immediate signal that you are systematic and serious. When a customer sees you photographing their concerns as they point them out, they feel those concerns are being taken seriously.On listening: Ask follow-up questions. 'How long have you noticed this?' 'Has it gotten worse over a particular season?' 'Have you done anything to address it?' These serve dual purposes — they surface diagnostic data and make the customer feel like an expert witness in their own case, rather than someone being managed through a sales process.Common mistake to call out: Reps who talk too much during the walk-around. If you are explaining the cause of every issue before you've finished the walk-around, you've lost the listening phase entirely. Save the education for the presentation. Right now your job is to hear everything they have to say.

#### Slide 4

STEP  01

Walk-Around — What to Ask
The right questions surface the right information
These questions do double duty: they gather diagnostic intelligence AND make the customer feel like the expert on their own home. Both outcomes matter equally.

"How long have you been noticing this?"
Establishes timeline — active vs. historic movement. Seasonal vs. progressive. Critical for scope.



"Has it gotten worse, stayed the same, or improved?"
Gauges rate of change. Progressive issues carry more urgency and justify faster action.



"Have you noticed anything else — doors sticking, cracks in drywall, uneven floors?"
Surfaces secondary symptoms the customer may not have connected to the foundation issue.



"Have you done anything to address it so far?"
Reveals prior repair attempts or other contractors — critical context for scope and trust.



"What's your biggest concern about it?"
Identifies the emotional driver — safety, home value, selling. Use this to frame the proposal.



"Is there anything else you want me to look at while I'm here?"
Closes the walk-around, ensures nothing is missed, and confirms the customer felt fully heard.





APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 01 — WALK-AROUND QUESTIONS

### Notes:
WALK-AROUND QUESTIONS — SPEAKER NOTESHow to use this slide in training: Have reps practice these questions out loud until they feel completely conversational. The goal is not to run through a checklist — it is to have a natural, curious conversation that happens to surface everything on this list.On 'How long have you been noticing this?' — This has a practical diagnostic purpose (active movement vs. old settled damage) but it also opens the customer up. People like to tell the story of their house. Give them that space.On 'Have you noticed anything else?' — This is often where the most valuable information surfaces. A customer who called about a wet crawl space may also mention that their back door has been sticking for a year — a structural symptom they never connected to foundation movement. That's a scope expansion hidden in a casual question.On 'What's your biggest concern about it?' — This is the most important question on the list. The answer tells you what to lead with in your proposal. A customer who says 'I'm worried about my family's safety' needs different framing than one who says 'I'm trying to sell the house in three months.' Same problem, different emotional driver, different proposal emphasis.On 'Is there anything else?' — Always end the walk-around with this. Never let a customer feel you rushed past something important to them. Even if they say 'no, that covers it,' asking the question confirms you were thorough and that their concerns drove the agenda.

#### Slide 5

STEP  02

Full Inspection


Independent · Systematic · Documented

Purpose: Now you conduct your own complete inspection — independent of the walk-around. The customer showed you their perspective. Now you build the objective evidence base that your proposal will rest on.

Exterior

Interior

Crawl Space






▸  Grade and drainage around the foundation
▸  Floors — slope, bounce, soft spots
▸  Moisture levels and humidity readings
▸  Downspout termination and runoff direction
▸  Doors and windows — sticking, gaps, alignment
▸  Standing water or active moisture staining
▸  Cracks in brick, block, or poured concrete
▸  Drywall cracks — diagonal, horizontal, vertical
▸  Wood degradation — beams, joists, sill plates
▸  Stair-step cracking in masonry
▸  Walls — bowing, leaning, ceiling separation
▸  Pier condition — settled, cracked, tilted
▸  Gaps at window and door frames
▸  Basement walls — cracks, staining, efflorescence
▸  Vapor barrier — presence, condition, coverage
▸  Evidence of previous repairs
▸  Evidence of water intrusion at slab or walls
▸  HVAC equipment condition and placement



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 02 — FULL INSPECTION

### Notes:
FULL INSPECTION — OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESFraming for reps: The full inspection is your independent professional assessment — separate from the walk-around. The customer showed you their perspective. Now you build yours from the ground up. You may find things they didn't notice or didn't think to mention. You may also confirm what they showed you with measurements and data that make it objective rather than subjective.On the exterior: This is where you read the story of the soil and drainage situation. Grade sloping toward the foundation, downspouts terminating too close to the house, and saturated soil are often the root cause of what's happening in the crawl space or basement. Photograph and document everything — even things not directly in your scope, because they're context for the customer's understanding of why the problem exists.On the interior: Don't skip the interior because 'the crawl space is where the work is.' Interior symptoms — sticking doors, drywall cracks, uneven floors — are often the most visceral evidence for the customer. They see these every day. When you connect those symptoms to what you found in the crawl space, the story becomes complete.On the crawl space: This is where most of your repair scope will come from. Take your time. Photograph everything systematically — go section by section, not just the worst area. A thorough crawl space photo set tells a story from access point to the far wall. Moisture readings, wood moisture content, humidity levels — record it all. Numbers in photos are more credible than verbal descriptions.General rule: when in doubt, photograph it. You can always choose not to show a photo in the presentation. You can never go back and take one you missed.

#### Slide 6

STEP  02

Documentation Standards
How you document is as important as what you document
Your photo and measurement set is the evidence base for your proposal. Treat it like a professional record — systematic, complete, and clear enough that the customer can follow along without needing you to explain every image.

Photography

Measurements




▸  Photograph systematically, not selectively. Go area by area — don't only shoot the worst spots.
▸  Floor slope readings — record in inches per foot. Note direction and which rooms are affected.
▸  Capture context before close-ups. Show where you are in the property before zooming in on damage.
▸  Wood moisture content — sill plates, beams, joists. Flag anything above 19% as elevated.
▸  Include a reference object — tape measure or hand — next to cracks to convey scale accurately.
▸  Relative humidity in the crawl space — note conditions and time of inspection.
▸  Photograph meter readings with the device visible in-frame. Numbers in photos beat verbal claims.
▸  Crack dimensions — width and length matter. Hairline vs. structural tells a very different story.
▸  Shoot before and during, not just the final problem. Show access conditions and general state first.
▸  Pier spacing and count — document layout so your repair scope is specific and defensible.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 02 — DOCUMENTATION STANDARDS

### Notes:
DOCUMENTATION STANDARDS — SPEAKER NOTESWhy this slide exists: Most reps know they need to take photos. Very few think about HOW to take photos in a way that makes their presentation compelling and their proposal defensible. This slide teaches that difference.On systematic photography: A customer who sees 40 photos organized by area — exterior, interior, crawl space access, crawl space sections — experiences something fundamentally different from one who sees 12 random close-ups. The systematic set tells a story. It says 'I looked at everything.' Random close-ups create doubt. The question the customer unconsciously asks is: 'Did they miss anything?'On context before close-up: Show the audience where they are before you show them the detail. A photo of a rotted sill plate means more when the customer can see it's in the southeast corner of their crawl space — not just a mystery piece of wood somewhere underground.On photographing meter readings: This is a trust move almost no rep does. When a customer sees a photo of a moisture meter reading 24% pressed against their floor joist, they don't question the number. When they just hear the rep say '24%,' there's a micro-doubt. Eliminate the doubt with the photo.On specific numbers: Emphasize that specific measurements are more persuasive than adjectives. 'Your floor has a 1.5-inch drop over 10 feet' hits differently than 'your floor slopes noticeably.' Numbers also protect the rep — if the customer later questions the scope, you have documented measurements to reference.

#### Slide 7

STEP  03

Post-Inspection Door Knock


Show the work · Deliver the promise · Set the table

Purpose: This is a deliberate, visible signal that you did real work — not a 15-minute walk-around. The moment the customer opens the door and sees you dirty from the crawl space, something shifts. You are no longer a salesperson. You are the professional who just spent 90 minutes under their house.

What to Do

Why It Works
▸  Knock on the front door — don't just walk back in
▸  Physical proof of effort.  No competitor who spent 20 minutes on-site can replicate this moment.
▸  Let them see you are visibly dirty from the crawl space
▸  Delivers on the agenda promise.  You said you'd come back when done. You did. Trust compounds.
▸  Confirm the inspection is complete
▸  Resets the dynamic.  The customer has been waiting and is now ready to listen.
▸  Ask for 15 minutes to clean up and organize your numbers
▸  Creates anticipation.  Asking for 15 minutes makes the presentation feel earned, not routine.
▸  Keep it brief — this is the transition, not the presentation
▸  Clean psychological break.  Separates the inspection from the presentation intentionally.
▸  Thank them for their patience if the inspection ran long



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 03 — POST-INSPECTION DOOR KNOCK

### Notes:
POST-INSPECTION DOOR KNOCK — SPEAKER NOTESThis is one of the most differentiated moments in the entire Apex process. Treat it that way in training — not as a logistical step, but as a deliberately engineered trust signal.The psychology behind it: Homeowners have a mental model of what a foundation 'inspector' looks like — usually someone who walked around for a bit and handed them a folder. When the door opens and they see a rep visibly dirty from actually being inside their crawl space, that mental model breaks. The customer recategorizes who they're dealing with. That recategorization has real dollar value at the close.On delivering on the agenda promise: Back in the warm-up, you told the customer that when you were done you'd come back and let them know. You are now doing exactly that. Every time you deliver on a small promise made earlier, the customer's confidence in your larger promises grows. Trust is built through a series of small kept commitments.On the 15-minute ask: This is not a stall — and the customer won't experience it as one, because you already told them about it in the agenda. Use that time to clean up, organize photos, review measurements, and confirm the scope you're recommending. Come back to that table completely prepared. The customer's anticipation and the brief wait actually increases the perceived value of what you're about to show them.Common mistake: Reps who come back in immediately without cleaning up. While being dirty is the trust signal, sitting down at the kitchen table still covered in crawl space debris is not professional. The goal is to be both thorough AND professional — not one at the expense of the other.

#### Slide 8

STEP  03

The Door Knock — Word for Word
Brief, clear, and confident
This interaction should take less than 60 seconds. Its job is to signal completion, show effort, and secure the sit-down — not to preview findings.

KEY PRINCIPLES
SCRIPT

1.  Don't preview findings at the door. 'I found some things' is fine. Details belong at the table.
YOU →
"Hey [Name] — just wanted to let you know I've finished up out there."

GOAL →
Deliver on the agenda promise. They were told you'd come back when done. Now you are.
2.  Let the visual do the work. The dirt speaks before you say a single word.

YOU →
"As you can see I got pretty well acquainted with your crawl space." [pause — let them react]

GOAL →
Let the visual do the work. Don't oversell the effort — let them notice the dirt themselves.
3.  Frame the 15 minutes as organized professionalism — not a delay or a stall.

YOU →
"I've got everything documented — photos, measurements, the whole picture. I just need about 15 minutes to clean up and organize everything so I can walk you through it properly."
4.  Always end with a question that gets a yes and reconfirms the sit-down.

GOAL →
Frame the 15 minutes as serving them — organized, thorough, professional.

YOU →
"Then I'd like to sit back down and show you exactly what I found. Sound good?"
5.  Stay calm and confident. Don't over-apologize for how long the inspection took.

GOAL →
Another micro-yes. Reconfirms they're still committed to the full sit-down process.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 03 — DOOR KNOCK SCRIPT

### Notes:
DOOR KNOCK SCRIPT — SPEAKER NOTESThe most important principle on this slide: do not preview findings at the door.This is the most common mistake reps make during the door knock. They're excited about what they found and want to start talking immediately. Resist this completely. The door knock is not the beginning of the presentation — it is the end of the inspection. If you start previewing findings now, you give up control of the presentation sequence and risk the customer forming opinions and objections before you've had a chance to frame the information properly.'I found some things I want to walk you through' is acceptable if the customer asks directly. 'I found active water intrusion in three areas and two joists are compromised' is not appropriate here. Save it for the table.On the 60-second rule: The door knock should genuinely be brief. The customer has been waiting and is ready for information. Don't stand on the porch filling dead air. Deliver the four beats — done, dirty, 15 minutes, sit back down — and get yourself cleaned up and organized.On staying calm: Newer reps sometimes get anxious during the door knock because they found significant problems and aren't sure how the customer will react. That anxiety is detectable. Practice delivering this script in a matter-of-fact tone. You've seen a hundred crawl spaces. This is another professional day. Your calmness is reassuring to the customer — it signals that whatever you found is manageable and that you have a plan.

#### Slide 9

STEP  03

The 15-Minute Window
Use it intentionally — don't waste it

Purpose: The 15 minutes between the door knock and sitting back down is not downtime. It is preparation time. What you do in this window directly determines how clean and compelling the presentation is.

Organize Your Photos
Review Your Measurements




Sequence them logically — exterior to interior to crawl space. Cull duplicates. The flow should tell a story, not dump a gallery.
Confirm your numbers. Know your moisture readings, floor slope figures, and crack dimensions cold before you sit down.

Confirm Your Scope
Clean Up Professionally




Decide what you're recommending and in what priority order. Walk in with a clear plan — don't still be deciding at the table.
Change your shirt if you can. Wipe down. Be the rep who was thorough enough to get dirty AND professional enough to clean up.

Review Their Stated Concerns
Get Your Head Right




Look back at what they told you during the walk-around. Make sure your presentation addresses their specific concerns directly.
Leave the crawl space behind mentally. You're about to serve this family with a clear, organized assessment. Step into that role.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 03 — THE 15-MINUTE WINDOW

### Notes:
THE 15-MINUTE WINDOW — SPEAKER NOTESWhy this slide exists: Most reps either rush back in before they're ready or take too long and let the customer's energy drop. This gives the 15 minutes a structure and a purpose.On organizing photos: The single biggest presentation upgrade a rep can make is shifting from 'here are my photos' to 'here is the story of your house, documented.' That shift happens entirely in how you organize. Exterior first — drainage, grade, visible cracks. Then interior — doors, floors, walls. Then crawl space access — so they can orient to where you were. Then crawl space sections moving inward from the access point. This sequence makes the presentation feel like a professional report, not a dump of raw evidence.On confirming scope before sitting down: Some reps walk back in still deciding what to recommend. That's a presentation killer. Uncertainty is detectable. Know your recommendation cold — what you're proposing, why, and in what priority order if there are multiple issues. If there's a clear Phase 1 and Phase 2, know exactly where that line is before you open your mouth.On getting your head right: This sounds soft but it genuinely matters. The rep who walks back in stressed, rushed, or uncertain presents differently from the rep who walks back in calm, organized, and confident. Whatever happened during the inspection — difficult access, worse conditions than expected, a complicated scope — leave it behind. You are now the trusted advisor who has the information this family needs. Walk back in like it.

The quality of your inspection determines the credibility of your proposal. There is no shortcut here.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  MODULE 2 — INSPECTION PHASE CHECKLIST

### Notes:
INSPECTION PHASE CHECKLIST — SPEAKER NOTESUse this as a self-audit tool after every appointment — especially missed closes.The most common skips and their consequences:'Let customer lead' skipped → Rep misses the customer's primary concern. The proposal addresses what the rep found, not what the customer cares most about. Objections at the close are often a symptom of this misalignment between what was found and what was felt.'Asked all six walk-around questions' skipped → Rep doesn't discover the customer's emotional driver. Can't frame the proposal around what actually motivates this particular customer to act.'Photographed systematically' skipped → Photo review becomes a disorganized collection of close-ups. Customer can't follow the story. Buy-in is harder to earn.'Organized photos into logical sequence' skipped → Rep sits down at the table still scrolling through a raw phone gallery. Professionalism takes a hit at exactly the moment it matters most.'Knows recommendation cold' skipped → Rep walks in still deciding what to propose. Customer senses uncertainty immediately. Close rate drops.Team exercise idea: Have each rep show both their raw inspection gallery and their organized presentation set at a team meeting. The difference between the two versions will be immediately obvious and make the value of organization concrete.

#### Slide 11

Evidence is the
foundation of trust.



How thoroughly you inspect and how carefully you document determines whether your proposal lands as an honest professional assessment — or just another quote.

Next Module: The Presentation Phase — Photo Review, Education & Building the Proposal

### Notes:
CLOSING SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESClose the module with the core message: the inspection is not just the work you do before the sale — it is part of the sale itself.A rep who inspects thoroughly and documents professionally walks into the presentation phase with an enormous structural advantage. They have evidence. The customer has watched them work. The door knock has already begun building the story of someone who takes this seriously. Every step of Module 2 is a deposit into the trust account that Module 3 will draw from.Leave reps with this thought: when you sit back down at that kitchen table, every photo you organized, every measurement you recorded, and every moment the customer watched you work during the walk-around is already doing sales work for you. The proposal you're about to present isn't a cold number from a stranger — it's the conclusion of a story that started the moment you asked them to show you around their house.Preview of Module 3: The Presentation Phase is where the inspection evidence turns into shared understanding. Photo review builds buy-in on the problems. Education reframes the customer as an informed partner rather than a skeptical buyer. And by the time the proposal appears, it should feel like the natural conclusion of a conversation the customer has been part of from the very beginning.
