#### Slide 1

MODULE 03


SALES PROCESS
The Presentation
Phase

Photo Review  ·  Education  ·  Building the Proposal
FLORIDA PANHANDLE  ·  SOUTH ALABAMA

For Internal Training Use Only

### Notes:
COVER SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESModule 3 is where the work of Modules 1 and 2 pays off. The rep has confirmed all decision-makers, built agenda and trust, conducted a thorough inspection, documented everything systematically, and knocked on the door dirty. Now they sit back down at the table and transform that evidence into a shared understanding with the customer.The core concept to frame this module around: most sales training focuses on how to present a solution. Module 3 is about something more powerful — how to co-create a diagnosis. A customer who helped build the problem list, who confirmed each issue as it was presented, and who understood the cause before they ever saw the price is a fundamentally different buyer than one who just received a quote. That customer doesn't have objections — they have questions. And questions are far easier to answer than objections.The three steps in this module each serve a distinct purpose. Photo review converts inspection data into shared understanding and earns buy-in on the problems. Education repositions the rep as a trusted expert and makes the repair feel inevitable rather than optional. Building the accomplish list co-creates the proposal scope so that when the number appears, it feels like a conclusion rather than a demand.

#### Slide 2

The Presentation Phase
Where inspection evidence becomes shared understanding
If the inspection phase built the evidence base, the presentation phase builds the case. You are not presenting to a customer — you are presenting with them. Every step is designed to earn a yes before the proposal ever appears.

01
02
03






Photo Review & Buy-In
Education
Building the Proposal
Evidence · Agreement · Accomplish list
Why it exists · Wrong fixes · Right repair
Co-create · Confirm · Set up the close



Key principle: A customer who helped build the scope doesn't experience the proposal as a sales tactic — they experience it as the logical conclusion of their own decision.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  MODULE 3 — PHASE OVERVIEW

### Notes:
PHASE OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESUse this slide to establish the mindset shift before you get into the mechanics. The critical reframe is this: most sales presentations are monologues — a rep telling a customer what they found and what it costs. The Apex presentation is a dialogue — a rep and customer reviewing evidence together and jointly deciding what needs to happen.That distinction isn't just philosophical. It has measurable impact on close rates. When customers feel like they participated in identifying the problem and designing the solution, they experience dramatically less resistance at the price reveal. The objection 'that seems really expensive' is far more common when the customer is hearing about their problem for the first time at the proposal stage. When they've been confirming problems one by one for the past 20 minutes, the proposal feels like the conclusion of a conversation they've been part of — not a surprise being handed to them.The three steps map precisely to three psychological shifts you need to accomplish. Photo review shifts the customer from 'I have a concern' to 'I have a documented problem.' Education shifts them from 'I wonder if this needs to be fixed' to 'I understand why it needs to be fixed and what the right fix is.' Building the proposal shifts them from 'I'm receiving a quote' to 'I'm confirming a scope I helped define.' By the time the number appears, the selling is already done.

#### Slide 3

STEP  01

Photo Review & Buy-In


Show the evidence · Get agreement · Build the list

Purpose: Walk the customer through everything you documented — systematically, in sequence. This is not a show-and-tell. It is an evidence review designed to earn an explicit yes on each problem before you ever mention a solution or a price.

Present Systematically
Narrate as You Go




Walk through photos in the same logical order you inspected — exterior, interior, crawl space. Sequence creates credibility. A random photo dump creates confusion.
Don't just show photos silently. Explain what you're looking at, what it means, and how it connects to what the customer told you during the walk-around.

Get Buy-In on Each Issue
Build the Accomplish List




After showing evidence of each problem, ask: 'Based on what you're seeing here, would you agree this is something that needs to be addressed?' Wait for the answer.
As the customer agrees to each issue, record it. This live list becomes the foundation of the proposal. They're not reviewing your scope — they built it with you.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 01 — PHOTO REVIEW & BUY-IN

### Notes:
PHOTO REVIEW & BUY-IN — OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESThe mindset to establish before this slide: the photo review is not a presentation about problems. It is a collaborative review of evidence. The customer is not your audience — they are your co-reviewer. That distinction changes everything about how the rep should carry themselves in this step.On presenting systematically: The sequence you established during the 15-minute window pays off here. Exterior photos first ground the customer in the big picture — grade, drainage, the exterior condition of their home. Then interior symptoms give them the emotional hook — the sticking door they've been fighting, the crack in the drywall they've been staring at. Then the crawl space documentation reveals the root cause. That arc — symptom, symptom, root cause — is a story structure. It's far more persuasive than a random collection of problems.On narrating as you go: Many reps show photos in silence, expecting the images to speak for themselves. They don't, not completely. The rep's narration is what connects the photo to the customer's experience. 'You mentioned this door has been sticking for about a year — here's what I found directly beneath it in the crawl space.' That sentence connects their daily frustration to your documentation. That connection is worth more than any amount of technical detail.On getting buy-in on each issue: This is the most important mechanic in the entire module. Do not rush past it. Show the evidence. Explain what it means. Then ask directly: 'Would you agree this is something that needs to be addressed?' The customer says yes. You record it. That yes is a commitment anchor. By the time you've collected four or five of them, the customer has effectively pre-approved the scope of work.On building the accomplish list: Frame this to the customer in real time. 'I'm going to make a list of everything we've agreed needs attention so we can make sure nothing falls through the cracks.' They are watching you build the list together. That list will become the proposal. But to them, it feels like their list — because it is.

#### Slide 4

STEP  01

The Buy-In Question
The single most important mechanic in the entire presentation
After showing evidence of each problem, you must stop and ask for explicit agreement before moving on. This turns a passive audience into an active participant who is co-authoring the diagnosis.
“

Based on what you're seeing here, would you agree this is something that needs to be addressed?








Ask after every issue
Wait for the answer
Record it visibly
Handle hesitation immediately
Not just the big ones. Even minor findings deserve a buy-in question. Each yes builds commitment and forward momentum toward the proposal.
Ask the question and then stop talking. Silence is not awkward here — it is the customer processing. Filling the silence with more talking undermines the question.
When they say yes, write it down on the accomplish list in front of them. The physical act of recording their agreement reinforces the commitment.
If they hesitate, ask what they're seeing differently. This surfaces objections early — at the problem stage, not the price stage — where they're far easier to resolve.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 01 — THE BUY-IN QUESTION

### Notes:
THE BUY-IN QUESTION — SPEAKER NOTESWhy this mechanic deserves its own slide: this single question is the highest-leverage move in the entire presentation phase. It sounds almost too simple — just ask if they agree. But the reason it works is deeply rooted in how commitment develops in human decision-making.The psychology behind it: When people verbally agree to something, they feel a psychological pressure to remain consistent with that agreement. It's called the consistency principle. Each time a customer says 'yes, that needs to be addressed,' they are making a small commitment. By the time they've made six or seven of those small commitments, reversing course at the proposal feels inconsistent with who they've been during the conversation. The proposal becomes the natural continuation of a series of decisions they've already made.On waiting for the answer: This is hard for reps who are nervous or eager. The instinct is to keep talking to fill the space. Train reps to count to three silently after asking the question. Three seconds of silence feels like forever when you're in it. It is actually a very short time for the customer to think. Filling it with more words interrupts their processing and weakens the commitment.On handling hesitation: When a customer hesitates or pushes back on a buy-in question, most reps panic and move on. That is exactly the wrong response. A hesitation is not a rejection — it is a signal that the customer sees something differently than you do, or that they need more context. Ask them: 'What are you seeing differently?' or 'What questions do you have about this one?' Resolve it at the problem stage. An unresolved problem doubt will surface as a price objection at the close, where it is much harder to handle.

#### Slide 5

STEP  02

Education


Why it exists · Wrong fixes · Right repair

Purpose: Before you present a solution, the customer needs to understand the problem completely — its cause, why common fixes fail, and why your approach is correct. Education earns the solution.

01

02

03






Why the Problem Exists
Why Common Fixes Fail
The Correct Repair
Explain the root cause — soil conditions, drainage, moisture, settlement. Use plain language. The customer should understand their specific situation, not just foundation problems in general. Connect directly to what you found in their inspection.
Walk through the typical DIY attempts and inferior contractor solutions. Show why they treat symptoms instead of causes. This step is critical — it pre-empts the customer from thinking a cheaper alternative will work after you leave.
Present the Apex approach — what it does, why it addresses the root cause, and what the customer can expect from the result. Ask the customer to confirm the repair plan before moving on. Their confirmation is another commitment anchor.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 02 — EDUCATION

### Notes:
EDUCATION — OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESThe education step is where the Apex rep separates most clearly from every competitor. Most foundation company reps show a problem, show a product, and quote a price. The Apex rep teaches the customer to understand their situation deeply enough to know why the proposed repair is not just an option — it is the correct answer.That distinction matters enormously at the close. A customer who was sold a solution will price-shop it. A customer who was educated to understand why the solution is correct will defend it to a spouse, weigh it against the consequences of inaction, and feel confident moving forward. Education converts a transaction into a decision.On why the problem exists: This section needs to be specific to the customer's actual situation, not a generic foundation lecture. 'Homes in the Florida Panhandle sit on sandy soils with high seasonal moisture variation — that's what's driving the settlement under your northeast corner' lands very differently than 'foundations settle because of soil movement.' Specificity is credibility.On why common fixes fail: This is the most underused and most powerful section of the education step. When a rep shows a customer why the obvious cheaper fix — a coat of waterproof paint, a new gutter, a bag of hydraulic cement — does not address the root cause, they eliminate the customer's mental alternative. Once a customer understands that the $200 DIY solution treats a symptom of a problem that will continue getting worse, the Apex price is no longer competing against a cheap alternative. It is competing against the cost of doing nothing.On confirming the repair plan: At the end of the education section for each issue type, ask directly: 'Does this approach make sense for what we found in your home?' This is another buy-in moment — the customer is confirming the method, not just acknowledging the problem. By the time you build the proposal, they've agreed to both the problems and the solutions. The price is the only remaining variable.

#### Slide 6

STEP  02

Using the Education Slide Decks
Your slide decks are teaching tools — not sales brochures
Apex's job-type slide decks exist for one reason: to make a complex technical subject understandable to a homeowner in 10 minutes. How you use them matters as much as what's in them.

Deliver It This Way

Avoid These Mistakes
▸  Show only the relevant deck. If it's a crawl space drainage job, use that deck. Don't show slides that don't apply.
✕  Don't rush through the slides. A customer who didn't understand the education phase won't trust the proposal.
▸  Pause at each section. Give the customer time to absorb each concept before moving to the next slide.
✕  Don't read the slides verbatim. The deck is a visual aid, not a script. You are the expert — the slide supports you.
▸  Connect slides to their home. 'This is exactly what I found in your northeast corner' is more powerful than any generic diagram.
✕  Don't show slides that don't apply. Irrelevant content dilutes credibility and wastes time the customer is now tracking.
▸  Ask for confirmation after each fix section. 'Does this make sense for what we found?' earns a yes on the solution, not just the problem.
✕  Don't skip the confirmation questions. Moving from education to proposal without checking for understanding creates confusion at the close.
▸  Let the customer ask questions. An engaged customer asking questions is not a distraction — they are investing in their understanding.
✕  Don't treat pushback as an obstacle. If a customer questions your solution choice, engage with it — it's an opportunity to reinforce your recommendation.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 02 — USING THE SLIDE DECKS

### Notes:
USING THE EDUCATION SLIDE DECKS — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide exists because having the right tool doesn't guarantee using it well. Reps who rush through the education decks to get to the proposal are leaving their most powerful persuasion asset on the table.On connecting slides to the customer's specific home: This is the single biggest upgrade a rep can make to their education delivery. The slides are designed to explain the concept — the rep's job is to bridge from the concept to the customer's actual situation. Every time you say 'this is exactly what I found under your house,' you convert a generic education moment into a personally relevant one. Generic education informs. Personal relevance motivates.On not reading slides verbatim: The customer can read. When a rep reads the slide aloud, they subordinate themselves to the tool. The rep should know the content of each slide well enough to explain it in their own words, using the visual as a reference. That demonstrates genuine expertise. Reading the slide demonstrates that the rep is delivering a presentation they don't fully own.On pausing for questions: Some reps fear questions during education because they think questions slow down the close. The opposite is true. A customer who asks questions during education is an engaged customer who is working to understand, not one who has checked out. Questions during education almost never delay the close — they prevent objections at the close. Answer every question thoroughly and treat each one as a trust-building moment.On showing only relevant decks: If you do a crawl space drainage job and show the pier and beam settlement deck, the customer will be confused and might start wondering whether they have problems you haven't addressed. Relevance discipline is professionalism.

#### Slide 7

STEP  03

Building the Proposal


Co-create · Confirm · Set up the close

Purpose: The proposal is not something you hand to the customer — it is something you build with them. By this point, they have agreed to the problems and confirmed the solutions. The proposal is the formalization of decisions they have already made.

Reference the Accomplish List
Present Each Line Item




Open by reviewing the list you built together during the photo review. 'Here's everything we agreed needs to be addressed — let me put together exactly what that looks like.' This anchors the proposal in their own decisions.
Walk through each scope item one at a time. Connect every item back to a specific photo, measurement, or thing the customer agreed to during the buy-in phase. No item should feel like a surprise.

Confirm as You Build
Present the Total as a Summary




After adding each item: 'That's the [X] we looked at together — does that make sense to include?' You are collecting a series of small yes answers on the scope before the total price appears.
When the full scope is on paper, present the total as a summary of everything already decided: 'Based on everything we've agreed on, here's what we're looking at.' It should feel like a conclusion, not a reveal.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 03 — BUILDING THE PROPOSAL

### Notes:
BUILDING THE PROPOSAL — OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESThe framing to establish here: a proposal that surprises the customer has already failed. By the time a rep opens their proposal software or pulls out their price sheet, every single line item should be something the customer has already agreed to. The only new information in the proposal presentation should be the total.On referencing the accomplish list: This move is psychologically elegant. You are not opening with 'here's what I'm proposing' — you are opening with 'here's what we agreed.' That shift of ownership from you to 'we' is not subtle. The customer perceives the proposal as the documentation of their own decisions rather than a price being presented to them.On presenting each line item with a photo connection: If you've organized your documentation well and run the buy-in process correctly, every line item in the proposal should have a corresponding photo and a customer yes behind it. When you can say 'this line covers the vapor barrier replacement we looked at in photos 12 through 15 — you agreed that was a priority,' that line item has a face. Customers don't argue with evidence they've already accepted.On presenting the total as a summary: Delivery matters enormously here. The rep who slides a paper across the table with 'so here's the total' is treating the price reveal as a transaction. The rep who says 'based on everything we've agreed needs to happen, here's what we're looking at' is treating it as the conclusion of a professional consultation. Same number, completely different psychological framing. Train reps on this exact language.

#### Slide 8

STEP  03

The Accomplish List — In Practice
A simple tool that fundamentally changes the close
The accomplish list is not a form or a template. It is a live document you build in real time with the customer during the photo review. Its power comes entirely from the fact that the customer helped create it.

After each buy-in yes, add the item to the list in plain language. 'Address moisture intrusion in northeast quadrant.' The customer watches you write it. The list grows as they agree.

PHOTO REVIEW

Reference relevant items from the list as you educate. 'The vapor barrier we just discussed ties directly to item two on your list.' This reinforces the connection between the diagnosis and the solution.

EDUCATION

Open the proposal step by reading the accomplish list back to the customer. 'Here's everything we agreed needs to be addressed.' Then build the proposal line by line from that list.

PROPOSAL BUILD

If the customer hesitates on price, return to the list. 'Everything on here is something we agreed needs to happen — let's make sure we have the right scope for your situation.' The list is your anchor.

AT THE CLOSE



The accomplish list converts 'your price is high' into 'let's make sure the scope is right.' That shift alone is worth the discipline of building the list every time.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 03 — THE ACCOMPLISH LIST

### Notes:
THE ACCOMPLISH LIST — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide teaches reps how the accomplish list functions across the entire presentation phase — not just at the point when the proposal appears.The key insight about the accomplish list: it is most valuable not when you use it to build the proposal, but when you use it to handle price resistance at the close. When a customer says 'that's a lot of money,' the natural rep response is to justify the price — to explain why it costs what it costs. That response is defensive and puts the rep on the back foot. The accomplish list allows a completely different response: 'You're right that it's a significant investment — let's go through what's on this list together and make sure we have the right scope for your situation.' Now you're not defending a number. You're reviewing a list of decisions the customer made. That is an entirely different conversation.On writing items in plain language: The accomplish list is for the customer, not for the rep. 'Encapsulation system with 20-mil liner, drainage matting, and dehumidifier' is an internal scope item. 'Fully seal and dry your crawl space to stop moisture from getting into your home' is an accomplish list item. Write what the customer is getting, not what you're installing. The proposal will have the technical details — the accomplish list should describe outcomes.On referencing the list during education: This is a technique most reps miss. As you move through the education slide decks, explicitly connect what you're teaching back to items on the list. This reinforcement cements the connection between the diagnosis (what's wrong) and the prescription (what needs to happen) in the customer's mind. By the time you build the proposal, that connection is solid.

#### Slide 9

STEP  03

Financing — Transition & Framing


Present options before resistance appears

Purpose: Financing should be introduced as a natural part of presenting the proposal — not as a reactive response to price objection. When financing is offered proactively, it is a service. When it is offered defensively, it feels like a concession.

When & How to Introduce It

Framing That Works
▸  Mention it briefly at the 2-day out call to plant the seed early — 'We do have some great financing options if that's ever useful.'
Say:   'Most of our customers use financing — it keeps a significant repair from disrupting your budget all at once.'
▸  Bring it back naturally when presenting the total: 'We work with Upgrade and GoodLeap — most customers find monthly payments make this very manageable.'
Say:   'We work with two lenders — Upgrade and GoodLeap. Both are straightforward and most decisions come back quickly.'
▸  Present the monthly payment figure alongside the total — not as a backup, but as a standard option.
Say:   'For a job like this, you're looking at roughly [monthly payment] a month — does that feel workable?'
▸  If the customer has already been warmed to financing, lead with the monthly number first, then show the total.
Avoid:   'I know it's a lot — we do have financing if you need it.' This frames financing as a response to sticker shock, not a standard option.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 03 — FINANCING FRAMING

### Notes:
FINANCING FRAMING — SPEAKER NOTESWhy financing gets its own slide in the presentation module: most reps treat financing as an objection-handling tool — something to pull out when the customer flinches at the price. That positioning is a mistake for two reasons. First, it signals to the customer that you expected them to find the price too high. Second, it introduces a new concept at the exact moment the customer is experiencing the most stress in the conversation.When financing is introduced proactively as a standard part of presenting the total, it lands as a service — a normal, professional option that Apex routinely provides. The customer doesn't experience it as a concession or a discount. They experience it as a helpful feature of doing business with a professional company.On leading with the monthly payment: For customers who have already expressed budget sensitivity — or who mentioned it during the walk-around questions — it can be worth presenting the monthly figure before the total. '$287 a month with approved credit' lands very differently from '$14,500.' This isn't deceptive — the total is still fully disclosed. It is simply a presentation order that makes the financial reality more accessible. Humans think in monthly terms. Mortgages, car payments, utilities — these are all monthly frames. Meeting the customer in that mental frame is good communication.On Upgrade and GoodLeap specifically: Reps should know both programs well enough to explain the basic terms, approval process, and any current promotional rates. Confidence in explaining the financing options is part of the overall professionalism signal. A rep who says 'I don't really know the details, they'll contact you' undermines the credibility they've been building for the past two hours.

#### Slide 10

STEP  03

Asking for the Business


A process, not a moment — and it must be direct

Purpose: After the proposal is presented, you must ask for the business directly and clearly. Everything in this module has been building toward this moment. Don't leave it implied.
FIRST ASK — Use this immediately after presenting the full proposal and total:

YOU →
"Based on everything we've looked at together, I'd like to earn your business today. Are you ready to move forward?"

GOAL →
Direct, confident, and grounded in the shared review. 'Move forward' is softer than 'sign' but still requires a clear answer.
IF THEY HESITATE — Diagnose, don't push:

YOU →
"Help me understand — is there a specific concern about what we've looked at, or is it more about the investment itself?"

GOAL →
This separates scope objections from price objections. They require completely different responses. Never assume which one you're dealing with.

YOU →
"I want to make sure you have everything you need to feel confident about this. What would help you get there?"


GOAL →
Opens the door for the customer to name the real barrier — which you can then address directly rather than guessing.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 03 — ASKING FOR THE BUSINESS

### Notes:
ASKING FOR THE BUSINESS — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide exists because many reps, even experienced ones, struggle to ask for the business directly. They hint at it, they trail off, they say things like 'so what are your thoughts?' and hope the customer volunteers a yes. That approach fails far more often than a direct ask.On the first ask: The phrasing 'I'd like to earn your business today' is deliberate. It is personal and direct — the rep is expressing a genuine desire — without being pushy or transactional. 'Are you ready to move forward?' requires a yes or no answer, which is what you need. Vague questions get vague answers. Clear questions get clear answers.On diagnosing hesitation: The most important skill in this section is separating scope objections from price objections. A customer who hesitates because they're questioning whether everything on the scope is truly necessary needs a conversation about the accomplish list and the evidence behind each item. A customer who hesitates because the total is more than they planned for needs a financing conversation or a right-sizing conversation. Treating a scope objection with a financing response — or vice versa — makes both problems worse. The diagnostic question ('is it the scope, or the investment?') tells you exactly which conversation to have.On 'what would help you feel confident?': This is a powerful open-ended question that many reps are afraid to ask because they fear the answer. Don't be. Whatever the customer says is actionable intelligence. If they say 'I'd like to get another opinion,' you can address that directly. If they say 'I need to talk to my wife,' you now know the decision-maker situation that should have been resolved at the 2-day call. In either case, you know what you're actually dealing with.

Presentation Phase Checklist
Complete every item on every appointment — no exceptions

STEP 03 — Proposal

☐  Engaged with all customer questions fully

☐  Diagnosed hesitation before responding to it



A customer who helped build the scope doesn't object to the price — they confirm it.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  MODULE 3 — PRESENTATION PHASE CHECKLIST

### Notes:
PRESENTATION PHASE CHECKLIST — SPEAKER NOTESUse this as a self-audit after every appointment, especially those that reached the close but didn't convert.The most common skips and their consequences:'Buy-in question asked after each problem' skipped → The customer sits through the photo review passively. By the time the proposal appears, they haven't committed to anything. Every line item is now subject to fresh scrutiny rather than prior agreement.'Waited for answer' skipped → The rep asks the buy-in question and then immediately explains more. The customer never has to say yes. The commitment anchor is never set.'Accomplish list built live' skipped → The proposal feels like it was written before you arrived, not built from their specific situation. Customers sense this and it reduces trust in the scope.'Connected slides to customer's specific home' skipped → Education becomes generic. The customer understands foundation problems in the abstract but doesn't feel personally compelled to act.'Asked for the business directly' skipped → Rep presents the total and then asks 'so what do you think?' Customer says 'let me think about it.' This is the most common and most preventable close failure in the entire process. The ask must be direct.'Diagnosed hesitation before responding' skipped → Rep responds to 'that seems expensive' with either a financing offer or a price justification — without knowing which one the customer actually needs. One of the two responses will make things worse. Diagnose first.

#### Slide 12

Don't present
a proposal.
Co-author one.



Every yes you earn during the photo review, every confirmation you collect during education, and every line item the customer agreed to is selling work that is already done before you ask for the business.

Next Module: The Close Phase — Objection Handling, Right-Sizing, the CAD Drawing & the Second Ask

### Notes:
CLOSING SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESClose with the core reframe of the entire module: the presentation is not a performance that happens to the customer. It is a conversation the customer participates in. The rep's job is not to persuade — it is to guide the customer through their own decision-making process using evidence the customer has already seen and agreements the customer has already made.The practical takeaway: when the buy-in process is working correctly, asking for the business should feel almost anticlimactic. The customer already knows the problems. They already understand the cause. They already confirmed the solutions. The proposal is just the paperwork for decisions they've been making for the last hour. A rep who runs this module well will tell you that the close 'just happened' — because it did. It happened incrementally, one buy-in question at a time.Preview of Module 4: The Close Phase covers what happens when things get harder — objections, price resistance, right-sizing conversations, the CAD drawing psychology, and the second ask. Module 3 is about setting up the close perfectly. Module 4 is about finishing what Module 3 started when the close doesn't happen immediately.

#### Slide 1

MODULE 04


SALES PROCESS

For Internal Training Use Only

### Notes:
COVER SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESModule 4 is where everything that was built in Modules 1 through 3 gets tested. The rep has done everything right — confirmed decision-makers, ran a thorough inspection, built the evidence base, co-created the diagnosis, and presented the proposal. Now the customer needs to make a decision. Module 4 teaches what to do when that decision doesn't come immediately.The essential mindset to frame this module: the close is not a single moment. It is a process that began the second the rep confirmed all decision-makers would be present. Everything in the previous modules was closing work — just distributed across the appointment rather than concentrated at the end. By the time we reach Module 4, a well-run Apex appointment should have a customer who is 80% of the way to yes. This module teaches how to bring them the rest of the way.The five steps covered here — objection handling, right-sizing, the CAD drawing, the second ask, and the leave-behind — are not a sequence that always plays out in full. Sometimes you'll ask for the business and get a yes immediately. That is the goal. These steps exist for the appointments where the customer needs more time, more information, or more confidence before they commit. Train reps to think of these steps as tools in a toolkit, not a script they must run through every time.

#### Slide 2

The Close Phase
What to do when the answer isn't immediately yes
A well-run presentation should produce a yes at the first ask most of the time. The steps in this module exist for the appointments that require more — more patience, more conversation, more information, or a right-sized scope. None of them are pressure tactics. All of them are professional service.

The goal is always a yes at the first ask. These steps exist to earn that yes when more conversation is needed — never to pressure.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  MODULE 4 — PHASE OVERVIEW

### Notes:
PHASE OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESThe framing for this slide is important because it sets the tone for the entire module. Module 4 is not about manipulation or pressure — it is about professional persistence and genuine service. Every technique here should feel like the rep is trying to help the customer get to a good decision, not trying to extract a signature.The distinction between a rep who closes professionally and one who closes pressurefully is often this: the professional closer is always working to understand the customer's real barrier to saying yes. The pressure closer is trying to overcome the customer's resistance. Those are completely different orientations, and customers feel the difference immediately.A well-run presentation will genuinely close at the first ask the majority of the time when Modules 1 through 3 were executed correctly. If a team is consistently reaching Module 4 steps without closing, the solution is almost always upstream — something in the pre-inspection confirmation, the buy-in process, or the proposal build. Emphasize this to reps: if you find yourself doing heavy objection handling on every appointment, the problem is not your close — it is your setup.On the five steps as a toolkit: make clear to reps that these steps don't always play out in sequence or in full. Sometimes the second ask is all you need. Sometimes right-sizing is the entire conversation. The sequence is presented logically but should be applied situationally. Reps who understand why each step exists will know when to use it far better than reps who are following a script.

#### Slide 3

STEP  01

Objection Handling


Diagnose first · Resolve at the source · Re-anchor

Purpose: An objection is not a rejection — it is a signal that the customer has a specific barrier to moving forward. Your job is to identify exactly what that barrier is before you attempt to address it. A wrong diagnosis produces the wrong response.

Step 1 — Diagnose
Step 2 — Acknowledge




Before responding to any objection, identify what type it is. Is it about scope, price, timing, trust, or a missing decision-maker? Ask a clarifying question rather than assuming you know what you're dealing with.
Validate the concern before addressing it. 'That's a fair question' or 'I completely understand why you'd want to think about that' costs nothing and creates the space for an honest conversation.

Step 3 — Resolve
Step 4 — Re-anchor




Address the specific objection with the specific evidence or information that resolves it. Return to the accomplish list, the photos, the measurements, or the education slides — whatever is most relevant.
After resolving the objection, re-anchor to prior agreements before asking again. 'We agreed this needed to be addressed — does what I've just shown you help with that concern?' Then ask for the business again.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 01 — OBJECTION HANDLING

### Notes:
OBJECTION HANDLING — OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESThe most important concept in this entire slide: diagnose before you respond. The two most common objections in foundation repair sales are 'that's a lot of money' and 'I want to get another opinion.' Those two sentences sound similar on the surface. But they come from completely different places and require completely different responses.'That's a lot of money' might mean the customer genuinely can't afford it, or it might mean they weren't expecting that number, or it might mean they're not yet convinced the problem is serious enough to justify the investment. Those three underlying issues require three different responses. If you respond to all three with a financing offer, you'll only solve one of them.'I want to get another opinion' might mean they have genuine trust concerns about Apex, or it might mean their spouse wasn't there and they need cover for not deciding alone, or it might mean they're skeptical that the problem is as serious as you described. Again, three different issues. The rep who launches into a defense of Apex's quality before understanding which one they're dealing with will almost certainly make things worse.On acknowledging before resolving: This step is almost always skipped by reps who are eager to solve the problem. Skipping it is a mistake. When a customer voices a concern and the rep immediately jumps to a counter-argument, the customer experiences the rep as defensive — which signals that the concern was justified. When the rep acknowledges the concern first, they signal confidence and empathy simultaneously. That creates the emotional space for an actual conversation.On re-anchoring: This is the step that turns a resolved objection into a close. If you address a concern successfully but don't connect it back to the prior agreements before asking again, you've defused the objection but not moved the close forward. The re-anchor — 'we agreed this needed to be addressed, and now we've resolved the question about X — does moving forward make sense?' — connects the resolution to the action.

#### Slide 4

STEP  01

The Most Common Objections
Know the category before you respond
Every objection in foundation sales falls into one of five categories. Identifying the category first tells you which tool to reach for. The wrong tool on the right objection makes things worse.

"That's more than I expected." / "That seems really expensive."
Diagnose first — is it truly unaffordable, or is the value not yet clear? If value: return to the accomplish list and photos. If affordability: introduce financing. If scope: offer right-sizing.

PRICE



"I want to get a second quote." / "I'd like to shop around."
Acknowledge it as reasonable. Then: 'When you get other quotes, here's what to ask for so you can compare apples to apples.' Gives them tools rather than arguing against the idea — which builds trust.

NEED ANOTHER OPINION



"Can I have some time to think?" / "Let me sleep on it."
Diagnose what specifically needs more thought. 'Absolutely — what part of the decision feels unresolved for you?' The answer tells you exactly what to address. Vague thinking time usually masks a specific concern.

NEED TO THINK ABOUT IT



"I need to talk to my husband / wife / partner."
This is a setup failure from the 2-day out call. If it happens: 'I completely understand — I want you both to feel good about this. Can we find a time to go through the findings together so you can ask questions?' Offer a follow-up call with both present.

MISSING DECISION-MAKER



"We're not ready right now." / "Maybe in a few months."
Acknowledge timing, then address consequences of delay specifically. 'I want to be honest with you about what typically happens to this kind of issue when it sits through another wet season.' Evidence-based, not fear-based.

TIMING





APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 01 — COMMON OBJECTIONS

### Notes:
COMMON OBJECTIONS — SPEAKER NOTESHow to use this slide in training: walk through each objection type and have reps practice their diagnostic question out loud before practicing the response. The diagnostic question is more important than the response script — because the right diagnostic question will often reveal that the customer's objection is different from what it appears on the surface.On the price objection specifically: this is the most common objection in any high-ticket home services sale and the most mishandled. Many reps hear 'that's expensive' and immediately jump to financing or a discount. The problem is that 'that's expensive' is not always a statement about affordability — it is often a statement about perceived value. If the customer doesn't yet believe the problem is serious enough to justify the investment, offering financing doesn't help. It just makes the unpersuasive proposal more accessible. Address the value gap first. Return to the documentation. Re-run the buy-in question on the most serious finding. Rebuild the evidence that the investment is warranted.On the missing decision-maker: this is a structural problem that should have been prevented at the 2-day out call. When it appears at the close, it must be handled with empathy — the rep cannot make the customer feel embarrassed about the situation. But the rep also cannot leave without a clear path to get in front of both decision-makers. A scheduled follow-up call or visit with both present is the only acceptable outcome here. A promise to 'talk it over and call you' is a lead that will go cold.On timing objections: the key principle is evidence-based urgency, never fear-based urgency. A rep who says 'this is going to get a lot worse if you wait' without evidence is applying pressure. A rep who says 'based on the moisture readings I took today, here's what typically happens to wood members at these levels through a wet season' is sharing professional information the customer needs to make a good decision. The outcome might be the same, but one feels like a scare tactic and the other feels like honest counsel. Customers respond to the latter with trust, not resistance.

#### Slide 5

STEP  02

Right-Sizing


Adjusting scope is not losing — it is closing

Purpose: When a customer cannot or will not proceed with the full scope, right-sizing offers a path forward that serves their most urgent need, protects the relationship, and keeps the door open for the remaining work.

When to Right-Size

How to Right-Size
▸  The customer has genuine budget constraints that financing does not fully resolve
▸  Return to the accomplish list.  Identify which items are highest priority and which can be phased.
▸  The scope includes Phase 1 and Phase 2 items and the customer wants to stage the work
▸  Let the customer lead.  'If we had to prioritize, which of these concerns you most?' Their answer drives the rescope.
▸  The customer agrees on the problem but wants to address the most urgent items first
▸  Never remove the root cause fix.  You can phase cosmetic or secondary work. The primary structural repair should stay in Phase 1.
▸  After a second ask, the customer is still not ready for the full scope
▸  Document Phase 2 clearly.  Put remaining items in writing so both parties understand what is being deferred and why.
▸  There is a clear priority repair that addresses the root cause even if not everything is included
▸  Schedule Phase 2 before you leave.  A firm follow-up date prevents deferred work from becoming forgotten work.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 02 — RIGHT-SIZING

### Notes:
RIGHT-SIZING — SPEAKER NOTESThe framing that matters most on this slide: right-sizing is not failure and it is not a concession — it is a professional service. A customer who leaves with a Phase 1 job that addresses their most urgent problem is a customer who got helped. They are also far more likely to call Apex for Phase 2 than a customer who was pushed into a full scope they couldn't afford and then cancelled.The single biggest right-sizing mistake is removing the root cause fix to bring the price down. This is where reps make deals that come back to hurt them. If a customer's crawl space has active moisture intrusion caused by improper drainage, and the rep removes the drainage solution from the scope to hit a budget number, the remaining work — encapsulation, wood treatment, dehumidifier — will fail. The customer will be unhappy. They will not call for Phase 2. And they may leave a negative review. Never phase out the fix that addresses the underlying cause.On letting the customer lead the right-sizing: this is both empathetic and strategically smart. When the customer chooses which items to prioritize, they take ownership of the decision. They are not being sold a smaller job — they are designing their Phase 1. That ownership matters when it comes time to close Phase 1 and schedule Phase 2. 'You chose to prioritize the drainage and the vapor barrier in Phase 1 — when do you want to schedule the structural piers for Phase 2?' That sentence feels like a natural continuation of a plan the customer built, not a follow-up sales call.On documenting Phase 2: this is critical for protecting everyone. Put the deferred items in writing on the proposal or in a follow-up email. This protects the rep if the customer later claims they were told everything was handled, and it gives the customer a clear record of what remains to be done.

#### Slide 6

STEP  03

The CAD Drawing


Professional signal · Productive silence · Commitment anchor

Purpose: While the customer is processing, produce a CAD drawing of the proposed repair to include with the proposal. This serves two purposes simultaneously — it signals a level of professionalism almost no competitor matches, and it gives the customer productive breathing room without you sitting across from them.






What It Signals
What It Creates
How to Use It
A CAD drawing says: we plan repairs carefully, we document our work, and we treat every job as a professional project — not a service call. Most foundation companies never produce one. The customer notices the difference even if they don't say so.
Productive silence. While you're drawing, the customer has a few minutes to sit with the proposal without you watching them. That breathing room reduces pressure and often produces organic conversation — questions, realizations, or a spouse arriving at a decision independently.
After presenting the proposal and asking for the business, don't hover. Say: 'Let me put together a quick site drawing to include with your proposal — it'll just take a few minutes.' Begin drawing quietly. Return with the drawing when it's complete and re-engage from a position of added value.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 03 — THE CAD DRAWING

### Notes:
THE CAD DRAWING — SPEAKER NOTESThis is one of the most uniquely effective steps in the entire Apex process — and also one of the easiest to underestimate. The CAD drawing is not primarily a technical document. It is a psychological tool.On what it signals: The customer has seen multiple foundation company reps over the years, either personally or through neighbors and family. The standard experience is: a guy comes, looks around, writes a number on paper, and leaves. When Apex's rep sits down and produces a professional site drawing to include with the proposal, the customer's mental model shifts. This person is not giving them a quote — they are providing a professional deliverable. That shift in perception has real dollar value, because customers pay differently for professional services than for estimates.On the breathing room effect: this is subtle but powerful. The moment you start drawing and the customer realizes you're occupied, the social pressure of the close temporarily lifts. They can talk to each other. They can look at the proposal again. They can think. This often produces exactly the organic conversation that leads to a close — not because you pushed, but because you gave the customer the space to arrive at the decision themselves. Reps who are trained on this consistently report that the customer will sometimes make a decision and communicate it before the drawing is even finished.On the transition back: when you return with the drawing, present it as additional professional value before asking for the business again. 'Here's a site layout showing the scope and placement — this goes with your proposal. [Show the drawing.] Based on everything, are you ready to move forward?' This second ask is now anchored in a fresh piece of professional collateral that has just reset the customer's perception of value.

#### Slide 7

STEP  04

The Second Ask


Return with value · Re-anchor · Close cleanly

Purpose: The second ask comes after the CAD drawing. It is not a repeat of the first ask — it is a new ask anchored in new value. The drawing has changed what the customer is looking at. The question should reflect that.
SECOND ASK SCRIPT

YOU →
"Here's the site drawing — this shows the full scope and placement of everything we discussed. This goes with your proposal copy."

GOAL →
Present the drawing as additional professional value. Let them look at it for a moment before asking the question.

YOU →
"Based on everything we've gone through today, I'd like to earn your business. Are you ready to move forward and get this scheduled?"

GOAL →
'Get this scheduled' is forward-motion language. It treats yes as the beginning of a project, not the end of a sales process.

YOU →
IF STILL HESITATING: "I want to make sure you feel completely confident about this. What's the one thing that's giving you pause right now?"

GOAL →
Open-ended and specific. Forces the customer to name their actual barrier rather than retreating to a vague 'I need to think about it.'

YOU →
"I hear that. [Address the specific concern directly.] With that resolved — are you ready to move forward?"


GOAL →
Address, re-anchor, ask again. Every objection that is genuinely resolved earns a renewed ask. Don't address and wait — address and ask.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 04 — THE SECOND ASK

### Notes:
THE SECOND ASK — SPEAKER NOTESThe second ask is not just asking for the business again — it is asking from a different position. Between the first ask and the second ask, the rep has produced a professional deliverable, given the customer breathing room, and returned with something new to offer. The second ask is therefore anchored in fresh value, not repetition.On the language 'get this scheduled': this is intentional. 'Are you ready to sign?' frames the decision as a legal commitment. 'Are you ready to move forward?' is better but still somewhat transactional. 'Are you ready to get this scheduled?' frames the decision as the beginning of a project — something that will solve the customer's problem. The customer is not being asked to sign a contract; they are being asked to pick a start date for the work that will protect their home. That framing aligns with what the customer actually wants.On the specific-barrier question: 'What's the one thing giving you pause right now?' is one of the most powerful questions in the close phase. It is specific (one thing), it acknowledges that there is something without being confrontational about it, and it requires the customer to articulate their actual objection. Reps who ask vague questions at this stage — 'is there anything I can help with?' or 'do you have any more questions?' — give the customer an easy out. The specific question makes avoidance harder and honest conversation easier.On address-and-ask: many reps make the mistake of addressing an objection and then waiting to see what the customer does. That creates an awkward silence that often gets filled with another objection. The correct sequence is always address, re-anchor, ask. You addressed the concern — now reconnect to the prior agreements and ask directly for the business. You are not being pushy. You are being professionally clear about what you need from this conversation to move forward.

#### Slide 8

STEP  04

Reading the Room
Know when to push forward — and when to let go
Not every appointment closes on the day of the visit. A professional rep knows the difference between a customer who needs more time and a customer who has made their decision. Pushing past that line destroys the relationship and the referral.

Signals to Continue

Signals to Step Back
▸  Customer is asking specific questions about the work or materials — engagement, not resistance
▸  Customer has clearly stated a decision-maker is not present and that person needs to be consulted
▸  Customer is comparing line items or asking about scope details — decision-mode thinking
▸  Customer is repeating the same objection after you've addressed it — they've made their decision
▸  Customer references scheduling, timing, or how long the work takes — mentally moving forward
▸  Body language has fully closed — crossed arms, looking away, short answers, checking the time
▸  Customer says 'let me think about it' but has not named a specific barrier
▸  Customer says explicitly they need to wait — for a reason they've named that you cannot resolve today
▸  Body language is open — they're still engaged in the conversation, not closed off
▸  Pushing further will cost the relationship, the referral, and any chance of a future close



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 04 — READING THE ROOM

### Notes:
READING THE ROOM — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide teaches one of the most nuanced skills in sales — knowing when to lean in and when to pull back. It is also one of the hardest to teach because it requires judgment rather than a script.On the signals to continue: the key insight here is that questions and comparisons are buying signals, not objections. A customer who is asking about how long the work takes, what material is used for the vapor barrier, or whether the crew will need to move storage items is a customer who is mentally planning the project. They are not resisting — they are preparing. A rep who interprets these questions as obstacles and pushes harder will interrupt the customer's natural decision-making process. A rep who answers these questions thoroughly and with enthusiasm accelerates it.On 'let me think about it' without a named barrier: this is the most ambiguous signal in the list. When a customer says they need to think about it but cannot name what specifically they're thinking about, the rep has earned one more clarifying question — 'what part of the decision feels unresolved for you?' That question often surfaces a real barrier that can be addressed. But if the customer responds with another vague answer, the rep should accept that this customer needs time and transition to the leave-behind gracefully.On signals to step back: the most important of these is the repeated objection. When a customer raises the same concern after you've already addressed it, they are signaling — usually politely — that they have made their decision and they are not going to change it today. Continuing to respond to the same objection a third or fourth time is not persistence — it is failing to listen. The professional response is to acknowledge their position, transition to the leave-behind, and commit to following up. Preserving the relationship preserves the referral and preserves the chance that they call you when they're ready.

#### Slide 9

STEP  05

The Leave-Behind


Always leave something tangible — always make the ask

Purpose: Whether the customer closed today or not, the appointment is not over until you've left a tangible proposal, made the referral ask, and set a clear follow-up expectation. A customer with a document is dramatically more likely to act than a customer with a memory.

Print or Email the Proposal
Make the Referral Ask




Before you leave, put the full proposal in their hands — printed copy or email confirmation. The proposal should include the accomplish list, itemized scope, total, financing options, and the CAD drawing. Leave nothing to memory.
Every appointment — close or no close — ends with a referral ask. 'Do you know anyone else dealing with something similar in their home?' Customers who were impressed by your professionalism are your best referral sources, even when they didn't buy today.

Set a Follow-Up Expectation
Exit With Professionalism




Before you leave, tell the customer exactly when they'll hear from you. 'I'll give you a call Thursday to answer any questions that come up.' A named day is far more likely to result in contact than 'I'll follow up soon.'
Thank them for their time genuinely. Express confidence that the work will make a real difference for their home. Leave them feeling well-served — not sold to. The last impression is the one that determines whether they call you back.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 05 — THE LEAVE-BEHIND

### Notes:
THE LEAVE-BEHIND — SPEAKER NOTESThe leave-behind is the final professional act of the appointment — and it is as important for the appointments that didn't close as for the ones that did. Many reps treat a non-closing appointment as a failed one and rush through the exit. That is exactly the wrong approach. A customer who didn't buy today but was treated with professionalism throughout is an asset. They may call next month. They may refer a neighbor next week. The leave-behind is what determines which of those outcomes is more likely.On the proposal leave-behind specifically: research on purchasing behavior consistently shows that customers who have a physical or digital document are far more likely to follow up than those who are relying on memory. The human brain is not good at retaining numbers and details from a conversation. The proposal is the system that retains those details on the customer's behalf. When they're discussing the job with their spouse that evening, the proposal gives that conversation structure and specificity. Without it, the conversation is vaguer, the number seems less certain, and the decision is easier to postpone indefinitely.On the referral ask: this must be a habit on every appointment, without exception. Reps who only ask for referrals when they've closed a job are leaving the majority of their referral opportunity on the table. A customer who didn't buy today might know three neighbors who have the same problem. A referral from someone who was genuinely impressed by the Apex process — even without buying — is still a high-trust introduction to a new lead. The ask should be casual and genuine: 'Do you know anyone else dealing with something similar in their home?' Frame it as trying to help others, not generating business for yourself.On setting a specific follow-up day: 'I'll follow up soon' has no accountability and no urgency. 'I'll call you Thursday morning' creates a mutual expectation. The customer knows to expect the call. The rep has a commitment to honor. And on Thursday, the rep has every reason to call because they said they would — which is very different from calling cold and hoping the lead hasn't gone cold.

#### Slide 10

Post-Appointment Follow-Up
The close doesn't end when you leave the driveway
Research consistently shows that 80% of sales that eventually close require five or more follow-up contacts — yet most reps give up after one. The follow-up system is not optional. It is a direct extension of the professional process that began at the 2-day out call.

1

2

3

4

5
Same Day
Day 2–3
Day 7
Day 10–14
Day 21+
Text or Email
First Call
Second Call
Value-Add Touch
Final Decision Ask
Send a brief, warm message the same evening: 'Thank you for your time today. Your proposal is attached — please reach out with any questions.' Confirms professionalism and keeps the proposal top of mind.
Call to check in. 'I wanted to make sure you received everything and see if any questions came up.' Don't push — gather information. Has the spouse seen it? Are there concerns? Adjust your next touch accordingly.
This is the named follow-up you set before leaving. Reference it directly: 'I mentioned I'd reach out Thursday — wanted to honor that.' Specificity signals that you do what you say.
Share something relevant — a note about seasonal timing, a reminder about financing deadlines, or a brief update. This touch is about staying present without being pushy. Something useful, not just a check-in.
Call with a direct, respectful close: 'I want to respect your time — can you let me know if this is still something you're planning to move forward with, or if the timing has changed?' A clear yes or no is better than indefinite follow-up.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  POST-APPOINTMENT FOLLOW-UP

### Notes:
POST-APPOINTMENT FOLLOW-UP — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide exists because follow-up is where the most recoverable revenue in any sales operation gets left on the table. Reps who do excellent appointments but weak follow-up are leaving a disproportionate amount of money behind. The customer liked them. The customer may well have decided to move forward. But the rep called once, didn't reach them, and moved on. The customer eventually called a competitor who called back twice.On the same-day text or email: this touch is low-effort and high-impact. The customer is home, talking about the appointment, and the proposal arrives in their inbox or as a text while the conversation is fresh. The timing is not coincidence — it is strategy. The rep who follows up the same evening is more professional in the customer's mind than the rep who sends the proposal three days later.On the Day 7 call being the named follow-up: the fact that the rep said they would call on Thursday and then calls on Thursday is a trust event. It is small but it is real. Every kept commitment builds credibility. Reps who skip the named follow-up — or who call on a different day without acknowledging the discrepancy — are quietly eroding trust they worked hard to build during the appointment.On the value-add touch at Day 10-14: the purpose of this touch is to stay in the customer's awareness without feeling like harassment. Something genuinely useful — a note about the upcoming rainy season and what that means for their crawl space issue, a reminder that the current financing rate is promotional and has a deadline — gives the rep a reason to make contact that is about the customer's interests, not the rep's quota. That distinction is felt.On the Day 21 final decision ask: teach reps not to be afraid of this call. Getting a clear no is vastly better than indefinite follow-up on a lead that will never close. A clear no frees the rep's time, preserves the relationship ('I appreciate your time and I'll be here if circumstances change'), and occasionally produces a referral. An honest 'this isn't the right time for us' from a well-served customer is not a failure — it is a professional conclusion to a professional process.

#### Slide 11

Close Phase Checklist
Complete every item on every appointment — no exceptions

STEPS 01–02 — Objections & Right-Sizing

STEPS 03–04 — Drawing & Second Ask

STEP 05 — Leave-Behind & Follow-Up

☐  Produced CAD drawing after first ask

☐  Used drawing time as productive breathing room

☐  Resolved with specific evidence, not generalities

☐  Referral ask made on every appointment

☐  Phase 2 documented and follow-up date set
☐  Address-and-ask sequence followed every time
☐  5-touch follow-up sequence initiated



The rep who follows up consistently wins the jobs that weren't closed on the day. Most of the competition quits after one call.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  MODULE 4 — CLOSE PHASE CHECKLIST

### Notes:
CLOSE PHASE CHECKLIST — SPEAKER NOTESUse this as a self-audit after every appointment. The checklist is most valuable when reps complete it honestly on appointments that didn't close — because the pattern of skipped items almost always explains the outcome.The most common skips and their consequences:'Diagnosed objection type before responding' skipped → Rep uses the wrong response tool on the wrong objection. Addressing a value gap with a financing offer, or addressing an affordability issue with more evidence, makes the objection worse rather than better.'Re-anchored to prior agreements after resolving' skipped → Rep successfully addresses the objection but doesn't connect the resolution to the close. The customer says 'okay, thanks for explaining that' — and the conversation goes nowhere because the rep didn't ask again.'Named specific barrier — not just any questions?' skipped → Rep asks if the customer has any questions and gets 'not really.' Conversation ends. Rep leaves without knowing what actually prevented the close.'Referral ask made' skipped → The most commonly skipped item in the entire process. This is pure left-on-the-table revenue with no downside risk. There is no reason not to ask every time.'5-touch follow-up sequence initiated' skipped → Lead goes cold within a week. This is the highest-value item on the list for appointments that didn't close on the day. Implementing a consistent follow-up system for every non-closing appointment would likely be the single highest-ROI change most Apex reps could make to their pipeline.

#### Slide 12

Professional
persistence
wins.



The rep who diagnoses before responding, adjusts scope without surrendering value, gives the customer breathing room, asks again with confidence, and follows up consistently will out-close every rep who relies on talent alone.

Next Module: Post-Appointment Follow-Up — The 5-Touch System, Converting Not-Yet Customers & Referral Capture

### Notes:
CLOSING SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESClose the module with the core message: the close is a process, not a moment, and it rewards the reps who are most systematic — not the ones who are most charismatic.The reps who consistently outperform their peers at the close are almost never the ones who are best at pressure or persuasion. They are the ones who diagnosed objections correctly, right-sized intelligently when necessary, gave customers the dignity of the CAD drawing and the breathing room that comes with it, asked again with calm confidence, and followed up reliably. Those are all learnable behaviors. None of them require unusual talent. They require discipline.Leave reps with this thought: every appointment where you didn't close is a question worth asking honestly. Was it a setup problem from Module 1? Was it a documentation gap from Module 2? Was it a buy-in failure from Module 3? Or was it a close phase failure from Module 4? The process is a diagnostic tool as much as it is a sales system. When a rep knows why they lost a job, they can fix the specific thing that cost them — rather than concluding they just 'didn't click' with the customer.Preview of Module 5: Module 5 takes the follow-up system from this module and builds it out into a full structure — the 5-touch sequence in detail, how to convert customers who said not-yet back into active opportunities, and how to build a referral capture system that generates high-trust leads from the professional reputation Apex builds on every appointment.

#### Slide 1

MODULE 05


SALES PROCESS

5-Touch System  ·  Converting Not-Yet Customers  ·  Referral Capture
FLORIDA PANHANDLE  ·  SOUTH ALABAMA

For Internal Training Use Only

### Notes:
COVER SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESModule 5 is where the money that most reps leave on the table gets recovered. Across every module in this curriculum, the emphasis has been on running a world-class appointment. Module 5 is about what happens after that appointment — and the data is unambiguous: most sales that eventually close require five or more follow-up contacts, yet the average rep gives up after one attempt.There is a specific reason why follow-up is so commonly skipped or done poorly. After a long, demanding appointment — especially one that didn't close on the day — the natural psychological response is to move on. The next lead feels more promising than a prospect who already said 'not yet.' This is exactly the wrong intuition. A prospect who sat through a 3-hour professional appointment, who built an accomplish list with the rep, who received a detailed proposal with a CAD drawing — that prospect has made a significant investment of their time. They are far closer to a yes than a cold lead ever is. Module 5 teaches reps to act accordingly.The three pillars of this module are the 5-touch follow-up sequence, the framework for converting not-yet customers over time, and the referral capture system. Together they turn a non-closing appointment from a failed event into the beginning of a longer relationship that will produce either a job or a referral or both.

#### Slide 2

Post-Appointment Follow-Up
The close doesn't end when you leave the driveway
Most reps treat a non-closing appointment as a failed lead. Top reps treat it as the opening of a longer conversation. The difference in close rate between reps who follow up systematically and those who don't is not marginal — it is the difference between a 20% close rate and a 40% one.
