Key insight: 80% of sales that eventually close require 5+ follow-up contacts. 44% of reps quit after just one. This is where professional persistence separates top performers.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  MODULE 5 — PHASE OVERVIEW

### Notes:
PHASE OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESThe statistic in the callout bar deserves real emphasis in training. 80% of eventual closes require five or more contacts. 44% of reps abandon the lead after a single follow-up attempt. If those two numbers are both true — and the research consistently supports them — then nearly half of all sales reps are systematically abandoning leads just as those leads are entering the window where most closes actually happen.This is not a motivational framing. It is a mathematical one. If the average non-closing appointment has a 20% chance of closing with persistent follow-up, and a rep runs 10 such appointments per month and follows up on all of them, they will recover 2 jobs per month that their competitors are leaving behind. At an average ticket of $8,000 to $15,000, that is a meaningful revenue number — generated entirely from work the rep has already done.The three components of this module each address a different time horizon. The 5-touch system covers the first three weeks after an appointment. The not-yet customer framework covers the period from three weeks out to as far as a year or more — because some customers genuinely need time, and a rep who stays professionally present through that period is the one who gets the call when they're ready. Referral capture is a parallel track that runs alongside both, turning every appointment — closed or not — into a potential lead source.

#### Slide 3

STEP  01

The 5-Touch Follow-Up System


Structure · Timing · Purpose

Purpose: A structured follow-up sequence ensures that every non-closing appointment receives the same professional persistence — not just the ones the rep feels good about. Consistency is the system. Instinct is not.

1

2

3

4

5
Same Day
Day 2–3
Day 7
Day 10–14
Day 21+
Text or Email
First Call
Named Follow-Up
Value-Add Touch
Final Decision Ask
Send a warm confirmation the same evening. 'Thank you for your time today — your proposal is attached. Please reach out with any questions.' Keeps the job top of mind while the conversation is still fresh.
Call to check in. 'I wanted to make sure you received everything and see if any questions came up.' Gather information — don't push. Has the spouse seen it? Are there concerns that surfaced after you left?
This is the specific day you named before leaving. Reference it: 'I mentioned I'd reach out Thursday — wanted to honor that.' Honoring a commitment you made signals that Apex does what it says.
Share something relevant and useful — seasonal timing information, a financing deadline reminder, or a brief note about what happens to their specific issue if it sits. Present it as information, not pressure.
Call with a direct, respectful close: 'I want to respect your time — can you let me know if this is still something you're planning to move forward with, or if the timing has changed?' A clear answer is better than indefinite follow-up.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 01 — THE 5-TOUCH SYSTEM

### Notes:
THE 5-TOUCH SYSTEM — OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESThe most important concept to establish here is the difference between a follow-up system and follow-up activity. Activity is calling when you remember to, following up when you feel like it, and moving on when a lead feels cold. A system is a defined sequence of contacts with specific timing and specific purpose — one that runs on every non-closing appointment without exception.Activity produces inconsistent results. A system produces predictable ones.On the same-day text or email: the timing of this touch is as important as the content. The customer is home, talking about the visit with their spouse, and the proposal arrives in their inbox that evening. The psychological effect is significant — the rep is demonstrating that they follow through immediately, which reinforces every promise made during the appointment. A rep who sends the proposal three days later has already sent a message about how they operate.On the Day 7 named follow-up: spend time on this one in training. Most reps don't name a specific day before leaving because it feels like a commitment they're not sure they'll keep. That's exactly backwards. Naming the day and then calling on that day is one of the lowest-effort, highest-impact trust events available to a rep. 'I mentioned I'd reach out Thursday' is a sentence that immediately differentiates the rep from every competitor who said 'I'll be in touch.'On the value-add touch at Day 10–14: the purpose of this contact is to stay in the customer's awareness without feeling like harassment. The key is that it must be genuinely useful to the customer — not just a check-in disguised as useful information. A real note about what happens to a moisture-saturated crawl space through a rainy season is useful. 'Just checking to see if you've made a decision' is not, and customers distinguish between the two immediately.On the Day 21 final decision ask: train reps not to fear this call. A clear no is not a failure — it is a professional conclusion that frees the rep's time and often produces a referral. The worst outcome in follow-up is indefinite ambiguity, not a clear no.

#### Slide 4

STEP  01

Touch 1 & 2 — Scripts
Same-day and first call word for word

TOUCH 1 — SAME DAY  (Text or Email)

YOU →
"Hi [Name] — thank you for your time today. It was a pleasure meeting you both. Your full proposal is attached, including the site drawing. Please don't hesitate to reach out if any questions come up."

GOAL →
Warm, brief, and professional. Arrives the same evening while the appointment is still fresh. The attached proposal is the anchor — it keeps the conversation alive in writing.

TOUCH 2 — DAY 2–3  (Phone Call)

YOU →
"Hi [Name], this is [Your Name] with Apex — I wanted to make sure you received the proposal and see if any questions came up after we talked."

GOAL →
Open-ended check-in, not a push. Listen more than talk. Has the spouse seen the proposal? Did any concerns surface after the appointment?

YOU →
IF SPOUSE HASN'T SEEN IT: "Would it be helpful if I set aside a few minutes to walk you both through the key points over the phone? I want to make sure everyone has the full picture."

GOAL →
This recovers the missing-decision-maker situation from Module 1. A phone walkthrough with both parties is the next best thing to an in-person presentation.

YOU →
IF CONCERN SURFACES: "That's a great question — let me address that directly..." [Resolve it, then:] "With that in mind, does moving forward make sense for you?"



GOAL →
Address and ask. Every resolved concern on a follow-up call earns a renewed ask. Don't address and wait — address and close.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 01 — TOUCH 1 & 2 SCRIPTS

### Notes:
TOUCH 1 & 2 SCRIPTS — SPEAKER NOTESOn Touch 1 delivery: the same-day message should be sent before 9pm on the day of the appointment. It should be warm but brief — the goal is not to re-pitch. The goal is to confirm professionalism, deliver the proposal, and make the rep easy to reach if questions arise that evening. Reps who send this the next day, or who forget to send it at all, are missing the highest-value timing window.On the attachment: the proposal should be a clean PDF that includes the itemized scope, the total, the financing options, and the CAD drawing. A customer who opens the proposal with their spouse that evening and finds a professional, complete document is a customer who is evaluating Apex against a high standard. A customer who opens a rough notes document or a photo of a handwritten quote is not.On Touch 2 — the critical diagnostic frame: this call is about gathering information, not delivering a pitch. The rep who calls on Day 2 already knowing what they're going to say is going to miss the most important information — what happened in the customer's household after the rep left. Did the spouse react well to the proposal? Did a concern come up that wasn't voiced during the appointment? Did a competing quote arrive? The rep who calls to listen will always outperform the rep who calls to talk.On the missing spouse situation: when the rep learns that the other decision-maker hasn't seen the proposal, the instinct is to say 'okay, let me know what they think.' That instinct is wrong. Offer to walk both of them through it together — by phone, if necessary. A guided walkthrough is infinitely more persuasive than an unsupported document review. Most customers will say yes to the offer, and that call becomes the real second appointment.

#### Slide 5

STEP  01

Touch 3, 4 & 5 — Scripts
Named follow-up, value add, and the final decision ask

TOUCH 3 — DAY 7  (Named Follow-Up)

YOU →
"Hi [Name], it's [Your Name] with Apex — I mentioned before I left that I'd give you a call this week, so I wanted to honor that. How are you two feeling about everything?"

GOAL →
Reference the commitment you made. This one sentence separates you from every rep who said 'I'll be in touch.' It signals that Apex keeps its word on small things — so it will keep its word on big ones.

TOUCH 4 — DAY 10–14  (Value-Add)

YOU →
"Hi [Name] — I wanted to pass along something you might find useful. We're heading into [season] — the kind of conditions that typically accelerate the type of moisture issue I documented in your crawl space. I wanted to make sure you had that context as you're thinking things through."

GOAL →
Genuinely useful, not manufactured urgency. This works because it is honest. Evidence-based timing information gives the customer relevant context — it doesn't invent pressure that doesn't exist.

TOUCH 5 — DAY 21+  (Final Ask)

YOU →
"Hi [Name] — I want to respect your time and I don't want to keep calling without purpose. Can you help me understand where things stand — is this still something you're planning to move forward with, or has the timing changed for you?"

GOAL →
Direct, respectful, and requires a real answer. A clear 'not right now' is not a failure — it opens the door to asking when would be better and to making the referral ask. A clear yes closes the job.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 01 — TOUCH 3, 4 & 5 SCRIPTS

### Notes:
TOUCH 3, 4 & 5 SCRIPTS — SPEAKER NOTESOn Touch 3 — the named follow-up: the psychological effect of this call comes entirely from the reference to the prior commitment. 'I mentioned I'd call this week' is not just a reminder — it is a demonstration of character. The customer is experiencing in real time the kind of company Apex is. A rep who follows through on a small commitment made before leaving has just provided evidence that they will follow through on every commitment in the proposal. Train reps to always name a specific day before they leave, and then to lead with that reference when they call.On Touch 4 — value-add vs. manufactured urgency: this distinction is worth significant time in training because reps often get this wrong. Manufactured urgency sounds like: 'I wanted to let you know our pricing changes next month' or 'We're booking up fast for this season.' Customers recognize these tactics immediately and they damage trust. Value-add sounds like: 'We're heading into the rainy season and I wanted to make sure you had the context about what that typically means for a crawl space with the moisture levels I documented.' The difference is that one is true, specific to this customer's situation, and useful. That customer may not close today. But they will remember Apex as the company that gave them honest information without pressure.On Touch 5 — the final ask: many reps avoid this call because they fear making the customer feel pressured. The opposite framing is more accurate. After four professional, non-pushy contacts, calling to ask for a clear answer is respectful — not aggressive. You're giving the customer permission to say no without guilt, which paradoxically makes it easier for them to say yes. And if they say 'not right now,' the follow-up question — 'when would be a better time to revisit this?' — often produces a specific future window that becomes a real appointment.

#### Slide 6

STEP  02

Converting Not-Yet Customers


Some customers need time — a system keeps you in front of them

Purpose: After the 5-touch sequence, some customers are genuinely not ready — not forever, but not yet. A not-yet customer who was well-served by Apex is a high-probability future job. The rep who stays present professionally will be the one they call.

Understand Why They Waited
Stay Present Without Pressure




Common legitimate reasons: financial timing, a major life event, wanting to observe the issue through another season, or waiting for a spouse's schedule to clear. None of these are no — they are scheduling. Treat them accordingly.
A monthly or quarterly check-in — by text, email, or brief call — keeps Apex top of mind without feeling like harassment. The key is that each contact adds something: a seasonal note, a relevant update, a 'just thinking of you' with genuine warmth.

Track and Re-Engage at Trigger Events
Keep the Proposal Current




Follow the customer on social media or in local community groups if appropriate. Life events — selling a house, a new baby, a renovation project — are natural trigger points for re-engagement. Reach out with context, not just a call.
If significant time has passed, offer a brief re-inspection before presenting updated numbers. This demonstrates care and ensures the scope still reflects current conditions. It also re-anchors the customer in the evidence without starting from scratch.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 02 — CONVERTING NOT-YET CUSTOMERS

### Notes:
CONVERTING NOT-YET CUSTOMERS — SPEAKER NOTESThe mindset shift to establish here: a not-yet customer is not a lost lead — they are a future job. This reframe changes everything about how the rep manages their pipeline. A rep who writes off non-closing appointments moves on and generates more leads. A rep who maintains a not-yet pipeline converts those leads at a dramatically higher rate than cold outreach because the trust work has already been done.On understanding why they waited: it is worth asking directly, with empathy, when the timing question comes up. 'I completely understand — can I ask what's driving the timing for you?' The answer to that question tells the rep everything about how to maintain the relationship. A customer who is waiting for a tax refund has a very specific re-engagement window. A customer who wants to observe the issue through the rainy season has told you exactly when to call back.On staying present without pressure: the standard to set is this — every contact with a not-yet customer should add something of value or acknowledge something real about their situation. A generic 'just checking in, have you decided yet?' is noise. A 'hey, we're heading into hurricane season and I was thinking about the drainage situation on the north side of your foundation' is a conversation. One feels like a sales call. The other feels like a professional looking out for them.On re-inspection for older proposals: this is a service differentiator that almost no competitor offers. When a rep calls a customer whose proposal is six months old and says 'I'd like to come back out and make sure the scope still reflects your current situation before we talk numbers again,' the customer hears something powerful: this company is more interested in getting it right than in closing a deal that was written before conditions changed. That reputation produces referrals before the job even closes.

#### Slide 7

STEP  02

The Long-Game Re-Engagement
How to reach back out after weeks or months
These scripts are for customers who responded to the 5-touch sequence with 'not yet' — and are now in your long-term follow-up pipeline. The goal is warmth, relevance, and a soft opening — not a hard close.

SEASONAL RE-ENGAGEMENT

YOU →
"Hi [Name], it's [Your Name] with Apex — I hope you're doing well. We're heading into [rainy season / summer humidity / freeze-thaw period] and your crawl space came to mind. How has everything been looking over there?"

GOAL →
Seasonal touchpoints are natural and credible. They demonstrate you're thinking about this customer's specific situation — not just running a follow-up script.

LONG-TERM CHECK-IN  (3–6 months)

YOU →
"Hi [Name] — it's been a little while and I just wanted to check in. Has anything changed with the situation we looked at, or has the timing lined up for you?"

GOAL →
Simple, low-pressure, and gives the customer an easy on-ramp to re-engage. Many customers will say 'actually, we've been thinking about it' — because they have.

RE-INSPECTION OFFER  (6+ months)

YOU →
"Hi [Name] — it's [Your Name]. It's been several months since I was out, and I want to make sure the proposal still reflects what's actually going on with your home. I'd like to come back out and take a quick look — no charge — just to make sure the scope is still accurate before we revisit anything."



GOAL →
This is the most powerful long-game move available. It resets the trust clock, produces updated evidence, and demonstrates that Apex cares about accuracy more than closing a stale deal.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 02 — LONG-GAME RE-ENGAGEMENT SCRIPTS

### Notes:
LONG-GAME RE-ENGAGEMENT — SPEAKER NOTESThe key to all three scripts on this slide is the same: specificity. The rep who re-engages with something specific to this customer's situation will always outperform the rep who sends a generic follow-up. 'Hey, just checking in' is deleted. 'Hey, we're heading into hurricane season and your crawl space drainage came to mind' is opened and responded to.On the seasonal re-engagement: this script works because it is honest. If the rep inspected a crawl space with active moisture and high wood moisture content, then calling before rainy season is genuinely warranted. The customer feels cared for rather than prospected. And the rep has a real reason to call — not a manufactured one. The foundation repair industry has natural seasonal timing built in. Use it.On the 3–6 month check-in: this script is deliberately low-key. After a customer has been in the pipeline for several months, a high-energy sales call will feel tone-deaf. This script gives the customer an easy, low-stakes re-entry point into the conversation. The rep asks a soft question and then listens. Often the customer will volunteer that they've been thinking about it, that circumstances have changed, or that they're ready to move forward. The rep's job on this call is mostly to get out of the way and let the customer talk.On the re-inspection offer: this is the highest-commitment long-game move, and it is worth the investment. Coming back out after six months demonstrates something no competitor is doing — that Apex is more interested in doing the job right than in closing a proposal that may no longer reflect current conditions. The re-inspection almost always produces a job within 60 days of the visit. And the proposal that comes out of it is stronger, because it is based on what is actually happening in the crawl space today.

#### Slide 8

STEP  03

Referral Capture


Every appointment is a referral opportunity — closed or not

Purpose: Referral leads close at more than 50% — nearly three times the rate of advertising leads. They arrive pre-warmed by trust and require far less education. A systematic referral capture process turns every appointment into a lead-generation event regardless of whether it closed.

When to Make the Ask

How to Frame the Ask
▸  On every appointment — before leaving, whether the job closed or not
▸  Keep it casual and genuine.  'Do you know anyone else dealing with something similar in their home?' Curiosity, not a pitch.
▸  During the leave-behind step of the close, after the proposal has been delivered
▸  Frame it as helping others.  'I'd love to be able to help someone else the same way I've tried to help you today.' Service, not sales.
▸  After a successful follow-up call when the customer is re-engaged and positive
▸  Never pressure for a name.  If they hesitate, move on. A forced referral is worth less than an organic one and damages rapport.
▸  After a job is completed — this is the warmest moment and produces the highest-quality referrals
▸  Follow up on every referral immediately.  Contact referrals within 24 hours and mention the name of the person who referred them in the first sentence.
▸  When a customer mentions a neighbor, family member, or friend who has a similar issue
▸  Close the loop with the referrer.  Let them know you reached out to their referral. This makes them more likely to refer again.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 03 — REFERRAL CAPTURE

### Notes:
REFERRAL CAPTURE — OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESOpen this section with the close rate data point. Referral leads close at over 50%, compared to 15–20% for advertising leads. That gap is not a coincidence — it reflects the fundamental difference in how trust works. An advertising lead found Apex through a search result. A referral lead was told about Apex by someone they already trust. The rep's credibility is borrowed from the referrer before the first call is made. That pre-established trust is worth a significant multiple of the marketing spend it would take to produce an equivalent effect through advertising.On making the ask on every appointment: this is the most consistently skipped step in the entire Apex process. After a long appointment, especially one that didn't close, the rep's energy is depleted and leaving quickly feels appealing. The referral ask takes 15 seconds. There is no logical argument for skipping it. Make it a non-negotiable standard — if the rep leaves without making the referral ask, the appointment is incomplete regardless of whether it closed.On framing it as helping others: this framing is not manipulative — it is accurate. If a rep has genuinely served the customer well, whether or not they bought today, then extending that service to someone the customer knows and cares about is a good outcome for everyone. The customer who hears 'I'd love to be able to help someone else the same way' experiences the ask as a continuation of the service they just received, not as a sales tactic appended to the end of the visit.On closing the loop with the referrer: this is the step that generates repeat referrals. When a customer refers someone and then hears back that you reached out, the message they receive is clear — Apex follows through. The next time they hear someone mention a foundation concern, they will think of Apex and they will make the referral without being asked.

#### Slide 9

STEP  03

Referral Scripts
The ask, the follow-up call, and closing the loop
Three moments. Three scripts. The referral system only works if the rep executes all three — not just the first ask.

THE ASK  (Before leaving — every appointment)

YOU →
"One last thing — do you happen to know anyone else in the neighborhood or in your family who might be dealing with something similar? I'd love to be able to help them the same way I've tried to help you today."

GOAL →
Casual, genuine, framed as service. Pair it with a card or contact info. If they give a name, write it down in front of them — that small act signals that you take referrals seriously.

THE REFERRAL CALL  (Within 24 hours of receiving the name)

YOU →
"Hi [Referral Name] — my name is [Your Name] with Apex Foundation Specialists. [Customer Name] suggested I give you a call — they mentioned you might have some concerns about your home's foundation. I'd love to set aside some time to come out and take a look if that's helpful."

GOAL →
Lead with the referrer's name in the first sentence — always. That name is the trust bridge. Everything that follows is easier because of it. Reach out within 24 hours while the referral is still fresh.

CLOSING THE LOOP  (After reaching the referral)

YOU →
"Hey [Customer Name] — I just wanted to let you know I reached out to [Referral Name] and we're going to set up a time to come out and take a look. I really appreciate you thinking of us. It means a lot."



GOAL →
Short, warm, and high-impact. This is the step that turns a one-time referrer into a repeat referrer. They now know their referral was followed through on — which tells them everything about how Apex operates.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  STEP 03 — REFERRAL SCRIPTS

### Notes:
REFERRAL SCRIPTS — SPEAKER NOTESOn the referral ask script: the phrase 'the same way I've tried to help you today' is deliberate. It connects the referral ask back to the service experience the customer just had. If the appointment was well-run — thorough inspection, professional proposal, honest advice — then this phrase is accurate and the customer hears it as such. If the appointment felt transactional, this phrase will ring hollow. This is another reason why running an excellent appointment matters beyond the immediate close. The quality of the appointment determines the quality of the referral ask.On the 24-hour rule for referral calls: this is non-negotiable. A referral that is not contacted within 24 hours begins to cool. The customer who gave you the name may mention to the referral that someone from Apex might call — and if that call doesn't come for a week, the referral's expectations are already disappointed. More importantly, the rep who calls within 24 hours demonstrates the same professional responsiveness that Apex promises to deliver as a contractor. The referral call is itself a first impression of how Apex operates.On leading with the referrer's name: this cannot be overemphasized. The difference between 'Hi, I'm with Apex Foundation Specialists — do you have a few minutes?' and 'Hi, [Customer Name] suggested I give you a call' is the difference between a cold call and a warm introduction. The referral call should never start without that name. Write it down before you dial. It is the most valuable word in the conversation.On closing the loop: most reps skip this step because they are already thinking about the next appointment. This is a mistake with compounding consequences. The customer who referred someone and never hears back from you about it will not refer again. The customer who gets a brief, warm call saying you reached out and things are in motion will refer three more people over the next year. Close the loop every time — it takes 60 seconds and it is the foundation of a referral-driven pipeline.

#### Slide 10

Building Your Follow-Up Pipeline
Treat your not-yet leads like the assets they are
Every non-closing appointment should live somewhere in an organized system — not in memory, not in a text thread, and not on a sticky note. A follow-up pipeline is the infrastructure that turns the 5-touch system from an intention into a habit.








ACTIVE FOLLOW-UP
NOT YET
LONG TERM
REFERRAL ACTIVE
Days 1–21
Weeks 3–12
3 Months+
Parallel Track
Customers currently in the 5-touch sequence. These get daily attention. The rep knows exactly where each one is in the sequence and what the next contact is. No one falls through the cracks in this window.
Customers who completed the 5-touch sequence without closing. These get monthly check-ins with a value-add framing. Track the reason for their delay so each contact can be relevant to their specific situation.
Customers who need more time. Seasonal re-engagement, trigger-event outreach, and re-inspection offers live here. These are future jobs — not dead leads. A well-managed long-term pipeline produces multiple closes per rep per year from no additional marketing spend.
Every customer who gave a referral or who is a referral source. This list gets priority relationship management — closing-the-loop calls, job completion thank-yous, and periodic check-ins. Referral sources are the highest-value relationships in the pipeline.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  BUILDING THE FOLLOW-UP PIPELINE

### Notes:
BUILDING THE FOLLOW-UP PIPELINE — SPEAKER NOTESThe purpose of this slide is to make the abstract concept of a follow-up pipeline concrete. Most reps know they should follow up more consistently. What they lack is not motivation — it is a system that makes consistent follow-up easy. This slide introduces the four-stage pipeline structure that turns the 5-touch system and the not-yet framework into an organized, manageable process.On the Active Follow-Up bucket: the standard to set is that every appointment produces a same-day entry into this bucket. The rep should know, at any point in time, exactly where each customer is in the 5-touch sequence and what the next contact is. This is not complicated — it can live in a CRM, a spreadsheet, or even a well-organized notes app. The format matters less than the habit.On the Not-Yet bucket: the key discipline here is capturing the reason for the delay when it is given. A customer who said 'we're waiting for our tax refund' needs to hear from you in April. A customer who said 'my husband wants to wait and see how the rainy season goes' needs to hear from you after the first significant rain event. Generic monthly check-ins are fine — but personalized re-engagement based on the customer's own stated reason for waiting is significantly more effective.On the Referral Active bucket: this is the most valuable list in the entire pipeline and it is the one most reps never formalize. Every customer who has referred someone — or who has indicated they might — belongs on this list and belongs in a relationship management cadence. These are not just leads. They are trust ambassadors. Managing these relationships deliberately, with regular appreciation and communication, is how a rep builds a self-generating referral engine over the course of a career.Tool recommendation: while any tracking system works, encourage reps to use whatever tool they will actually maintain. A perfect CRM that never gets updated is worse than a simple spreadsheet that the rep checks every morning. Consistency of use beats sophistication of tool.

Complete every item on every appointment — no exceptions

The rep who follows up consistently wins jobs their competition doesn't know they lost.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  MODULE 5 — POST-APPOINTMENT CHECKLIST

### Notes:
POST-APPOINTMENT CHECKLIST — SPEAKER NOTESUse this as a self-audit tool after every appointment. The checklist is most powerful when reps treat it as a measure of professional completion — every appointment is incomplete until these items are done, regardless of whether the job closed.The most commonly skipped items and what skipping them costs:'Same-day text or email' skipped — The proposal reaches the customer the next day or later, missing the highest-value window when the appointment is still fresh in both partners' minds. The rep who sends same-day has already differentiated from 90% of competitors before the first follow-up call.'Named follow-up day honored' skipped — The rep called Thursday 'soon' and then called the following Wednesday. The customer barely noticed. The named rep called on Thursday exactly as promised. The customer told their spouse 'that Apex guy called exactly when he said he would.' These two reps are playing different games.'Referral ask made on every appointment' skipped — This is the most expensive skip in the checklist. A single referral per month, at a 50% close rate and an $8,000 average ticket, is $48,000 per year in revenue generated from a 15-second question that costs nothing. Make the ask every time.'Loop closed with referrer after contact made' skipped — The referrer hears nothing. They mentioned Apex to their friend, nothing visible happened, and they mentally file Apex as a company that doesn't follow through. The next time someone mentions a foundation concern, they think twice before bringing up Apex. The loop closure is not just courtesy — it is reputation maintenance.

#### Slide 12

Most of your
competition quits
after one call.



The rep who follows up five times, manages a not-yet pipeline, and captures referrals systematically will out-earn every rep who relies on appointment quality alone. The appointment opens the door. The follow-up is what closes it.

This completes the Apex Sales Process Curriculum — Modules 1 through 5.

### Notes:
CLOSING SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESClose Module 5 — and the full curriculum — with the cumulative message of the entire series.The Apex Sales Process is not a collection of scripts and techniques. It is a system — a deliberately designed sequence of professional behaviors that compound on each other. The 2-day out call sets up the inspection. The inspection sets up the presentation. The presentation sets up the proposal. The proposal sets up the close. And the follow-up system captures everything the close didn't.Every rep in the room now has a complete picture of what an excellent Apex appointment looks like from first contact to final referral. The question is not whether they understand the system — they do. The question is whether they will execute it consistently, appointment after appointment, until it is no longer something they have to think about. Until it is simply how they work.Close with a challenge to the team: pick one step from each module that you don't currently do consistently, and make that step your focus for the next 30 days. Not five things — one thing per module, five total. Work on them deliberately. Track them against the checklists. In 30 days, those five habits will have moved your close rate, your referral rate, and your follow-up conversion rate in ways that will be visible in your numbers.The system works. The reps who work the system win.

#### Slide 1

MODULE 06


SALES PROCESS

Psychology

How Homeowners Decide  ·  Emotional Drivers  ·  Applying the Science
FLORIDA PANHANDLE  ·  SOUTH ALABAMA

For Internal Training Use Only

### Notes:
COVER SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESModule 6 is different from every module that came before it. Modules 1 through 5 taught reps what to do and when to do it. Module 6 teaches why any of it works — the psychological principles that explain why the Apex process produces closes at the rate it does when it is executed correctly.Understanding the psychology behind the sales process does two things for a rep. First, it makes them better at executing the process because they understand what each step is actually accomplishing in the customer's mind. A rep who knows that the accomplishment list creates commitment through the consistency principle will be more careful and deliberate about building it. A rep who understands loss aversion will know exactly how to frame the consequences of inaction without resorting to fear tactics. Second, it makes reps more adaptable. When something in the process isn't landing, a rep who understands the psychology can diagnose why and adjust, rather than simply repeating the same script louder.The goal of this module is not to make reps manipulative. It is to make them fluent in how human beings actually make high-stakes financial decisions — so that the Apex process serves customers in a way that is both effective and genuinely in their interest. The most powerful insight in this entire module is that the psychological principles that produce closes are the same ones that produce good customer outcomes. Helping a customer understand a real problem clearly enough to make a confident decision is not manipulation. It is professional service.

#### Slide 2

Customer Psychology
Understanding why customers buy — and why they don't
Customers don't make high-ticket decisions the way they buy groceries. A $12,000 foundation repair triggers a completely different decision-making process than a routine purchase. Understanding that process is what separates reps who close consistently from those who rely on luck.

01
02
03
04








How Decisions Are Made
The Core Emotional Drivers
The Principles in Practice
Reading Buyer Personalities
Emotion first · Logic second
Fear · Trust · Identity · Urgency
Commitment · Loss · Social proof
Four types · One process



Key insight: Customers don't object to price — they object to uncertainty. The entire Apex process is designed to eliminate uncertainty before the number appears.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  MODULE 6 — OVERVIEW

### Notes:
OVERVIEW — SPEAKER NOTESThe key insight in the callout bar at the bottom of this slide deserves to be the through-line of the entire module. Customers don't object to price — they object to uncertainty. When a customer says 'that seems expensive,' what they almost always mean underneath that statement is one of three things: 'I'm not sure the problem is really that serious,' 'I'm not sure your solution is actually the right one,' or 'I'm not sure I can trust your company to deliver what you're promising.' All three of those are forms of uncertainty. None of them are actually about the number.This reframe is transformative for reps, because it changes the question from 'how do I justify my price?' to 'how do I eliminate the uncertainty that is making the price feel unjustifiable?' The answer to the second question is the entire Apex process — the systematic documentation, the buy-in questions, the education, the co-built proposal. Every step is an uncertainty-reduction mechanism.The four sections of this module build on each other. Understanding how decisions are made gives reps the foundational mental model. Understanding the emotional drivers gives them the vocabulary for what's happening in the customer's mind at each moment. Understanding the psychological principles in practice gives them the specific levers the Apex process is pulling. And understanding buyer personalities gives them the adaptability to modulate their approach based on who they're talking to.

#### Slide 3

PART  01

How Decisions Are Made


Emotion opens the door · Logic walks through it

Purpose: Before a rep can influence how a customer decides, they need to understand the sequence in which decisions actually happen. Most reps act as though logic drives purchase decisions. The science says otherwise.

The Emotional Brain Acts First

The Logical Brain Justifies After
▸  Decisions are made emotionally.  Neurological research shows that the emotional centers of the brain activate before the rational centers in virtually every purchasing decision.
▸  Logic does not drive the decision —  it defends it. After the emotional brain has leaned toward yes, the logical brain looks for reasons to confirm that choice.
▸  The emotional driver is always present,  even when customers say 'I'm just doing research' or 'I just want to see what it costs.' There is an emotional stake underneath every inquiry.
▸  This is why documentation matters so much.  Photos, measurements, and the education slides give the logical brain the evidence it needs to ratify a decision the emotional brain has already started to make.
▸  In foundation repair, the primary emotions  are fear (my home is at risk), protection (I want to keep my family safe), and identity (I take care of what I own).
▸  Objections are almost always logical,  even when they feel emotional. 'That seems expensive' is the logical brain trying to pump the brakes on a decision the emotional brain is ready to make.
▸  A rep who connects to the emotional driver  before presenting facts will always outperform a rep who leads with technical specifications and measurements.
▸  The rep's job is to serve both brains:  connect emotionally so the customer wants to say yes, then provide logical evidence so the customer feels confident saying yes.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  PART 01 — HOW DECISIONS ARE MADE

### Notes:
HOW DECISIONS ARE MADE — SPEAKER NOTESThis is the foundational concept of the entire module, and it is worth significant time in training because it directly challenges how most reps think about their job. Most reps believe they are in the business of presenting facts and evidence that lead a logical customer to a rational conclusion. That model is not wrong — but it is incomplete in a way that costs closes.The actual sequence is almost always: emotional recognition of the problem → emotional motivation to act → logical evaluation of options → logical justification for a decision already leaning toward yes. The rep who enters this sequence at the logical evaluation stage — who shows up with measurements and slide decks without first connecting to the customer's emotional stake — is starting in the middle of the movie. The customer's emotional brain has already decided whether it trusts this person and whether it wants to engage. The logical presentation that follows is either confirming a positive emotional impression or trying to overcome a negative one.For foundation repair specifically, the emotional stakes are unusually high. This is not a discretionary purchase like a kitchen renovation. A homeowner who has discovered foundation problems is often frightened — their most significant financial asset may be compromised, their family's safety could be at risk, and they feel exposed because they don't understand the problem well enough to evaluate the solutions being offered. A rep who acknowledges and validates that emotional reality before presenting anything technical will build trust faster than any amount of credentials or documentation.Practical application: the walk-around question 'what's your biggest concern about it?' from Module 2 is not just a data-gathering exercise. It is an invitation for the customer to articulate their emotional driver. The rep who listens to the answer and reflects it back — 'so your main concern is that this is going to get worse and affect the structural integrity of the floor above it' — has just demonstrated emotional intelligence. That demonstration is worth more at the close than any measurement reading.

#### Slide 4

PART  02

The Core Emotional Drivers


Every foundation customer is moved by one of four motivations

Purpose: Identifying a customer's primary emotional driver within the first 15 minutes of an appointment changes everything about how the rep should frame the problem, the solution, and the close.





FEAR & LOSS AVERSION

PROTECTION & SAFETY
"What happens if I don't fix this?"
"I need to keep my family safe."
The most common driver in foundation repair. The customer is afraid their home is losing value, becoming structurally unsafe, or getting worse with each passing month. Loss aversion is twice as powerful as the desire for gain — meaning the fear of losing $20K in home equity motivates more action than the promise of gaining $20K.
Particularly strong when there are children in the home, when a customer is risk-averse by nature, or when the issue involves structural elements above living spaces. These customers respond to clarity about risk — they want to understand exactly what is and is not in danger and what the repair will definitively resolve.





HOME AS IDENTITY

FINANCIAL & EQUITY
"I take care of what I own."
"What does this do to my home's value?"
These customers see home maintenance as a reflection of who they are. They are often detail-oriented, ask technical questions, and want to understand the repair as thoroughly as a professional would. They respond to competence signals — your documentation quality, the CAD drawing, and the specificity of your measurements all register strongly with this type.
Buyers approaching a sale, recent purchasers, and investors are often motivated primarily by financial impact. These customers respond to ROI framing — what does the repair cost vs. what does it protect or restore in home value? They are often the most comfortable discussing numbers and the most receptive to a well-structured proposal.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  PART 02 — CORE EMOTIONAL DRIVERS

### Notes:
CORE EMOTIONAL DRIVERS — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide gives reps a diagnostic framework for the most important question they can ask themselves within the first 15 minutes of any appointment: what is this customer's primary motivation? Getting that answer right changes how the rep frames every subsequent conversation — the problem description, the education, the proposal, and the close.On Loss Aversion specifically: this is the most important psychological principle in high-ticket home services sales, and it is worth spending extra time here. Daniel Kahneman's research established that humans feel the pain of a loss approximately twice as intensely as the pleasure of an equivalent gain. For foundation repair, this means that framing the problem as 'this is costing you home equity every month it goes unaddressed' is approximately twice as motivating as framing it as 'this repair will increase your home's value.' Both statements can be true. But the loss frame activates a more powerful psychological response. The rep who frames the consequences of inaction in terms of what the customer stands to lose — not what they stand to gain — is working with human psychology rather than against it.On identifying the driver in practice: the walk-around questions in Module 2 are specifically designed to surface this. 'What's your biggest concern about it?' is the direct route. But listening carefully to how the customer describes the problem also reveals the driver. A customer who says 'I'm worried about this before we list the house' is a financial driver. A customer who says 'I've got three young kids and I don't want them living on a compromised floor' is a safety driver. A customer who says 'I've been working on this house for 20 years and this is driving me crazy' is an identity driver.On using the driver in the close: once the rep has identified the primary driver, they should return to it explicitly at the proposal stage. 'We talked earlier about your concern for your kids — what we're proposing today eliminates that risk entirely.' That sentence connects the proposal to the customer's deepest motivation. It is not manipulation — it is relevance.

#### Slide 5

PART  02

Loss Aversion — The Most Powerful Driver
Framing inaction as loss is twice as motivating as framing action as gain
Loss aversion is not a sales tactic — it is a documented feature of human psychology. Used honestly, it helps customers understand the real cost of waiting. Used dishonestly, it is fear-mongering. The Apex standard is always evidence-based urgency.
“

The pain of losing $15,000 in home equity is felt more than twice as intensely as the pleasure of gaining $15,000. This means the fear of loss is your most honest and most powerful motivator — when backed by real evidence.






Framing That Works
Evidence-Based, Not Fear-Based
The Equity Conversation
"Based on the moisture levels I measured, wood members at this saturation typically begin to degrade within one to two wet seasons. That's the difference between a $6,000 repair today and a $20,000 structural repair in two years."

This is honest, specific to this customer's measurements, and frames the cost of waiting concretely.
Every loss-aversion framing must be backed by the rep's actual documentation. The moisture reading must be real. The cost escalation range must be credible. A rep who invents urgency will be caught — either in the moment by a skeptical customer or later when the prediction doesn't match reality.
For customers motivated by financial impact: "Foundation issues are one of the top five factors that reduce a home's sale price and extend time on market. The documentation I'm leaving you today actually helps — buyers and their inspectors will see a professional assessment and a proposed solution rather than an unknown problem."



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  PART 02 — LOSS AVERSION

### Notes:
LOSS AVERSION — SPEAKER NOTESThis is the slide that, handled well, will produce the single biggest mindset shift for reps in the entire curriculum. Loss aversion is the most well-documented and most powerful principle in behavioral economics, and it maps almost perfectly onto the foundation repair sales context.The concept, established by Kahneman and Tversky in their landmark research on prospect theory, is that losses are felt approximately twice as intensely as equivalent gains. A homeowner who stands to lose $15,000 in equity due to an unaddressed foundation issue will be motivated to act approximately twice as strongly as a homeowner who is told they stand to gain $15,000 by doing the repair. The math is the same. The psychological experience is radically different.For foundation repair, this principle applies directly and honestly. A crawl space with elevated wood moisture content, active drainage issues, and compromised sill plates is genuinely going to get worse if left unaddressed. That is not an invented sales tactic — it is the nature of moisture damage. A rep who communicates the real, evidence-based consequences of inaction is giving the customer information they genuinely need. The loss framing is not manipulation when it is accurate.The standard Apex holds is evidence-based urgency. The rep's documentation — the moisture readings, the photos, the measurement data — must support every claim about what happens if the customer waits. A rep who says 'if you wait, this will cost you twice as much' must be able to explain specifically why: what is actively degrading, at what rate, and what the downstream structural consequences are. A rep who invents urgency without evidence is not applying loss aversion — they are fear-mongering, and customers recognize the difference.On the equity conversation specifically: this framing is particularly powerful for customers who are considering selling their home within the next few years. Foundation issues are one of the top five deal-killers in residential real estate transactions. A customer who understands that an undocumented foundation problem will either kill their sale or dramatically reduce their selling price has a concrete, financially quantifiable reason to act — regardless of whether they experience any structural consequences in the meantime.

#### Slide 6

PART  03

The Principles in Practice


The science behind why the Apex process works

Purpose: Every major step in the Apex process is grounded in a documented psychological principle. Understanding which principle each step activates helps reps execute with precision — and diagnose which principle was missed when an appointment doesn't close.

COMMITMENT & CONSISTENCY

SOCIAL PROOF
Buy-In Questions (Module 3)
Referral Book / How They Found You (Modules 1 & 5)
Each 'yes, that needs to be addressed' is a commitment anchor. By the time the proposal appears, reversing six prior commitments feels psychologically inconsistent.
People follow others' behavior in uncertain situations. A neighbor referral pre-establishes trust before the first word is spoken. Local job photos extend it.

AUTHORITY & EXPERTISE

THE IKEA EFFECT
Documentation, CAD Drawing, Education (Modules 2 & 3)
The Accomplish List / Co-Built Proposal (Module 3)
Systematic photography, measurement data, slide decks, and the CAD drawing all signal expertise — making every recommendation more persuasive.
People value things they helped create. The customer who identified problems and confirmed solutions experiences the proposal as their own — not something imposed.

SCARCITY & TIMING

LIKING & RAPPORT
Evidence-Based Urgency (Modules 4 & 5)
Warm-Up, Trust Unlock, Customer Walk-Around (Modules 1 & 2)
Damage accumulates, costs escalate, and repair conditions don't last. Genuine timing constraints — backed by inspection data — motivate action without manufactured pressure.
People buy from people they like. The warm-up, trust-unlocking moment, and customer-led walk-around deliberately build the relationship that makes everything else land.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  PART 03 — PRINCIPLES IN PRACTICE

### Notes:
PRINCIPLES IN PRACTICE — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide is the most conceptually dense in the module, and it is worth slowing down for in training. The goal is not for reps to memorize principle names — it is for them to understand that the Apex process is not a collection of arbitrary best practices. It is a system that was designed, consciously or not, to work with the grain of human psychology rather than against it. Every step has a reason. When reps understand the reason, they execute the step with more conviction.On Commitment and Consistency: this is Robert Cialdini's principle from Influence, and it is the most directly applicable to the buy-in question mechanic in Module 3. The psychological research is clear — once someone has publicly committed to a position, they experience social and internal pressure to remain consistent with it. The buy-in questions work because each one is a small public commitment. The rep who collects six of these has built a commitment ladder that makes reversal feel psychologically costly. This is why the buy-in questions must be spoken — a customer who thinks 'yes that needs fixing' is not making a commitment. A customer who says it out loud is.On the IKEA Effect: this is one of the most counterintuitive findings in behavioral economics. Named after the furniture company that famously has customers assemble their own furniture, the effect demonstrates that people assign significantly higher value to things they helped create — even when the objective quality is identical to a ready-made alternative. The accomplish list and co-built proposal harness this directly. The customer who points to a line item in the proposal and says 'yes, we agreed that needed to be done' is experiencing their own judgment being reflected back at them. They are not being asked to trust Apex's assessment — they are being invited to trust their own.On Scarcity and Timing: it is worth emphasizing the distinction between genuine urgency and manufactured urgency. Genuine urgency in foundation repair is abundant — moisture damage is progressive, seasonal conditions matter, and construction schedules fill up. Manufactured urgency — invented pricing deadlines, false scarcity of materials, fabricated seasonal offers — is both dishonest and detectable. Customers who feel manipulated by fake urgency don't just fail to close. They tell other people. The Apex standard is always that urgency must be something you can explain with reference to the specific customer's documentation.

#### Slide 7

PART  04

Reading Buyer Personalities


One process — four conversations

Purpose: The Apex process is consistent across every appointment. But how a rep communicates within that process should flex based on who they are talking to. The same information, delivered differently, lands very differently with different personality types.





THE ANALYTICAL

THE DRIVER
Signals: Asks technical questions, wants to see data, may have already researched the issue online. Takes time to decide. Skeptical of claims without evidence.
Signals: Decisive, fast-paced, may interrupt. Wants the bottom line quickly. Already has opinions about what needs to happen. Values efficiency and confidence.
Approach: Feed the logical brain. Slow down on documentation. Show the meter readings. Explain the measurement methodology. Don't rush to the close — this customer needs to feel they have all the information before they can say yes.
Approach: Match their energy. Be direct. Lead with the core problem and the bottom-line recommendation. Don't over-explain — get to the point, demonstrate confidence, and ask for the decision. They respect directness.
Avoid: Avoid generalizations, vague timelines, or claims you can't back with your inspection data.
Avoid: Avoid long educational sequences if they're clearly impatient. Give them the summary, offer to go deeper if they want it.





THE AMIABLE

THE EXPRESSIVE
Signals: Warm, relationship-focused, may take a long time to get to the point. Concerned about the impact on the family. Wants to feel good about the people they're working with.
Signals: Enthusiastic, big-picture thinker, may jump around in conversation. Responds to vision and story. Not detail-oriented. Wants to feel excited about the solution.
Approach: Invest in rapport before business. Ask about the family, the neighborhood, how long they've been in the home. Connect emotionally before presenting evidence. The close will come through trust, not through logic or urgency.
Approach: Tell the story of the repair — what the crawl space looks like today, what it will look like after. Use vivid language. Connect the repair to a bigger vision: 'your home protected for the next 30 years.' Keep energy high and close with enthusiasm.
Avoid: Avoid pressure tactics completely. Any hint of high-pressure will cause this customer to disengage. A gentle, confident ask will outperform any close technique.
Avoid: Avoid drowning them in technical detail. They will lose interest. Give them the vision, confirm the key facts, and ask for the decision while their energy is high.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  PART 04 — READING BUYER PERSONALITIES

### Notes:
READING BUYER PERSONALITIES — SPEAKER NOTESThe four personality types on this slide draw on well-established frameworks in behavioral sales training, most directly the DISC model and variations of it used in professional sales coaching. The specific labels and descriptions have been adapted for the foundation repair context, but the underlying behavioral patterns are consistent with decades of sales research.The most important point to make in training: the Apex process does not change based on personality type. What changes is the communication style the rep uses within the process. An Analytical customer and a Driver customer both get the photo review, the buy-in questions, the education, and the proposal. But the rep moves faster for the Driver, slows down and adds measurement detail for the Analytical, leads with emotional connection for the Amiable, and uses story and vision for the Expressive.On identification: reps can usually identify a customer's personality type within the first five to ten minutes of the warm-up — often sooner. The Analytical will be the one who has already looked at the issue online and wants to compare what the rep found against what they read. The Driver will already be talking about what they think needs to happen. The Amiable will be asking about the rep's family, offering coffee, and spending 15 minutes telling the story of the house before getting to the problem. The Expressive will be enthusiastic about having someone finally come look at this.On couples and mixed types: many appointments involve two decision-makers with different personality types. The rep who can identify both and modulate their communication accordingly — giving the Analytical their data while keeping the Expressive engaged with the story — will close at a dramatically higher rate than the rep who picks one style and sticks with it. The walk-around is an ideal time to calibrate, because both partners will typically reveal their type through how they describe the problem.

#### Slide 8

PART  04

Handling the Skeptic
The most common and most mishandled customer type
Skeptics are not hostile — they are self-protective. They have usually had a bad experience with a contractor, done their own research, or been burned by a high-pressure sales process before. They are not your enemy. They are your biggest opportunity.

How to Spot a Skeptic

What Actually Works

What to Say
▸  Arms crossed, guarded posture at the start
▸  Lead with the trust-unlocking moment — earlier and more deliberately than usual
"I get it —  there are a lot of companies that come out, find all kinds of problems, and give you a big number. My job is to give you an honest picture, not a big number."
▸  "We've already had two companies out"
▸  Acknowledge their skepticism directly: 'Most people I talk to have had at least one bad experience with a contractor'
▸  "We're just getting quotes"
"If I find things that don't need to be fixed,  I'll tell you that too. Not everything I document is going to require action."
▸  Let evidence do the work. Don't tell them what to think — show them what you measured and let them reach their own conclusions
▸  Challenges early claims or measurements
▸  Never argue. A rep who gets defensive with a skeptic confirms the skeptic's worst fears
▸  Asks pointed questions about pricing upfront
"I'd rather you leave today feeling well-informed  than feeling sold to. You can always call me back — I'm not going anywhere."
▸  Ask more questions and make fewer statements than you normally would
▸  Minimal small talk — wants to get to the point



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  PART 04 — HANDLING THE SKEPTIC

### Notes:
HANDLING THE SKEPTIC — SPEAKER NOTESThe skeptic is the personality type that most consistently derails less experienced reps — not because the skeptic is impossible to close, but because inexperienced reps make the same two mistakes with them every time. The first mistake is becoming defensive when the skeptic challenges a claim or a price point. The second is trying to overcome the skeptic's resistance with more persuasion. Both responses confirm exactly what the skeptic feared: that this rep has an agenda.The counter-intuitive truth about skeptics is that they are often the easiest customers to close for a rep who handles them correctly. Here is why: a skeptic who was given every reason to distrust contractors — and then met one who was genuinely transparent, evidence-driven, and undefensive — experiences a powerful contrast effect. That contrast is what the trust-unlocking moment was designed to create. For a skeptic, deliver it earlier, more slowly, and with more eye contact than you would for any other customer type.On acknowledging the skepticism directly: many reps are afraid to name the elephant in the room. In practice, acknowledging it is disarming. When a rep says 'I know most people have had at least one bad experience with a contractor before calling us,' the skeptic hears that the rep has seen this before, is not offended by it, and is comfortable enough to address it head-on. That comfort signals confidence — and skeptics respect confidence.On letting evidence do the work: the rep's job with a skeptic is to be a tour guide, not a salesperson. Show them what you found. Tell them what the measurements mean. Then ask what they see. When a skeptic draws their own conclusions from evidence you've laid out, those conclusions are far more persuasive than anything you could tell them. A skeptic who says 'that looks pretty bad' about a photo of their own crawl space has just sold themselves. The rep's job is now to confirm their conclusion and connect it to the solution.

#### Slide 9

PART  03

Psychology at the Close
Why some closes feel natural and others feel forced
The close that feels forced is always the result of unresolved uncertainty — in the customer's emotional brain or logical brain, or both. When the process has been run correctly, the close should feel like the natural conclusion of a conversation the customer has been part of.

The Anatomy of a Natural Close
Diagnosing a Forced Close




A close feels natural when: the customer fully understands the problem, they trust the rep's assessment, they believe the solution is correct for their situation, the price feels proportionate to what it protects, and they have been asking questions because they are already mentally planning the project. When all five of these conditions are true, the ask for the business is the least important moment in the appointment.
A close feels forced when one of the five conditions above is missing. If the customer doesn't fully trust the assessment, more evidence won't help — more rapport will. If the customer doesn't believe the solution is right, more pressure won't help — more education will. Identifying which condition is missing tells the rep exactly where to go in the process to recover.

The Anchoring Effect at the Proposal
The Psychology of Silence




The first number a customer hears anchors their perception of value. This is why building the proposal line by line — with each item connected to a prior agreement — produces significantly less resistance than presenting a lump sum. The customer who has watched the total grow incrementally, while agreeing to each component, experiences the final number as a sum they built rather than a price they were handed.
After asking for the business, the rep who remains silent gives the customer's decision-making brain the space it needs to arrive at a conclusion. The rep who immediately starts talking after asking has interrupted the customer's internal process. Silence after the ask is not awkward — it is the sound of the customer deciding. Train yourself to be comfortable with three to five full seconds of quiet.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  PART 03 — PSYCHOLOGY AT THE CLOSE

### Notes:
PSYCHOLOGY AT THE CLOSE — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide ties the psychology module back into the sales process in a practical and actionable way. The conceptual framework here — that a forced close is always the symptom of an unresolved condition, not a problem to be overcome with technique — is genuinely useful for reps who are trying to improve their close rate.On the anatomy of a natural close: the five conditions listed (understanding, trust, solution belief, price proportionality, and planning mentality) are a diagnostic checklist that a rep can run through in their head at any point during the appointment. If the customer is asking good questions about the repair process and timeline, condition five is met. If the customer says 'I just want to make sure we're doing the right thing,' condition three is likely not yet met. The rep who can identify which condition is missing can intervene at the right point rather than defaulting to a close technique that addresses the wrong problem.On the anchoring effect: this is one of the most well-documented findings in behavioral economics. The first number in any negotiation or pricing context serves as an anchor that all subsequent numbers are evaluated against. In the proposal context, this means that a customer who has watched the total accumulate line by line — each item confirmed before the next is added — experiences the final number as the sum of things they agreed to, not as a price being presented to them. The psychological difference between these two experiences is significant and measurable in close rates.On the psychology of silence: this is a skill that is genuinely difficult to train and genuinely worth training. The discomfort of silence after asking for a decision is one of the most universal human experiences in sales. And the instinct to fill that silence with more talking is almost universally counterproductive. When a rep asks for the business and then immediately begins explaining things or softening the request, they signal uncertainty — which the customer interprets as a sign that the rep themselves is not confident in the proposal. Silence, by contrast, signals the confidence that the proposal stands on its own. Practice it deliberately.

#### Slide 10

Psychology Across the Full Process
Where each principle activates in the Apex curriculum
Every module in the Apex curriculum is doing psychological work — whether or not the rep is conscious of it. This reference connects the principles from Module 6 to the specific moments where they are being applied.

Module 1 — Pre-Inspection
Authority & Liking
The trust-unlocking moment, the structured agenda, and financing seeding all signal expertise and disarm skepticism before the rep is in the room.

Module 2 — The Inspection
Authority & Social Proof
Systematic photography, in-person documentation, and the door knock physically demonstrate competence. Evidence quality directly impacts credibility.

Module 3 — The Presentation
Commitment & the IKEA Effect
Buy-in questions build a commitment ladder. The accomplish list harnesses co-creation. Each yes is psychological progress toward the close.

Module 3 — Education
Authority & Loss Aversion
The education sequence positions the rep as a trusted expert while establishing the real cost of wrong fixes and inaction.

Module 4 — The Close
Anchoring & Consistency
The line-by-line proposal build uses anchoring. Re-anchoring to prior agreements after objections applies consistency. The CAD drawing uses authority.

Module 5 — Follow-Up
Liking, Scarcity & Reciprocity
Consistent follow-up builds relationship equity. Value-add touches create reciprocity. The named follow-up demonstrates integrity, which compounds trust.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  PSYCHOLOGY ACROSS THE PROCESS

### Notes:
PSYCHOLOGY ACROSS THE PROCESS — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide serves as the integration point for the entire module — and in some ways for the entire curriculum. Use it to help reps see that Modules 1 through 5 were not just process steps. They were a carefully sequenced application of psychological principles that compound on each other.The rep who arrives at a close having run the process correctly has, by that point, activated authority through systematic documentation, built commitment through six or seven buy-in agreements, created an IKEA effect through the co-built proposal, established liking through the warm-up and walk-around, and grounded urgency in evidence-based loss framing. The close is not a single moment of persuasion — it is the final step of a psychological process that has been running for two hours.A useful training exercise with this slide: ask reps to identify which psychological principle they most consistently under-apply in their appointments. For many reps, the answer will be commitment — they skip or rush the buy-in questions. For others it will be the IKEA effect — they write the proposal themselves rather than building it with the customer. For others it will be silence at the close. Whatever the answer, that represents the highest-leverage single improvement available to that rep.One final point worth making: all of these principles are being applied in the customer's genuine interest. A homeowner who has a real foundation problem is better served by a rep who helps them understand it fully, connects to their emotional stake, and builds a proposal that reflects their actual situation than by a rep who rushes through a 30-minute visit and hands them a number. The psychology makes the process more effective. But it also makes the outcome better for the customer. That alignment — between what is psychologically effective and what genuinely serves the customer — is what makes the Apex process worth executing with full conviction.

Psychology Checklist
Apply these principles on every appointment

Understanding why the process works is what turns a rep who follows instructions into a rep who adapts intelligently.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  MODULE 6 — PSYCHOLOGY CHECKLIST

### Notes:
PSYCHOLOGY CHECKLIST — SPEAKER NOTESThis checklist is different from the ones in Modules 1 through 5. Those checklists tracked whether the rep completed specific actions. This one tracks whether the rep was applying conscious psychological awareness throughout the appointment. It is a self-audit for the quality of execution, not just the completion of steps.The most valuable use of this checklist is as a post-appointment reflection tool. After a non-closing appointment, a rep should work through the list and identify which items were not achieved. This often reveals something more specific than 'the customer wasn't ready' or 'the price was too high.' A rep who realizes they never identified the customer's primary emotional driver, or who acknowledged that they rushed the buy-in questions because the appointment was running long, has identified a specific and correctable cause for the outcome.The hardest item on the entire list for most reps: 'Used silence after the close ask — did not fill it with talking.' This item is hard not because reps don't know they should do it, but because the discomfort of silence in a high-stakes moment is a very strong impulse to override. It requires deliberate practice — literally sitting in a chair, asking a question, and not speaking for five seconds. Have reps practice it in training pairs. The first time someone experiences five seconds of genuine silence after making a close ask, they understand why it works.A good closing conversation for this module: ask reps which psychological principle they think has the most potential to improve their close rate if they applied it more deliberately. Then have them commit to one specific behavioral change for the next 30 days. Psychological awareness without behavioral change is just interesting information. Behavioral change is what moves the number.

People don't
buy with logic.
They buy with
emotion —
and justify
with logic.



The rep who understands this serves customers better, closes more consistently, and adapts intelligently when the standard approach needs to flex.
