#### Slide 1

JOB TYPE TRAINING


CRAWL SPACE
Drainage

Bulk Water vs. Vapor  ·  Perimeter Drain  ·  Sump Pump  ·  Relationship to Encapsulation
FLORIDA PANHANDLE  ·  SOUTH ALABAMA

Internal Training Use Only

### Notes:
COVER SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESCrawl space drainage sits at the intersection of two different problems that customers often conflate: water vapor coming up from the soil, and bulk liquid water physically entering the crawl space. The encapsulation deck addressed the vapor problem. This deck addresses the liquid water problem — and teaches reps the critical distinction between the two, because treating one without understanding the other produces systems that appear complete but fail to address the actual source of moisture.The most important concept for reps to internalize before any drainage appointment: a sump pump and perimeter drain manage water that has already entered the crawl space. They do not prevent water from entering. Encapsulation manages vapor. Drainage manages bulk water. When both problems exist — which is common in the Gulf South — both solutions are required, and neither alone is sufficient.This deck follows the same structure as the other job type decks: problem definition, causes, wrong approaches, the correct system explained component by component, technical specs, and field communication guidance.

#### Slide 2

Vapor vs. Bulk Water — The Critical Distinction
Understanding which problem exists determines which solution is correct

The single most important diagnostic skill in crawl space work is distinguishing between a vapor problem and a bulk water problem. They look similar on the surface — both produce moisture in the crawl space — but they have different causes, different solutions, and different consequences if the wrong solution is applied.

VAPOR  (Ground Moisture / Humidity)

BULK WATER  (Active Liquid Intrusion)
▸  Source: Water evaporating from exposed soil and outdoor humid air entering through vents and gaps.
▸  Source: Groundwater rising with the water table, surface water flowing toward the foundation, or drainage failure directing water toward the crawl space.
▸  Presentation: High relative humidity readings, condensation on surfaces, elevated wood moisture content, musty odor. No standing water visible.
▸  Presentation: Standing water visible on the crawl space floor, water staining on foundation walls or piers, silt or mineral deposits at wall bases, efflorescence on block walls.
▸  Solution: Encapsulation — vapor barrier, sealed vents, dehumidifier. These address vapor migration at the source.
▸  Solution: Interior perimeter drain system and sump pump — these capture water that enters and remove it before it causes damage.
▸  Sump pump alone: Has zero effect on vapor. A customer who installs a sump pump for a vapor problem will still have elevated humidity, mold, and wood moisture issues.
▸  Encapsulation alone: Cannot stop bulk water entry. A liner installed without drainage in a wet crawl space traps water beneath the liner, creating a hidden pond that accelerates wood and soil damage.
▸  Dehumidifier alone: Can reduce humidity but at enormous operating cost if vapor entry points are not sealed. Analogous to running AC with the windows open.
▸  When both exist: Drainage must be installed first, then encapsulation. Installing the liner before drainage is a sequencing error that produces failure.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  VAPOR VS. BULK WATER — THE CRITICAL DISTINCTION

### Notes:
VAPOR VS. BULK WATER — SPEAKER NOTESThis is the most conceptually important slide in the deck, and the one that most clearly separates a technically fluent rep from one who is guessing. The distinction between vapor and bulk water is not always obvious from the surface — both produce moisture in the crawl space, both cause wood damage and mold, and both present similarly to a homeowner who is not trained to look for the diagnostic differences.The diagnostic approach: when doing the inspection, look specifically for water staining on foundation walls and piers, silt or mineral deposits at the base of walls (evidence that water has been flowing in and evaporating), and efflorescence — the white chalky mineral deposits that appear on block or concrete walls when water has been carrying dissolved minerals through the wall and depositing them on the surface as it evaporates. These are signs of bulk water intrusion, not vapor. Vapor does not produce staining, silt, or efflorescence.On the sequencing point for combined problems: this is a critical installation sequencing principle that reps must understand. When a customer needs both drainage and encapsulation, the drainage must go in first. If the liner is installed before drainage in a wet crawl space, water that enters after installation has nowhere to go — it pools beneath the liner, the liner floats, and the system fails. The correct sequence is: drainage installed and functional, then liner installed and tucked into the sump lid or drain channel edge, then dehumidifier installed. This sequence ensures that any water that enters after the liner is in place is captured by the drain channel beneath the liner and routed to the sump.Practical advice for the inspection: when you see water staining or evidence of bulk water entry, spend extra time investigating the source. Is it groundwater rising from below? Surface water draining toward the foundation? A downspout discharging too close to the foundation wall? Identifying the source helps you frame both the drainage recommendation and the recommendation for addressing the upstream cause.

#### Slide 3

THE  PROBLEM

Why Crawl Spaces Get Wet


Bulk water sources — what the inspection should look for

Purpose: Customers who have standing water in their crawl space know they have a problem, but almost never know why. Identifying the source — not just the symptom — is what separates an Apex assessment from a contractor who just shows up and quotes a drain.

High Water Table —

01
In coastal and low-lying areas of the Panhandle, the water table can rise seasonally to within a few feet of the surface. During heavy rains or prolonged wet periods, groundwater rises through the soil and enters the crawl space from below — often appearing to bubble up through the dirt floor. This source cannot be prevented externally; it can only be managed internally with drainage and a sump pump.

Poor Surface Grading —

02
Soil that slopes toward the house directs roof runoff and surface water toward the foundation wall. Water that pools against the foundation eventually infiltrates the block or concrete and enters the crawl space through the wall or at the footing-wall joint. This is one of the most common and most correctable bulk water sources — regrading the soil or adding exterior drainage away from the foundation often reduces intrusion significantly.

Gutter and Downspout Failure —

03
Gutters that are blocked, overflowing, or that discharge directly against the foundation wall are a primary cause of localized, wall-seam water intrusion. A home that handles its roof runoff poorly effectively concentrates the rainfall from the entire roof surface against the foundation at a few discharge points. Downspout extension or underground routing is a critical part of the exterior drainage picture for these homes.

Footing Drain Failure —

04
Most homes built in the past 40 years have an exterior footing drain — a perforated pipe installed around the foundation perimeter at the time of construction. Over time, these pipes clog with silt, roots, and mineral deposits and lose their capacity to manage groundwater. When the exterior footing drain fails, groundwater that was previously managed begins entering the crawl space, often producing water intrusion patterns that appear suddenly after years of a dry crawl space.

Structural Cracks in Foundation Walls —

05
Settlement cracks, construction cold joints, and deteriorating mortar joints in block foundations all create pathways for water to enter under hydrostatic pressure. These entry points become more severe over time as water further erodes the surrounding material. Sealing cracks alone does not solve the water entry problem — it merely redirects it to the next weakest point unless the hydrostatic pressure is also relieved.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  THE PROBLEM — WHY CRAWL SPACES GET WET

### Notes:
WHY CRAWL SPACES GET WET — SPEAKER NOTESThe most common question after this slide is: 'Can we fix the source from the outside instead of installing drainage inside?' The honest answer is: sometimes yes, always partially, and almost never completely in the Gulf South.External corrective measures — regrading soil, extending downspouts, repairing gutters, adding exterior French drains — reduce the volume of water entering the crawl space and are almost always worth recommending. Reps should document and recommend exterior corrections as part of every drainage proposal because they reduce the workload on the interior drainage system and extend its effective life. But exterior measures alone are rarely sufficient in this region because of the high water table. When the water table rises, it enters from below regardless of what is happening at the surface.On footing drain failure: this is a source that customers are almost never aware of. When a customer says 'it was dry for 20 years and then suddenly started getting wet,' the most likely explanation is that the exterior footing drain has finally failed. The drain was managing the groundwater successfully until it clogged, and now it isn't. The customer experiences this as a sudden new problem, but the underlying condition — high groundwater near the foundation — has always been present. This framing is useful because it explains why the problem appeared when it did without implying that something was done wrong or that the home has a fundamental flaw.On hydrostatic pressure and crack sealing: this is an important point to make clearly when customers ask about just sealing the cracks. Hydraulic cement and crack sealants can stop water at one entry point, but if the hydrostatic pressure around the foundation continues, the water will find the next weakest point. The interior drainage system relieves the pressure by giving the water somewhere to go — which is why it is more effective than crack sealing alone as a long-term solution to hydrostatic water entry.

#### Slide 4

WRONG  FIXES

What Doesn't Solve It


Three inadequate approaches reps will encounter in the field

Purpose: Customers will frequently arrive at the appointment having already tried something that didn't work, or considering a cheaper alternative. Reps who can explain specifically why each approach falls short give customers the evidence to make a confident decision.

CRACK SEALING AND SURFACE WATERPROOFING
Why customers choose it:  Visible cracks in the foundation wall seem like the obvious entry point. Hydraulic cement, epoxy injection, and waterproofing paints are readily available and significantly cheaper than drainage systems.  Why it doesn't solve it:  Sealing a crack stops water at that specific point but does not relieve the hydrostatic pressure pushing against the entire foundation wall. The water redirects to the next weakest point — another crack, a mortar joint, the footing-wall joint — and re-enters. This is why homes that have been 'waterproofed' with coatings repeatedly still get wet: the pressure source was never addressed. Interior drainage relieves the pressure by giving the water a managed pathway inside.

SUMP PUMP WITHOUT A PERIMETER DRAIN
Why customers choose it:  A sump pump is cheaper and simpler than a full drainage system. Homeowners who want to manage standing water often start with a standalone pump, hoping it will be sufficient.  Why it doesn't solve it:  A sump pump at a single location can only remove water that reaches the pump. In a large or irregularly shaped crawl space, water that enters at the perimeter wall on the far side may never reach the sump unless there is a perimeter channel directing it there. The result: the pump runs continuously near its location while the far corners remain wet. The perimeter drain is what routes water from all entry points to the sump — the pump is only the last step in the system, not the system itself.

ENCAPSULATION WITHOUT DRAINAGE IN A WET CRAWL SPACE
Why customers choose it:  A homeowner or contractor focuses on the vapor/mold problem and installs encapsulation without evaluating whether bulk water is also entering. The liner appears to solve everything at once.  Why it doesn't solve it:  If bulk water continues to enter after the liner is installed, it pools beneath the liner with nowhere to go. The liner may float or buckle. Water trapped beneath the liner stays in contact with the soil and foundation constantly — producing conditions worse than an unsealed crawl space in some cases. The correct sequence is always: drainage first, encapsulation second. The liner is designed to be tucked into the drain channel, so water entering the wall flows beneath the liner to the drain rather than pooling on top of or beneath the system.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  WRONG APPROACHES — WHAT DOESN'T SOLVE IT

### Notes:
WRONG APPROACHES — SPEAKER NOTESAll three wrong approaches on this slide involve either treating a symptom without addressing the cause, or addressing one part of the system without the other parts that make it functional. Reps who understand these failure modes are equipped to have honest, credible conversations with customers who have already spent money on one of these approaches and are frustrated that the problem persists.On crack sealing: the key concept is hydrostatic pressure. Water under hydrostatic pressure will find a path through the foundation as long as the pressure exists. Sealing one path does not eliminate the pressure — it redirects it. The only way to manage water entry in a foundation under hydrostatic pressure is to give the water a managed path inside (interior drainage) or to actively relieve the pressure outside (exterior French drain, sump, or improved site grading). Crack sealing is useful as a secondary measure after the pressure is managed, but not as a primary solution.On the standalone sump pump: be careful how you frame this with customers who already have a sump pump. The pump may be working correctly — it's just not receiving water from the far corners of the crawl space because there's no drain channel routing it there. Frame it as: 'Your sump pump is doing what it's supposed to do — it's managing water that reaches it. What we need is a perimeter channel that collects water from all the entry points along the walls and routes it to the pump. Right now the pump is only serving the area immediately around it.'On the encapsulation sequencing error: this is the most consequential wrong approach because it produces failure in what should be a complete waterproofing system. If a customer has had encapsulation installed and is still experiencing water issues, this is almost certainly what happened — the liner was installed in a crawl space with active bulk water entry, and now the liner is trapping or channeling water in ways the homeowner can't see. Always inspect for evidence of bulk water entry before recommending or installing encapsulation, and always install drainage first when both problems exist.

#### Slide 5

RIGHT  REPAIR

The Complete Drainage System


How the components work together — and why each one is necessary

Purpose: Like encapsulation, a drainage system is not a single product — it is a collection of components that must work together. A rep who can explain each component's role builds credibility and makes the proposal defensible when customers ask why everything is necessary.

01
Perimeter Drain Channel

02
Gravel Bed and Filter Fabric




A drainage pipe or channel is installed along the interior perimeter of the crawl space at the base of the foundation walls. It captures water seeping through the wall at the footing-wall joint — the most common entry point. The channel is sloped to direct all collected water toward the sump basin. Without this, water entering at the perimeter has no managed path to the pump.
The drain channel is surrounded by washed gravel, which provides a clear drainage path and prevents fine soil particles from clogging the pipe. Filter fabric wraps the assembly to further prevent silt intrusion. Over time, an unprotected drain pipe in clay-bearing soil will clog — the gravel and fabric are the components that give the system longevity.

03
Sump Basin

04
Primary Sump Pump




A perforated plastic basin is excavated at the lowest point of the crawl space to receive water from the perimeter drain. The basin's perforations allow groundwater rising from below to also enter the system. The basin is sized based on the expected water volume and the pump's capacity. An undersized basin produces continuous pump cycling, which shortens pump life.
A submersible pump installed inside the basin activates automatically via a float switch when water reaches a set level. It discharges through a pipe routed through the foundation wall or crawl space floor to daylight, well away from the foundation. The discharge endpoint matters — water discharged too close to the foundation simply re-enters the system.

05
Battery Backup Pump

06
Integration with Encapsulation




A battery-powered secondary pump provides protection during power outages — exactly when it is most needed, since power outages frequently accompany the storms that produce the most water intrusion. A primary pump without battery backup is a significant vulnerability in storm-prone areas like the Florida Panhandle. Battery backup is not optional — it is essential.
When encapsulation is part of the scope, the vapor barrier is installed after drainage and is tucked into or lapped over the drain channel at the wall. This ensures that water entering the wall is directed beneath the liner and into the drain, rather than being trapped between the liner and the wall. The two systems together — drainage and encapsulation — address both bulk water and vapor comprehensively.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  THE COMPLETE DRAINAGE SYSTEM

### Notes:
THE COMPLETE DRAINAGE SYSTEM — SPEAKER NOTESThe framing to use with customers: 'Think of this system like a controlled drain for your crawl space. Right now, water enters wherever it can and accumulates wherever it ends up. The drainage system gives that water a single, managed path — it enters the channel, flows to the basin, and the pump removes it automatically. You go from an uncontrolled wet environment to one where water is actively managed 24 hours a day.'On the perimeter drain specifically: the most important installation detail reps should understand is the slope. A drain channel that is perfectly level will not drain — water must flow by gravity to the sump basin, which requires a slight negative slope along the entire channel. Installation crews who cut corners on slope produce systems that trap water in low spots and eventually become ineffective as silt accumulates. Reps should know to ask whether the installation crew verifies slope during installation.On the battery backup: this is the component that customers most often try to remove from the scope to reduce cost. It is also the component that most often makes the difference between a system that worked and a system that failed. The most common scenario for crawl space flooding is a major storm that produces heavy rain and also knocks out power. The primary pump cannot run without power. The battery backup pump runs on stored battery power and keeps the system functioning during exactly the conditions that produce the most water. Frame it clearly: 'You can install the system without battery backup. But the situations where you'll need the pump most — major storms — are exactly when you're most likely to lose power. I'd rather you have it and not need it.'On the discharge endpoint: this detail is often overlooked and is one of the most common drainage system failure modes. Water discharged within a few feet of the foundation, or onto soil that slopes back toward the house, simply re-enters the drainage system in a continuous loop. The discharge pipe must terminate at grade, well away from the foundation, and on a path that routes water away from the structure permanently. Reps should ask about the discharge plan during the inspection and verify it during the installation review.

#### Slide 6

WHAT TO  EXPECT

What the Customer Should Expect


Installation process, results timeline, and maintenance requirements

Purpose: Drainage systems require ongoing attention — unlike encapsulation, they have mechanical components that must be maintained. Customers who understand this before the job are more likely to maintain the system and to call Apex when service is needed.

Installation Process

Results and Ongoing Maintenance
▸  Duration: Typically 1 to 2 days for a complete system in an average crawl space. Combined drainage and encapsulation jobs run 2 to 3 days.
▸  Immediate: Standing water is eliminated as soon as the system becomes operational. The crawl space will be visibly dryer within the first rain event.
▸  Excavation: A trench is dug along the interior perimeter of the crawl space walls and a pit is excavated for the sump basin. Soil is removed and disposed of.
▸  Weeks 1–4: As bulk water is managed, humidity levels in the crawl space begin to drop — especially if encapsulation is also part of the scope.
▸  Access: Crew will need unobstructed access to the crawl space hatch and may need to remove storage items from the perimeter.
▸  Annual maintenance: The pump should be tested annually by pouring water into the basin and verifying activation. Check the discharge line for obstructions or vegetation growth.
▸  Electrical: The sump pump requires a dedicated electrical circuit. If no circuit is available at the sump location, electrical work may be needed before or during installation.
▸  Battery backup: Battery backup units typically require battery replacement every 3 to 5 years. Test annually, replace on schedule — do not wait for failure.
▸  Discharge routing: The discharge pipe will exit through the foundation wall or floor. The exit point and above-ground routing will be visible — discuss this with the customer before work begins.
▸  Drainage channel: Clean-out ports in the perimeter channel allow flushing of sediment every 3 to 5 years. Document their location for the customer during the final walkthrough.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  WHAT TO EXPECT — INSTALLATION & MAINTENANCE

### Notes:
WHAT TO EXPECT — SPEAKER NOTESThe ongoing maintenance conversation is one that reps often skip because it feels like it might reduce the attractiveness of the proposal. The opposite is true. A customer who is told exactly what the system requires to maintain its effectiveness, and who then experiences the system performing as described, becomes a loyal customer who calls Apex for annual service and who refers others with the same problem.On the electrical requirement: this is one of the most common surprises that derails installation schedules. Many crawl spaces do not have a dedicated electrical outlet anywhere near the planned sump location. If the existing outlet is on a shared circuit with other equipment, the pump's current draw can trip the breaker during high-demand operation. Reps should ask about electrical access during the inspection and flag this for the operations team so that the need for electrical work can be addressed before the installation date.On the discharge routing: customers sometimes have strong opinions about where the discharge pipe exits the foundation and how the pipe runs above grade. Getting ahead of this conversation during the sales process — 'I want to walk through where the discharge line will exit and run so you're not surprised on installation day' — prevents friction during the job. The above-grade portion of the discharge pipe will be visible, and some customers want it buried or routed differently than the most practical path. Understanding this preference before the crew arrives avoids conflict.On the maintenance conversation: present it as the normal standard of care for any mechanical system, not as an asterisk on the warranty. 'Like any mechanical system, the pump needs periodic testing and the battery backup needs periodic replacement — about every 3 to 5 years. We can set you up on a service schedule if that's helpful.' This opens the door to an ongoing service relationship that is valuable for both Apex and the customer.

#### Slide 7

TECH  SPECS

Technical Specifications


Component specs and key thresholds every rep needs before a drainage appointment
Drainage appointments attract customers who have researched their options and customers who have had systems fail. Both need reps who know the specifications.

Perimeter Drain System

Sump Pump System

Diagnostic Thresholds
Channel type: Perforated drain pipe or proprietary drain tile
Basin depth: Minimum 18" below crawl space floor; 24"+ preferred
Bulk water indicators: Water staining, silt deposits, efflorescence on walls, standing water
Diameter (pipe): Typically 4" perforated PVC or equivalent
Basin diameter: 18"–24" depending on expected flow rate
Vapor indicators: High RH (>60%), elevated wood MC (>19%), no staining or standing water
Bed material: Washed gravel, typically 1/2"–3/4" clean stone
Primary pump type: Submersible, float-activated, 1/3 to 1/2 HP typical
Both indicators: Drainage first, encapsulation second — never reversed
Filter wrap: Non-woven geotextile filter fabric to prevent silt entry
Pump capacity: 1,500–3,000+ GPH depending on water table and drainage area
Discharge endpoint: Minimum 6 feet from foundation; must slope away from home
Slope requirement: Minimum 1/8" per foot toward sump (1/4" preferred)
Activation: Float switch — activates at set water level, deactivates after removal
Pump testing: Annual — pour water into basin, verify float activation
Clean-out ports: Installed every 10–20 feet for flushing access
Discharge pipe: 1.5"–2" PVC, routed to daylight well away from foundation
Battery replacement: Every 3–5 years regardless of apparent condition
Wall connection: Channel positioned at footing-wall joint (primary entry point)
Check valve: Required on discharge line to prevent backflow
Channel flushing: Every 3–5 years via clean-out ports
Integration: Liner tucked into/lapped over channel when encapsulation is included
Battery backup: 12V deep-cycle marine battery; replace every 3–5 years; test annually
Electrical requirement: Dedicated 15A or 20A GFCI-protected circuit



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  TECHNICAL SPECIFICATIONS

### Notes:
TECHNICAL SPECIFICATIONS — SPEAKER NOTESThe technical questions drainage customers ask most frequently:'Why does the slope of the drain channel matter?' Water does not flow uphill. A drain channel that is perfectly flat, or that has low spots, will trap water rather than routing it to the sump. That trapped water becomes stagnant, accumulates sediment faster, and eventually the channel loses drainage capacity entirely. A minimum slope of 1/8" per foot — about 1" of drop for every 8 feet of channel length — ensures consistent flow toward the basin.'How big does the pump need to be?' Pump capacity is determined by the drainage area and the expected rate of water entry — which depends on the water table depth and the permeability of the surrounding soil. A pump that is too small for the drainage area will cycle continuously during heavy rain events and eventually fail from overwork. Reps should know that Apex's operations team sizes the pump based on the specific inspection findings, not a one-size formula.'Why do I need a battery backup? My pump is plugged in.' The most common scenario for crawl space flooding is a major storm. Major storms frequently cause power outages. A primary pump without a battery backup cannot operate during a power outage — which is precisely when it is most needed. The battery backup pump activates automatically when the primary pump cannot run. Without it, the customer is relying on luck that the power stays on during the worst storms. In the Florida Panhandle, that is not a reasonable risk to accept.'Where does the water go after the pump removes it?' The discharge pipe routes water through the foundation wall or floor and discharges it at grade, well away from the home. The discharge endpoint must be on a path that slopes away from the foundation — discharging water within a few feet of the house, or onto soil that drains back toward the house, effectively recycles the water through the system. The rep should identify the discharge plan during the inspection and confirm it routes water permanently away from the structure.

#### Slide 8

IN  THE  FIELD

How to Talk About Crawl Space Drainage


Connecting the system to what the customer actually cares about

Purpose: Drainage appointments often involve customers who are frustrated — they've had water in their crawl space for years, tried things that didn't work, and are skeptical. Reps who lead with acknowledgment and evidence earn trust faster than those who open with a pitch.

In the field: "What I documented in your crawl space today is not just a nuisance — it's active damage. Standing water is the most aggressive moisture condition a crawl space can have. The wood members I photographed are already showing elevated moisture content, and the mold I documented on the sill plates means that air from this space is entering your living area above it every day. The longer this goes without a managed drainage system, the more expensive the secondary repairs become."

LOSS AVERSION — STRUCTURAL & HEALTH CONSEQUENCES
Technical: Standing water in a crawl space accelerates wood rot, creates persistent mold conditions that affect indoor air quality, and undermines the soil support beneath piers and beams. Consequences compound over time.

In the field: "What you tried before wasn't wrong — it just wasn't a complete system. Sealing a crack addresses one entry point but doesn't relieve the pressure that causes water to find the next one. Installing a pump without a perimeter channel means the pump can only manage water that physically reaches it. What we're proposing is a system that captures water from every entry point along the perimeter and routes all of it to a single managed exit. That's the difference between plugging individual holes and actually managing the water."

VALIDATION — FOR CUSTOMERS WHO TRIED BEFORE
Technical: Previous failed approaches (crack sealing, standalone pump, inadequate encapsulation) are extremely common and represent incomplete systems rather than fraudulent work. Understanding why they failed explains why a complete system is now necessary.

In the field: "Once this system is in, it works automatically — you don't have to think about it. The pump activates the moment water reaches the float, and the battery backup means it keeps running even if the power goes out during a storm. A lot of our customers tell us that the biggest benefit was just being able to sleep during a heavy rain without worrying about what was happening under their house. That peace of mind is real, and it starts the first night after installation."

PEACE OF MIND — FOR THE STORM-SEASON CUSTOMER
Technical: Battery backup provides system continuity during power outages. Automatic float-switch operation means the system requires no human activation during storms. The customer does not need to be home or awake for the system to protect the crawl space.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  IN THE FIELD — CONNECTING TECHNICAL TO SALES

### Notes:
HOW TO TALK ABOUT DRAINAGE — SPEAKER NOTESThe validation framing on this slide is uniquely important for drainage appointments because a disproportionate number of drainage customers arrive having already tried something that didn't work. They are frustrated and skeptical. A rep who treats that frustration as an obstacle is making the job harder. A rep who treats it as an opportunity to demonstrate empathy and expertise is making the job significantly easier.The validation framing works because it is accurate. Crack sealing, standalone pumps, and partial encapsulation without drainage are all incomplete solutions — they are not fraudulent or incompetent, they just don't form a complete system. When a rep can explain specifically why each previous attempt was insufficient — not as a criticism of whoever did the work, but as an explanation of what a complete system requires — the customer experiences the rep as the most knowledgeable person who has walked into their home on this issue. That experience closes jobs.On the peace of mind framing: this is the most emotionally resonant closing argument for customers in hurricane and storm-prone areas. The Florida Panhandle has the full range of storm conditions — hurricane season from June through November, thunderstorms throughout the year, and the occasional major rainfall event that produces flooding even without a named storm. A customer who has woken up during a storm and wondered whether their crawl space was flooding will immediately understand the value of an automatic system with battery backup. The selling point is not the technology — it is the freedom from anxiety that the technology provides.A note on the frustration these customers often bring: some drainage customers have been dealing with water in their crawl space for years. Some have spent significant money on solutions that didn't work. Some have been told the problem isn't that serious by previous contractors who didn't have a complete solution. These customers can be guarded or even hostile at the opening of the appointment. The single most effective thing a rep can do with these customers is to listen to their story before presenting anything — let them describe what they've experienced, what they've tried, and what they've been told. That listening alone differentiates the appointment from every previous one they've had.

#### Slide 9

Common Customer Questions
The questions every rep will face in a drainage appointment

"I already have a sump pump. Why isn't it working?"
"Can't I just slope the yard away from the house?"
Validate first: 'Your pump may be working perfectly — it's only removing water that physically reaches it. Without a perimeter channel routing water from every entry point along the walls, the pump only serves the area immediately around it. The wet corners on the far side of your crawl space are wet because they have no path to the pump.' Then walk through the system concept.
Honestly: 'Regrading the soil is a great first step and I'd recommend it regardless of what else we do — it reduces the volume of water hitting your foundation. But in this area, the water table rises seasonally regardless of surface grading. When the water table comes up, it comes through the soil from below, and no amount of regrading addresses that. The interior drainage manages the water once it's in — exterior measures reduce how much gets in.',

"Do I need both drainage AND encapsulation?"
"What if the power goes out during a storm?"
Explain based on what the inspection found: 'If I documented standing water or staining, you need drainage — encapsulation alone won't stop bulk water. If I also documented high humidity and elevated wood moisture, you need encapsulation too. The two systems solve different problems. Drainage removes water that enters as a liquid. Encapsulation controls vapor. In your case, you have both problems.' Then explain the installation sequence.
This is exactly why battery backup is included in the proposal: 'The primary pump runs on your home's electricity. During a storm — exactly when you need it most — you're likely to lose power. The battery backup pump activates automatically when the primary pump can't run and keeps the system working through a power outage. It's not optional for this region.',

"How often will I have to deal with maintenance?"
"What's the difference between this and what [Competitor] quoted?"
Be specific: 'Once a year, pour a bucket of water into the basin and make sure the pump activates — that's the main thing. Every 3 to 5 years, replace the battery backup battery and flush the drain channel through the clean-out ports. If you want, we can set you up on a service schedule so you don't have to remember it.' This frames maintenance as manageable and opens a service relationship.
Ask what their scope included: 'Did they include a perimeter channel or just a sump pump? Did they include battery backup? What drain pipe type and gravel bed? A proposal without a perimeter channel is a pump without a collection system — it will only work near its location. Let me walk you through exactly what our scope includes so you can compare the two.' Compare on components, not on price.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  COMMON CUSTOMER QUESTIONS

### Notes:
COMMON QUESTIONS — SPEAKER NOTESThe 'I already have a sump pump' question is the single most common opening in drainage appointments, and it is a diagnostic opportunity dressed up as an objection. A rep who hears 'I already have a pump' and thinks 'this is going to be a hard sell' is misreading the situation. A rep who hears it and thinks 'now I need to understand what the existing pump is connected to and why it's not solving the problem' is in exactly the right frame.The answer almost always comes down to the absence of a perimeter channel. The existing pump is functioning — it's just not receiving water from the areas of the crawl space that are wet because there's no drain system routing that water to the pump's location. Once the customer understands this, the proposal shifts from 'why do I need another pump?' to 'oh, I need the system that connects all the wet areas to the pump I already have.' That reframe often closes the job.On the encapsulation question: always answer this one based specifically on what the inspection documented. If the inspection found standing water and staining but not elevated humidity or wood moisture, the customer may need drainage only. If the inspection found both bulk water and vapor indicators, both systems are warranted. Never make a recommendation that isn't supported by the inspection findings — that's how Apex maintains its professional credibility and avoids recommending unnecessary work.On the competitor comparison: the competitive differentiation in drainage almost always comes down to three elements — the presence or absence of a perimeter channel, battery backup, and drain pipe quality and gravel bed. A proposal that includes only a sump pump with no perimeter channel is not equivalent to a full drainage system, regardless of the price difference. Walk the customer through the scope difference component by component and let them draw their own conclusion.

Crawl Space Drainage — Rep Knowledge Checklist
What every Apex rep must know before any drainage appointment

DIAGNOSTIC KNOWLEDGE

The rep who can explain why the previous solution failed — specifically — earns the trust that converts the skeptical customer.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  REP KNOWLEDGE CHECKLIST

### Notes:
KNOWLEDGE CHECKLIST — SPEAKER NOTESThe most consequential item on this entire checklist is the first one in the Diagnostic Knowledge column: 'Can distinguish vapor indicators from bulk water indicators.' A rep who cannot make this distinction will either propose encapsulation in a crawl space that needs drainage (producing a failed installation when the liner floods), or propose drainage in a crawl space that only has a vapor problem (producing an installed system that addresses the wrong problem at significant unnecessary cost). Both outcomes damage Apex's reputation and the customer relationship.The coaching approach for this checklist: have the rep describe the inspection findings from their most recent crawl space appointment and identify whether the problem was vapor, bulk water, or both. If they can't describe the specific diagnostic indicators that led to their conclusion, they need more time in the field with an experienced rep before running drainage appointments independently.The validation framing item — 'asks about previous failed attempts and can explain why they failed' — is worth specific practice in training because it requires the rep to be both empathetic and technically credible simultaneously. Have reps role-play the response to 'I've already had this done twice and it keeps coming back.' The correct response acknowledges the frustration, validates that the previous work may have been done correctly, and then explains specifically what was incomplete about each approach and what a complete system includes. That response requires all three elements — empathy, technical accuracy, and confident specificity — to work. Practice until it sounds natural.

a channel is a
bucket without

The rep who understands the system — every component, why each exists, and why each previous attempt fell short — is the one customer finally believes. That belief closes the job and generates the referral.

Next Job Type: Basement Drainage — the final job type deck

### Notes:
CLOSING SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESThe closing analogy on this slide — 'a pump without a channel is a bucket without a drain' — is worth using in field conversations. It captures the core system concept in a single sentence that every customer can understand immediately. The pump is the emptying mechanism. The perimeter channel is the collection system. Without both, the system only works where the pump happens to be located.This deck, combined with the encapsulation deck, gives reps a complete picture of crawl space moisture management — the two major problems (vapor and bulk water), the two major solutions (encapsulation and drainage), why they must be combined when both problems exist, and why the sequence matters. A rep who has internalized both decks will be able to diagnose and propose correctly for every crawl space moisture scenario they encounter.The referral opportunity in drainage appointments: frustrated customers who finally get a complete solution become highly motivated referrers. They have often talked to neighbors, family members, or coworkers about their crawl space problem over the years — because it has been affecting their home, their health, and their peace of mind for a long time. When the Apex system finally solves the problem, that customer has a story to tell. The rep who made that happen will be the first call when anyone in that customer's network mentions a similar problem.Next up: Basement Drainage — the final job type deck, which covers the interior drainage system for homes with full basements rather than crawl spaces. The core principles are nearly identical, but the installation approach, the customer concerns, and some of the technical details differ enough to warrant a dedicated deck.

### Notes:
COVER SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESThis deck is the technical companion to the sales process curriculum. Where Modules 1 through 6 taught reps how to run an appointment, this deck teaches reps what they are actually selling when the job type is crawl space encapsulation.There are two ways to use this deck.
