#### Slide 2

What Is Crawl Space Encapsulation?
The one-sentence answer — and what it actually means
“

Crawl space encapsulation is the complete sealing of the crawl space from the outside environment — isolating it from ground moisture, outdoor humidity, outside air, and pests — so that the space below your home behaves like a controlled part of the building rather than an extension of the outdoors.

A Complete System — Not Just a Liner
Closed vs. Vented: The Core Distinction




Encapsulation is not putting plastic on the floor. It is a sealed envelope that covers the floor, walls, and all penetrations, combined with a dehumidifier to control humidity and drainage where needed. Every component works together. Installing only the liner without humidity control is like putting on a raincoat and leaving the hood down.
Traditional crawl spaces were vented to the outside air — an approach building science has now demonstrated is counterproductive in humid climates like the Florida Panhandle. Outdoor air in summer carries enormous amounts of water vapor. When that air contacts the cooler surfaces in the crawl space, it condenses. Encapsulation closes the vents and controls humidity mechanically instead.

The Crawl Space Is Part of the Home
Energy and Comfort Benefits




Research by the EPA confirms that 40–50% of the air in the first floor of a home comes from the crawl space below it — pulled upward by the stack effect. This means whatever condition exists in the crawl space — mold spores, moisture, pest activity, volatile compounds from soil — is actively entering the living space above it. Encapsulation addresses the building as a connected system.
Research consistently shows that homes with sealed crawl spaces save 15% or more on annual heating and cooling costs. The crawl space in a vented home acts as a thermal bypass — conditioned floor above, outdoor conditions below. Sealing it closes that bypass and stabilizes floor and room temperatures. Many customers notice the difference in floor comfort within the first winter.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  WHAT IS CRAWL SPACE ENCAPSULATION

### Notes:
WHAT IS ENCAPSULATION — SPEAKER NOTESThe one-sentence definition at the top of the slide is worth memorizing — not word-for-word, but as a concept. Reps who can clearly articulate what encapsulation actually is in plain language, without jargon, establish credibility immediately with customers who have done their own research.On the system concept: this is the most important technical distinction for reps to understand. The most common DIY and low-quality contractor mistake is installing a liner without addressing humidity control. A liner seals in ground moisture — which is significant — but humid air still enters through any un-sealed vent, gap, or penetration. And in a sealed space, if there is no dehumidifier, the relative humidity can actually increase because the liner stops evaporation of ground moisture into the soil, effectively trapping it in the air of the crawl space instead. A complete system includes the liner, sealed vents, sealed penetrations, and a dehumidifier sized for the space.On the vented vs. sealed distinction: reps need to be ready for customers who say 'my neighbor said crawl spaces should breathe.' This was the conventional wisdom for decades and it is still reflected in many older building codes. The building science community has largely moved past it, particularly in humid climates. The argument for venting was that it would exhaust moisture out of the crawl space. In practice, in high-humidity regions, venting brings more moisture in than it takes out during the summer months. The EPA and the Building Science Corporation both now recommend sealed and conditioned or sealed and dehumidified crawl spaces as best practice.On the 40–50% air exchange figure: this is a powerful statistic for customer education because it makes the crawl space viscerally relevant to a homeowner's daily life. They are breathing crawl space air. Their children are breathing it. If there is mold in the crawl space, those spores are in the living space. This is not an abstract structural concern — it is a health and comfort issue.

#### Slide 3

THE  PROBLEM

The Moisture Cascade


A process, not an event — five stages that compound over time

Moisture damage in a crawl space is not a single problem — it is a cascade of connected consequences. Understanding the progression helps reps explain urgency accurately without resorting to scare tactics.

Ground Moisture Evaporation —

STAGE 1
Up to 10–15 gallons of water evaporates daily from exposed soil in a typical crawl space. This moisture becomes vapor, raising the relative humidity in the crawl space — often to 80–100% in summer without intervention.

Condensation on Structural Wood —

STAGE 2
When warm, humid air from the crawl space contacts cooler wood surfaces (floor joists, sill plates, subfloor), it condenses. Wood moisture content begins to rise. At 19%+, wood is considered elevated. At 28%+, active wood rot fungi can establish.

Mold Colonization —

STAGE 3
Mold requires only three conditions to grow: organic material (wood), moisture, and temperatures above 40°F. A crawl space with elevated humidity provides all three year-round. Mold typically appears on the underside of floor joists and subfloor first — invisible from above.

Wood Rot & Structural Degradation —

STAGE 4
Persistent moisture above 28% activates wood-decay fungi. Sill plates — the lowest and most exposed wood members — typically degrade first. Then floor joists. Advanced rot produces floors that flex, squeak, or develop soft spots. This is structural damage — not cosmetic.

Stack Effect Contamination —

STAGE 5
The stack effect draws air upward through the floor system into the living space. Mold spores, dust mite allergens, pest activity, and soil gases (including radon in some geologies) travel this path daily. Indoor air quality degrades. Occupants experience respiratory symptoms, musty odors, and allergies without knowing the source.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  THE PROBLEM — MOISTURE CASCADE

### Notes:
THE MOISTURE CASCADE — SPEAKER NOTESThe cascade structure on this slide is the most important concept for rep education in the problem phase. Most customers experience the problem at Stage 4 or Stage 5 — they notice a soft spot in the floor, a musty smell, or increased allergy symptoms — without understanding that the process that produced those symptoms began at Stage 1 years earlier.This cascade creates a specific opportunity for the rep: when a customer says 'I don't see any mold' or 'the floor feels fine,' the rep who understands the cascade knows that the absence of visible damage does not mean the process hasn't begun. Moisture readings above 19% on wood members are Stage 2–3 evidence that Stage 4 is coming if the conditions are not changed. Photographing the moisture meter reading and explaining the cascade during the photo review gives the customer the scientific basis for acting before visible damage appears.On the 10–15 gallons daily figure: this is a striking statistic that most customers have never heard. Make it concrete — a gallon of water weighs about 8 pounds. The exposed soil in a typical crawl space is evaporating the equivalent of a large bag of dog food in water weight every single day, directly into the space below the customer's living room floor. That image is memorable and accurate.On the 19% and 28% moisture thresholds: reps should know these numbers because they are what the moisture meter readings mean. When a rep records a 24% wood moisture content on a floor joist and shows the customer the photo, being able to say 'normal wood moisture content in a dry environment is 12–15%. Above 19% is elevated. Above 28%, wood-decay fungi become active. Yours is at 24%' is vastly more credible than 'this looks pretty wet to me.' Numbers convert observations into evidence.

#### Slide 4

THE  CAUSE

Why This Region Is High-Risk


Climate + soil conditions create a perfect moisture environment

Purpose: The Florida Panhandle and South Alabama have some of the most challenging crawl space conditions in the country. Reps who understand the regional factors can explain urgency specifically rather than generically.

High Ambient Humidity
Temperature Differential




Average summer relative humidity in the Florida Panhandle exceeds 80%. This means the air in and around the home is carrying enormous amounts of water vapor even on clear, dry days. There is no season where a vented crawl space can 'dry out' the way it might in an arid climate. The moisture source is constant and atmospheric.
The crawl space stays cooler than the outdoor air for most of the year. When warm, humid summer air enters a vented crawl space and contacts cooler surfaces, it condenses immediately. The greater the temperature differential, the more aggressive the condensation. In the Gulf South, this differential is extreme for 6–8 months of the year.

Sandy Soil & High Water Table
Year-Round Growing Season for Mold




Much of the Panhandle sits on sandy coastal soils with a relatively high water table. Sandy soils drain quickly but also have limited capillary resistance — meaning ground moisture migrates readily upward. Homes on these soils see higher ground vapor transmission rates than homes on denser clay soils further inland.
Unlike the upper South where cold winters interrupt mold growth cycles, the Panhandle rarely sees temperatures cold enough to halt mold activity for extended periods. Mold can grow year-round in a crawl space with elevated humidity, meaning the problem compounds continuously rather than seasonally.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  WHY THIS REGION IS HIGH-RISK

### Notes:
WHY THE PANHANDLE IS HIGH-RISK — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide gives reps the regional specificity that turns a generic foundation repair pitch into a local expertise demonstration. A rep who can say 'the Florida Panhandle is one of the most challenging crawl space environments in the country, and here's exactly why' is demonstrating knowledge that a competitor reading from a national sales script cannot match.On the humidity point: 80%+ relative humidity is dramatically higher than what most building standards assume. The Building Science Corporation's crawl space guidance, which has been adopted by many states, was developed partly in response to how poorly traditional venting strategies perform in the Gulf South. Reps should know that the conventional wisdom about venting — which customers often cite — was essentially disproven by field research in high-humidity regions.On the temperature differential: this is the mechanism behind why the venting-makes-it-worse phenomenon occurs. In a simple, intuitive model, people assume that hot air carries moisture and cold air is dry — so ventilating with outside air makes sense. The reality is more nuanced: warm air can hold more moisture than cool air, meaning when warm humid outdoor air enters a cooler space, it cannot maintain its moisture load and deposits it as condensation on every surface. The crawl space effectively acts as a dehumidifier for outdoor air — pulling the moisture out of the incoming air and depositing it directly onto the wood structure.On the mold point: many customers assume that the Panhandle's occasional freezes protect their crawl spaces from serious mold problems. They do not. Mold can survive (though not actively grow) at temperatures below 40°F, and the Panhandle almost never sustains temperatures cold enough to kill established mold colonies. Active growth resumes immediately when temperatures rise. Year-round monitoring and moisture control is the only reliable protection.

#### Slide 5

WRONG  FIXES

Wrong Fixes — Why They Don't Work


The four inadequate approaches reps will encounter in the field

Purpose: Most customers have tried one of these approaches or will consider them as cheaper alternatives. Reps who can explain exactly why each one fails give customers the evidence they need to decide with confidence.

6-MIL BLACK PLASTIC

OPENING MORE VENTS
Why customers choose it
Why customers choose it
Cheap, familiar, widely available at hardware stores. Many contractors install it as a 'vapor barrier.' Customers often already have it and wonder why they still have problems.
Intuitive — 'more airflow will dry it out.' Often recommended by general contractors or well-meaning family members. Was standard building advice for decades.
Why it fails
Why it fails
6-mil poly is not an encapsulation system — it is a partial moisture reducer. It does not seal the walls, seal the vents, or seal penetrations. Moisture still enters from all these paths. It tears easily during any access. It is not tape-sealed at seams, so vapor migrates through every overlap. And without a dehumidifier, humidity in the space continues to rise even with plastic on the floor.
In the Gulf South, outdoor summer air carries more moisture than the crawl space air it replaces. Opening vents pulls humid outdoor air into the crawl space, where it condenses on cooler surfaces and deposits its moisture load. Research by the Building Science Corporation has demonstrated that venting actually increases moisture levels in crawl spaces in hot-humid climates during summer months.

FIBERGLASS BATT INSULATION

MOLD TREATMENT ONLY
Why customers choose it
Why customers choose it
Standard insulation product that many builders install between floor joists as a matter of course. Customers often think the presence of insulation means the crawl space is 'done.'
Lower upfront cost. Customers who see visible mold want it gone. Some contractors offer mold remediation without addressing the moisture source.
Why it fails
Why it fails
Fiberglass insulation absorbs moisture and loses virtually all its insulating effectiveness when wet. In a humid crawl space, batts stapled between floor joists quickly become saturated, sag, fall, and create ideal conditions for mold growth — providing a large, warm, damp organic surface directly in contact with wood. Best practice is to remove fiberglass from the joist bays and insulate the crawl space walls instead, keeping the floor system within the conditioned envelope.
Mold treatment without moisture remediation is the most expensive short-term solution available — because it will need to be repeated. Mold returns to any surface where moisture conditions support growth, typically within one to two seasons. The mold is a symptom. The moisture is the disease. Treating the symptom without fixing the cause is guaranteed recurrence.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  WRONG FIXES — WHY THEY FAIL

### Notes:
WRONG FIXES — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide is one of the most important in the deck for field use. The wrong fixes section of the education phase in Module 3 is where Apex reps create the most dramatic competitive separation — but only if the rep actually knows why each alternative fails in specific, credible terms.On the 6-mil plastic conversation: this is the most common alternative customers cite, often because they already have it or a previous contractor installed it. The response is not 'that doesn't work' — it is a specific explanation of what it does and does not accomplish. It reduces ground vapor transmission, which is valuable. What it does not do is seal the space. Without sealed vents, sealed penetrations, and dehumidification, the humidity in the space continues to rise from other sources. The 6-mil becomes a floor covering in an otherwise uncontrolled environment.On the venting conversation: this is likely the most counterintuitive point in the entire deck because it contradicts decades of conventional wisdom. Reps need to be confident and specific here. The argument is not 'venting is bad' — it is 'in hot-humid climates, venting during summer months brings in more moisture than it removes, because incoming air is warmer and more humid than crawl space air, and deposits its moisture load when it cools.' If a customer pushes back, the Building Science Corporation and the EPA both now recommend sealed crawl space design for hot-humid climate zones.On the fiberglass insulation point: many customers who had their crawl spaces 'done' by a general contractor or builder have fiberglass batts in the joist bays. They are often surprised to learn that this actually makes moisture problems worse. Showing them a photo of collapsed, mold-covered batt insulation makes this abstract concept concrete immediately.On mold treatment only: this is the one that requires the most care because some customers have already paid for mold remediation and are now facing the same problem again. The framing is empathetic but clear: the treatment was probably done correctly, but without fixing the moisture source, recurrence was inevitable. This is not a failure of the previous company's workmanship — it is what happens when the symptom is treated without addressing the cause.

#### Slide 6

RIGHT  REPAIR

The Complete Encapsulation System


Why every component is necessary — and what it accomplishes

Purpose: A complete encapsulation system is not a single product — it is a series of components that work together to seal the space, control humidity, and manage any water that enters. Reps who can explain each component's role are far more persuasive than those who just quote a total price.

01
Heavy-Duty Liner (20-mil)

02
Vent Sealing




The foundation of the system. A 20-mil reinforced polyethylene liner covers the entire floor and extends up the foundation walls, creating a sealed moisture envelope. 20-mil is the industry standard for durability — 10-mil and 6-mil alternatives tear under foot traffic and equipment access, compromising the system.
All crawl space vents are permanently sealed. This eliminates the primary entry point for humid outdoor air. Sealed vents are essential — a liner without sealed vents is a floor with an open window. In the Florida Panhandle climate, this step alone can dramatically reduce summer humidity levels in the crawl space.

03
Commercial Dehumidifier

04
Drainage System (when needed)




A crawl space-grade dehumidifier maintains relative humidity below 55% — the threshold above which mold cannot sustain active growth. Standard household dehumidifiers are not engineered for the temperature and humidity conditions in a sealed crawl space. A properly sized commercial unit runs automatically, drains to daylight or a sump, and requires annual service.
When bulk water entry is present — seasonal seepage, high water table, or active intrusion through walls or floor — a perimeter drainage system and sump pump are added before the liner installation. The drainage system manages water that enters the space; the liner and dehumidifier manage vapor and humidity. Both may be needed in wet conditions.

05
Sealed Access Door

06
Pier Wrap & Penetration Sealing




The crawl space access door is replaced with a sealed, insulated unit that prevents air infiltration. An unsealed access door is a significant humidity entry point — essentially an open hatch to the outdoors. Sealed access doors are often overlooked but are a meaningful component of a complete air-sealed system.
All piers, columns, and utility penetrations (pipes, wires, HVAC ducts) that pass through the liner are sealed individually. Every unsealed penetration is a bypass in the moisture envelope. Tape and foam sealants designed for crawl space conditions are used — not standard duct tape or caulk, which fail under the humidity and temperature cycling in a crawl space.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  THE COMPLETE ENCAPSULATION SYSTEM

### Notes:
THE COMPLETE SYSTEM — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide is the technical heart of the education phase for this job type. The rep's goal is to help the customer understand that each component exists for a specific reason — and that omitting any one of them leaves a gap in the system.On leading with the system framing: before going through each component, establish the overall concept: 'A complete encapsulation system works like a thermos — not just the walls, but the lid, the seal, and the insulation all working together. Remove any one component and the whole system's effectiveness is compromised.' This analogy gives the customer a mental model before the technical details.On the liner mil thickness: customers who have done any online research will often have seen references to cheaper 6-mil or 10-mil options. The explanation of why 20-mil is worth the premium is simple: crawl spaces require access for HVAC service, plumbing maintenance, pest inspections, and other routine work. A 6-mil liner will develop punctures and tears within one to two years of regular access. A 20-mil liner is engineered to withstand that traffic for 15–25 years. The premium is not materials margin — it is longevity.On the dehumidifier sizing point: reps are often asked whether a customer can just buy a dehumidifier at Home Depot and put it in the crawl space. The answer is technically yes, but practically no. Standard household dehumidifiers are designed for conditioned space temperatures — typically 65–85°F. Crawl spaces in the Gulf South frequently see temperatures outside that range, and the efficiency of a standard unit degrades dramatically. Crawl space-grade dehumidifiers are designed for wider temperature ranges and higher continuous duty cycles. They also typically include auto-drain capability, which is essential in a crawl space where the homeowner is not going to be emptying a tank regularly.On drainage and when it's needed: reps should be comfortable distinguishing between vapor (what the liner and dehumidifier address) and bulk water (what drainage and a sump pump address). Many crawl spaces need only the encapsulation system. Some also need drainage. The inspection documentation — photos of water staining, evidence of past flooding, measured soil moisture — determines which components are warranted. Never propose drainage components that the inspection data doesn't support. Never omit them when it does.

#### Slide 7

WHAT TO  EXPECT

What the Customer Should Expect


Installation, timeline, results, and long-term care

Purpose: Customers who know exactly what to expect before, during, and after the job are more likely to say yes and far less likely to have complaints afterward. Managing expectations is part of the sale.

Installation Process

Results & Timeline
▸  Crew access: Crew will need crawl space access and clear path from entry point. Any storage items should be moved out beforehand.
Days 1–7:  Humidity levels in crawl space begin dropping immediately. Dehumidifier runs more frequently at first as it processes years of accumulated moisture.
▸  Prep work first: Old fiberglass insulation, debris, and any existing liner are removed. Mold treatment is applied if active mold is present.
Weeks 2–4:  Musty odors in the living space typically diminish as the air exchange cycle clears. Many customers notice this before any other change.
▸  Drainage first: If a drainage system is part of the scope, it is installed before the liner — never after.
Month 1–3:  Wood moisture content drops as lumber equilibrates to the new lower-humidity environment. Floor stiffness and squeak reduction often noticed.
▸  Liner installation: Typically 1–2 days for an average-sized crawl space, depending on access difficulty and scope.
Year 1:  Energy bills typically reflect 10–15% reduction in heating and cooling costs. Pest activity decreases as conditions become inhospitable.
▸  Dehumidifier setup: Unit is installed, drain line routed, and tested before crew departs. Rep walks customer through controls.
Annual:  Dehumidifier filter cleaning and service check recommended. Liner inspection for any tears or gaps. Pest and drainage inspection if applicable.
▸  Final walkthrough: Crew walks the customer through the completed space and explains the annual maintenance schedule.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  WHAT TO EXPECT — INSTALLATION & RESULTS

### Notes:
WHAT TO EXPECT — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide closes the sales-framing section of the deck. Its purpose is to ensure the customer has a clear mental picture of what they are agreeing to — which reduces post-sale anxiety, eliminates common objections, and produces satisfied customers who are more likely to refer.On managing the installation timeline: customers who are told 'it'll take a day or two' and then find a crew in their crawl space for three days are unhappy, even if the work is excellent. Set the expectation precisely based on the scope. A straightforward liner and dehumidifier job on a typical 1,200 sq ft crawl space is typically one day. A job that includes drainage installation, mold treatment, and insulation removal before encapsulation can run two to three days. Know what's in the scope and set the specific expectation.On the early dehumidifier run time: customers sometimes call concerned because their dehumidifier is running constantly in the first few weeks. This is normal and should be explained proactively. The dehumidifier is processing the moisture that has accumulated in the wood structure, the soil, and the air over potentially years of elevated humidity. Once the system reaches equilibrium, run time normalizes significantly. Telling customers this in advance prevents a worried call in week two.On the energy savings claim: be careful here. The 15% figure is from research on closed crawl spaces generally, and it is a reasonable average for the region. But individual results vary significantly based on existing insulation levels, HVAC system efficiency, and home configuration. The honest framing is: 'Research consistently shows homes with sealed crawl spaces in this climate save 10–20% on heating and cooling costs. Your results will depend on your specific system, but lower energy bills are one of the benefits most customers notice within the first full year.'

#### Slide 8

TECH  SPECS

Technical Specifications


Product knowledge every rep should know cold
These specifications are what analytical customers ask about. Knowing them cold separates a rep who commands credibility from one who has to say 'I'll get back to you on that.'

Liner

Dehumidifier

Key Measurements
Thickness: 20-mil minimum (0.020")
Type: Commercial/crawl space grade only
Normal wood MC: 12–15% (equilibrium moisture content)
Material: Reinforced polyethylene, multi-ply
Capacity sizing: Pints/day based on sq footage + conditions
Elevated wood MC: 19%+ (risk zone)
Color: White interior for visibility and inspection
Target humidity: Below 55% relative humidity
Rot threshold: 28%+ (active decay fungi)
Coverage: 100% floor + walls to 6" above grade
Drain: Auto-drain — no manual emptying
Safe crawl RH: Below 60% (below 55% preferred)
Seams: 12" overlap, vapor barrier tape sealed
Temperature range: Rated for 40°F–100°F operation
Mold growth RH: Above 60% sustained
Wall attachment: Butyl tape or mechanical fasteners
Controls: Digital humidistat with setpoint control
Daily soil vapor: 10–15 gallons per typical crawl space
Lifespan: 25+ years with proper installation
Service: Annual filter cleaning + service check
Energy savings: 15%+ HVAC savings typical
Moisture rating: Near-zero vapor permeability
Warranty: Varies by manufacturer — typically 5 yr
Air exchange: 40–50% of 1st floor air from crawl space



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  TECHNICAL SPECIFICATIONS

### Notes:
TECHNICAL SPECIFICATIONS — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide is a field reference. Reps should review it before any encapsulation appointment until these numbers are genuinely familiar. The goal is not memorization of every figure — it is the ability to answer the most common technical questions without hesitation.The questions reps are most likely to face from analytical customers:'Why 20-mil and not 10-mil or 12-mil?' — Because crawl spaces require periodic access for HVAC, plumbing, and pest service. 20-mil withstands foot traffic and equipment contact. 10-mil develops tears within 2–3 years of regular access. The additional cost is recovered in longevity.'Can I just buy a dehumidifier at Home Depot?' — Standard household dehumidifiers are rated for 65–85°F operation and typical home humidity levels. Crawl space conditions exceed both parameters. Commercial crawl space dehumidifiers are rated for 40°F–100°F and continuous high-humidity operation. They also drain automatically — you cannot rely on a homeowner to empty a tank in a crawl space.'What moisture reading is actually dangerous?' — 19% is elevated and represents risk if conditions persist. 28% is the threshold where active wood decay fungi can establish. Most of the crawl spaces Apex inspects in this region show 22–30% on floor joists and sill plates. That range is active damage territory.'How much does it really save on energy bills?' — Research consistently shows 10–20% reduction in HVAC costs for homes with sealed crawl spaces in hot-humid climates. Avoid citing a specific number as a guarantee. The honest range and the mechanism (closing the thermal bypass between conditioned floor and outdoor conditions below) is more credible than a precise promise.

#### Slide 9

IN  THE  FIELD

How to Talk About Encapsulation


Connecting technical knowledge to what the customer actually cares about

Purpose: Technical knowledge only produces closes when it is translated into language that connects to the customer's emotional driver. This slide bridges the technical and the sales-psychological.

FEAR & LOSS AVERSION
What you know:
Wood moisture above 19% indicates active deterioration risk. Mold spores are present in crawl spaces with RH above 60%. Stack effect moves these conditions upward into living space.
What you say:


'Based on the moisture readings I took today, your floor joists are in a range where deterioration can become active. Left unaddressed over another wet season or two, we move from a remediation conversation to a structural repair conversation — which is a very different cost.'

PROTECTION & SAFETY
What you know:
40–50% of first-floor air comes from the crawl space. Mold spores, allergens, and pest debris travel via the stack effect into living areas occupied by children, elderly, and anyone with respiratory sensitivities.
What you say:


'I want to be straightforward with you about this — the air your family is breathing on the first floor of this home contains a significant percentage of air from that crawl space. What we found down there doesn't stay down there.'

FINANCIAL & EQUITY
What you know:
Foundation and crawl space issues are among the top five deal-killers in real estate transactions. Homes with documented encapsulation systems command premium positioning and smoother sales processes.
What you say:


'I'll leave you the full documentation from today regardless of what you decide. If you're planning to sell, having a professional inspection on record — and ideally a completed encapsulation system — changes the conversation you'll have with buyers and their inspectors significantly.'



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  IN THE FIELD — BRIDGING TECHNICAL TO SALES

### Notes:
HOW TO TALK ABOUT ENCAPSULATION — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide is the integration point between the technical content and the sales process curriculum. Module 6 (Customer Psychology) taught reps that customers have different emotional drivers, and that the most effective communication connects technical evidence to the specific driver that motivates this customer.The three examples here map to the three most common emotional drivers for encapsulation customers. The loss aversion framing is for the customer who is worried about the problem getting worse. The safety framing is for the customer with children, elderly parents, or anyone with respiratory sensitivities. The financial framing is for the customer who is approaching a sale or thinking about home equity.The technical content does not change based on the driver — what changes is which aspect of the technical reality is emphasized and how it is connected to what the customer cares about. A rep who knows the technical content deeply enough will find that this translation feels natural rather than scripted, because the connection is genuine. The air quality impact really is relevant to safety. The moisture cascade really does have financial consequences. The documentation really does help with real estate transactions.A practical exercise for team training: have reps read a given technical fact aloud, then practice stating the same information in language a homeowner would find personally relevant — without using any technical jargon. 'Wood moisture content of 24% represents elevated risk for decay fungi establishment' becomes 'the wood under your floor is wet enough that we're already seeing the conditions that lead to rot.' Same fact. Very different impact.

#### Slide 10

Common Customer Questions
The questions every rep will face — with confident answers

"Is encapsulation really necessary, or is it just a contractor upsell?"
"We had a plastic liner put down years ago. Why isn't that enough?"
Acknowledge the skepticism directly: 'That's a fair question — it gets oversold in some markets. What I can tell you is what I actually measured in your crawl space today: [specific reading]. At that level, here's what the research shows happens over time...' Evidence-based framing, not defensive framing.
The liner reduced ground vapor but didn't seal the space. Your vents are still open, your access door isn't sealed, and without a dehumidifier the humidity continues to rise from outdoor air. Think of it as putting a tarp on the floor of a room with the windows open. The floor might stay drier, but the air is still fully humid.

"How is this different from what [Competitor Name] quoted me?"
"My house was built this way — it's been fine for 40 years."
Ask what the competitor's scope included. Many quotes omit the dehumidifier, use thinner liner, or don't seal the vents. Ask them: 'Did their quote include a commercial dehumidifier? What mil liner? Did they include vent sealing?' Let the scope comparison do the work rather than speaking negatively about the competitor directly.
The home may have been fine for 40 years despite the conditions — or the conditions may have been accumulating damage that is only now becoming visible. The moisture readings and photos from today show the current state of the wood. Whether it has been 'fine' for 40 years is less relevant than what is measurably happening now.

"What happens if I don't do anything?"
"Can I do part of it now and the rest later?"
Be specific and honest: 'Based on what I documented today, the most likely progression is continued wood moisture increase through the next wet season, which moves you closer to the rot threshold. I can't give you a precise timeline — moisture damage is variable. What I can tell you is that the conditions that are present do not self-correct.'
Phase the work intelligently: drainage and liner first if water management is the primary issue. But be clear that a liner without a dehumidifier is not a complete system — it seals in moisture rather than eliminating it. The minimum complete system is liner + sealed vents + dehumidifier. Drainage is added when bulk water is present.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  COMMON CUSTOMER QUESTIONS

### Notes:
COMMON QUESTIONS — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide is a field-prep reference. Before an encapsulation appointment, reps should review these questions and answers until they feel natural — not scripted, but genuinely ready.The most important question on the list is the first one: 'Is this really necessary, or is it a contractor upsell?' This question is increasingly common as homeowners do online research before appointments. The correct response is not defensive and not dismissive — it is to acknowledge that the skepticism is reasonable and then immediately pivot to the specific evidence from this customer's inspection. 'Here's what I measured in your crawl space today' is always a more persuasive response than any general argument for encapsulation.On the competitor question: this is the one reps often handle poorly, either by speaking negatively about the competitor (which damages trust) or by avoiding the question (which makes the rep seem evasive). The correct approach is to ask what the competitor's scope actually included and let the comparison happen on scope, not on rhetoric. In many cases, comparing a complete system proposal with a stripped-down alternative reveals that the Apex price is actually comparable on a component-by-component basis.On 'it's been fine for 40 years': this objection is really about the customer questioning whether the problem is real. The answer is not to argue about the past — it is to focus on the present measurements. Whatever happened over the past 40 years, the moisture readings from today show the current condition of the wood. That is the relevant fact.On phasing: the minimum complete encapsulation system is liner + sealed vents + dehumidifier. The reason not to split these three is that a liner without a dehumidifier in a hot-humid climate can actually increase relative humidity in the sealed space because it prevents ground vapor from absorbing back into the soil. The dehumidifier is not optional — it is what makes the sealed system functional.

========== FILE: 02 - Job Type Education/Apex JobType PolyInjection.pptx ==========
#### Slide 1

JOB TYPE TRAINING


POLY INJECTION /
Slab Leveling

Why Slabs Sink  ·  How Poly Works  ·  vs. Mudjacking  ·  Tech Specs
FLORIDA PANHANDLE  ·  SOUTH ALABAMA

Internal Training Use Only

### Notes:
COVER SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESPoly injection is one of the most visually impressive repairs Apex performs — and it is also one of the most misunderstood by customers who have seen it online without context. A customer who watched a video of foam lifting a driveway on YouTube will have some familiarity with the concept, but usually doesn't understand why the slab sank in the first place, why poly is superior to the alternatives, or when it is and isn't the right solution.This deck trains reps to be credible experts on all three of those dimensions. The structure follows the same sales presentation flow as the encapsulation deck: problem first, then cause, then wrong fixes, then the correct repair and its specific advantages, then technical specs and field language.The most important thing for reps to understand about poly injection is this: it is not just a cosmetic repair. When a slab has sunk because the soil beneath it has eroded, settled, or lost bearing capacity, a void exists under some portion of that slab. Filling the void and lifting the slab back to grade is both a safety repair and a structural one. Reps who understand this framing will sell it very differently from reps who describe it as 'leveling out a bumpy driveway.'

#### Slide 2

What Is Poly Injection / Leveling?
The one-sentence answer — and why it matters structurally
“

Polyurethane injection leveling is the process of lifting a sunken concrete slab back to its original grade by injecting a high-density, two-part expanding foam beneath it — filling the voids that caused the settling and restoring the soil's load-bearing capacity, without tearing out the existing concrete.

Concrete Doesn't Just Settle — Soil Fails First
The Chemistry: Two Parts Become Foam




Concrete is rigid and extremely heavy — a typical residential slab weighs 12–15 lbs per square foot. When the soil beneath it loses density, bearing capacity, or continuity, the concrete above follows gravity into the void. Poly injection doesn't just fix the surface — it restores the soil condition that supports the slab.
Polyurethane foam is produced by mixing two liquid components — an isocyanate and a polyol — that undergo an exothermic chemical reaction on contact. The resulting foam expands to approximately 15 times its original volume in seconds, filling voids and exerting upward pressure that lifts the slab. It reaches 90% of final strength within 30 minutes.

Small Holes, Big Impact
Not Just Driveways — Also Structural Applications




Injection requires drilling holes approximately 5/8" in diameter — roughly the size of a dime — through the slab at strategic locations. Injection ports are inserted, the foam is pumped beneath the slab, and the lift is monitored in real time. Holes are patched with cement after the slab reaches grade. The repair is nearly invisible and the surface is usable within an hour.
Poly injection is used for driveways, sidewalks, pool decks, garage floors, interior slabs, steps, and any other settled flatwork. It is also used for void fill beneath foundation footings and slabs-on-grade where soil loss has created unsupported areas beneath the structure itself — a higher-stakes application with significant structural implications.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  WHAT IS POLY INJECTION / LEVELING

### Notes:
WHAT IS POLY INJECTION — SPEAKER NOTESThe framing that matters most on this slide is the distinction between a cosmetic repair and a structural one. Many customers — and many reps who haven't been trained on this — think of poly injection as a way to make a driveway look level. That framing undersells the repair and makes it harder to justify the price.The correct framing: when a slab has sunk, it sank because the soil beneath it lost its ability to support the concrete's weight. That soil failure created a void — an unsupported area beneath the slab. A slab with an unsupported area beneath it is not just unlevel; it is structurally compromised. It is more likely to crack under load, more likely to continue settling, and in extreme cases more likely to fail entirely. Poly injection fills that void, restores the load path through the soil, and lifts the slab back to grade. That is a structural repair.On the chemistry: reps don't need a chemistry degree, but understanding the basic mechanism — two liquids react, foam expands to 15x its volume, lifts the slab — gives them the ability to answer the question 'how does it actually work?' with confidence. The exothermic reaction produces heat that can sometimes be felt through the concrete during injection, which can surprise customers who are watching. Knowing this in advance lets reps explain it rather than being caught off guard.On the structural applications: this is the most technically significant paragraph and the one most likely to surface in jobs involving interior slabs-on-grade or footings. When soil has eroded beneath a footing or a load-bearing interior slab, the structural stakes are higher than a settled driveway. Reps should identify these situations and ensure the proposal is framed accordingly.

#### Slide 3

THE  PROBLEM

Why Slabs Sink


Five causes — all rooted in soil instability beneath the concrete

Purpose: Every customer asks some version of 'why did this happen?' A rep who can answer specifically — with reference to what was actually observed — establishes credibility and connects the repair directly to the root cause.

Soil Erosion from Water —

01


The most common cause in the Panhandle. Water from gutters, downspouts, surface runoff, or leaking plumbing infiltrates the soil beneath the slab and physically carries away fine soil particles over time. Each rain event removes more material. The void grows until the slab can no longer bridge it and begins to drop. Open cracks and expansion joints are common entry points.

Poor Compaction at Construction —

02


When concrete is poured on fill soil that was not adequately compacted, the loose material compresses under load over the following years — often 5 to 10 years after initial construction. The slab gradually sinks as the soil beneath it densifies under its own weight and the weight of the concrete above. This is particularly common in newer subdivisions built on disturbed ground.

Shrink-Swell Soil Cycles —

03


Clay-bearing soils expand when wet and contract when dry. In the Gulf South's seasonal humidity cycles, this expansion and contraction repeats annually. Over time, the shrinkage phase creates gaps beneath the slab that don't fully refill when the soil re-expands. Each cycle can deepen the void slightly, accelerating settlement over years.

Plumbing Leaks —

04


A slow leak from an underground supply or waste line beneath a slab continuously saturates and erodes the surrounding soil. The leak may go undetected for months or years while progressively undermining the slab's support. Settlement from a plumbing leak typically produces localized, rapid sinking directly above the leak path. The leak must be repaired before poly injection.

Organic Decay and Pest Activity —

05


When organic fill material — wood scraps, stumps, debris buried during construction — decomposes beneath the slab, it leaves a void exactly the shape of the original material. Similarly, burrowing pests (moles, chipmunks, ants in fire ant country) displace soil and create voids. Both produce irregular settlement patterns that can be difficult to anticipate from surface observation alone.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  THE PROBLEM — WHY SLABS SINK

### Notes:
WHY SLABS SINK — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide gives reps the vocabulary to answer the single most common customer question in a poly injection appointment: 'How did this happen?' The answer to that question is important for two reasons. First, it establishes the rep's expertise. Second, it determines whether any pre-conditions need to be addressed before the injection — particularly plumbing leaks.On soil erosion: in the Florida Panhandle, this is by far the most common cause. The combination of sandy soils, high rainfall, and the frequent construction pattern of routing downspouts to drain beside patios and driveways creates ideal conditions for soil washout over years. Reps doing the inspection should look for water staining patterns, gutters that drain toward the settled slab, and any evidence of water intrusion through the slab itself.On poor compaction: this is the cause that makes customers feel like they were let down by whoever built their home. Handle it with appropriate empathy — 'this is extremely common in homes built in the last 15 to 20 years as development expanded in this area' — while being honest about the cause. Customers who feel the builder was careless will sometimes direct anger at the rep. Stay factual.On plumbing leaks specifically: this is the most important diagnostic flag in this slide. If a customer describes localized, rapid, or unexpected settlement — especially on an interior slab — a plumbing leak should be suspected. Poly injection applied over an active plumbing leak will produce temporary results at best and may mask a worsening problem. Never proceed with injection until the possibility of a plumbing leak has been evaluated. This is both ethical and practical — the rep who catches a plumbing leak the customer didn't know about becomes a trusted advisor rather than just a contractor.

#### Slide 4

THE  PROBLEM

Recognizing the Problem


What customers see — and what it actually means

Purpose: Customers describe symptoms — not causes. Reps who know how to translate a customer's symptom description into a specific structural diagnosis arrive at the proposal stage with far more credibility.

What the Customer Says

What the Rep Hears
▸  "My driveway has a step at the garage"
→  Differential settlement — slab on one side of joint dropped relative to the other
▸  "The patio is lower on one side"
→  Uneven void beneath one panel — that panel has lost soil support
▸  "There's a crack running across my sidewalk"
→  Slab has cracked at a weak point under stress from differential settlement
▸  "My garage floor slopes toward the middle"
→  Central panel lost support — possible plumbing leak or organic decay beneath
▸  "Someone tripped on our pool deck"
→  Tripping hazard — safety liability, and a strong urgency argument
▸  "The steps are pulling away from the house"
→  Steps have settled independently of the foundation wall — needs individual panel lift



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  THE PROBLEM — RECOGNIZING THE SIGNS

### Notes:
RECOGNIZING THE PROBLEM — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide trains reps to develop a diagnostic ear — the ability to hear a customer's description of a symptom and immediately understand the structural condition it represents. This translation skill is one of the clearest visible signals of expertise during the customer walk-around.On the step at the garage: this is probably the most common presentation. The concrete slab of the driveway has settled independently of the garage floor, creating a lip at the threshold. This is almost always a void-fill and lift scenario — the driveway slab panel adjacent to the garage has lost soil support and dropped. The garage floor itself typically hasn't moved because it is protected by the structure above it.On the central slope in the garage floor: this symptom pattern — higher at the edges, lower in the center — is the diagnostic signature of a plumbing leak or organic decay beneath the central portion of the slab. The perimeter of the slab is typically supported by grade beams or the foundation wall, while the interior is supported only by the soil beneath. When the interior soil fails, the slab droops in the middle. Reps who see this pattern should explicitly note the possibility of a plumbing issue in their inspection documentation and discuss it with the customer before proposing poly injection.On the tripping hazard framing: the liability angle is genuine and worth mentioning. A lip of 1/2" or more at a sidewalk or pool deck joint is a recognized trip hazard in building code and insurance contexts. For customers who are rental property owners, HOA managers, or anyone with regular foot traffic, the liability angle is not scare tactics — it is relevant professional advice. Frame it as: 'From an insurance and liability standpoint, a differential of this magnitude at a public access area is something most property owners want to address.'

#### Slide 5

WRONG  FIXES

The Alternatives — And Their Limits


Understanding the competitive landscape gives reps confident answers

Purpose: Customers will compare poly injection to mudjacking, grinding, and full replacement. Reps who understand each option's genuine strengths and weaknesses will handle these comparisons with credibility — not defensiveness.

MUDJACKING (TRADITIONAL SLABJACKING)
Pros: Established method. Lower material cost. Uses natural materials (soil, sand, water, cement slurry).  Cons: Requires 2"–2.5" holes (vs. 5/8" for poly). Adds significant weight to already-stressed soil. Slurry can wash out again over time. Does not permanently stabilize soil — fills the void but does not address the cause. Slower cure (24–48 hrs before full load). Aesthetic impact is greater due to hole size.
Bottom line: Mudjacking is a legitimate option for flat, accessible slabs on stable soil where aesthetics are less of a concern. It is not better than poly — it is different. Know when to say so.

GRINDING / SURFACE LEVELING
Pros: Fast. Inexpensive. No drilling required. Good for small lips at joints that are safety hazards.  Cons: Does not address the void or the cause. The slab continues to settle. Grinding exposes the concrete's pores to moisture damage and accelerates deterioration. A lip that is ground down will reappear as the settling continues. Best viewed as a temporary safety measure, not a repair.
Bottom line: Grinding is appropriate for very minor trip hazards where the slab is otherwise stable and the differential is minimal. Not a substitute for poly injection when a void exists.

FULL SLAB REPLACEMENT
Pros: Produces new concrete. Eliminates severely cracked or deteriorated slabs that cannot be lifted. Sometimes the only option.  Cons: Demolition disturbs and loosens the soil beneath — the primary cause of settlement in many cases. New concrete poured on disturbed soil will often settle again within 5–10 years. Significantly more expensive. Days to weeks of disruption and cure time. No environmental advantage — concrete waste is landfilled.


Bottom line: Replacement is warranted when slabs are severely crumbled, cracked beyond repair, or need to be redesigned. For slabs that are structurally sound but sunken, poly injection is faster, less invasive, and more cost-effective.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  ALTERNATIVES — MUDJACKING / GRINDING / REPLACEMENT

### Notes:
ALTERNATIVES — SPEAKER NOTESThe framing for this slide is critical: present these alternatives as a professional comparative analysis, not as a dismissal of competing methods. Reps who speak poorly of mudjacking — 'that's the old way, it doesn't work' — lose credibility with customers who have had mudjacking done successfully or who have a relative who swears by it. Instead, the goal is accurate characterization of each method's genuine strengths and the specific conditions under which each is and is not optimal.On mudjacking specifically: the most important differences to communicate are hole size, weight, and longevity. Mudjacking requires 2" holes, which are more visually obvious and harder to patch invisibly. The slurry adds 100+ pounds per cubic foot to the soil — additional weight on soil that has already demonstrated an inability to support load. And the slurry can erode out of the void over time, producing re-settlement. Poly weighs approximately 3 lbs per cubic foot, does not erode, and does not add meaningful load. These are factual differentiators, not marketing claims.On grinding: be very clear with reps that grinding is never a substitute for poly injection when a void exists beneath the slab. The grinding removes material from the high side of a joint to eliminate the trip hazard — but the slab continues to settle, and within 6–18 months the differential typically returns. Grinding without void fill is treating the symptom without addressing the cause. The appropriate use of grinding is for extremely minor differentials (1/4" or less) on otherwise stable slabs where no void is suspected.On replacement: the key point here — and one that surprises customers — is that replacing a settled slab often produces another settled slab within a decade. The demolition process disturbs and loosens the soil beneath. New concrete poured on freshly disturbed soil will settle again as that soil naturally compacts under load. Without soil stabilization or void fill as part of the replacement process, full replacement just restarts the clock.

#### Slide 6

RIGHT  REPAIR

How Poly Injection Works


Step by step — what happens and why it lasts

Purpose: A rep who can walk a customer through the process step by step — before the crew arrives and again during the work — builds enormous credibility. The process is genuinely impressive when properly understood.

01
Inspection & Hole Placement Planning

02
Drilling the Injection Holes




The rep or crew identifies the settled panels, maps the void extent by probing and visual inspection, and marks injection hole locations strategically. Holes are placed to achieve even foam distribution beneath the slab — too few holes and the foam doesn't reach all areas; too many and the slab can over-lift.
5/8" holes are drilled through the slab at the marked locations using a rotary hammer drill. These holes are roughly the diameter of a dime. Injection ports are inserted into each hole to create a sealed delivery pathway for the foam. The drilling typically takes a few minutes per hole and produces minimal noise or dust.

03
Foam Injection and Lift

04
Grade Verification




A two-component polyurethane system is mixed at the injection gun and delivered beneath the slab. The foam expands rapidly — within 10–15 seconds of injection — filling voids and compressing surrounding soil. The technician monitors the lift in real time, making incremental injections and allowing the foam to cure slightly between injections to prevent over-lifting. The process is precise.
After each panel is lifted, the technician verifies the result against reference grades using a level or laser level. Adjacent panels are compared to ensure even transition. If one injection point over-lifted slightly, adjacent points can compensate. The goal is a smooth, even surface — not necessarily perfect original grade, but safe and aesthetically consistent.

05
Port Removal and Hole Patching

06
Addressing the Root Cause




Injection ports are removed and the holes are filled with a cement patch material that is finished flush with the surrounding concrete surface. The patch is color-matched as closely as possible, though some visible difference in patch color is normal and should be disclosed to customers before work begins. The foam reaches 90% strength in 30 minutes; the surface is typically usable within an hour.
The poly injection restores the slab to grade and fills the void — but if the underlying cause was water erosion from a gutter, a downspout directed at the slab, or a plumbing leak, that cause must be addressed or re-settlement will occur. Always document the likely cause and make a specific recommendation about how to prevent recurrence.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  HOW POLY INJECTION WORKS — STEP BY STEP

### Notes:
HOW POLY INJECTION WORKS — SPEAKER NOTESWalking a customer through this process before the crew arrives is one of the highest-value things a rep can do for job satisfaction and referral generation. When a customer understands what is about to happen, they interpret the experience differently. The drilling sounds become 'the part they told me about.' The foam bubbling up at an adjacent crack becomes evidence that the void is being filled rather than a cause for alarm.On hole placement: many customers ask why there seem to be so many holes, or why some are placed where they wouldn't expect them. The answer is that void distribution beneath a slab is not always predictable from the surface — a slab that appears to have sunk uniformly in the center may have its void extending asymmetrically to one side. The injection pattern is designed to reach the void wherever it is, not just directly beneath the low point.On incremental injection: this is the most technically nuanced part of the process and the one most worth explaining. The foam expands rapidly, and the technician cannot simply pump in a large volume and walk away. Small injections, brief pauses to allow curing, monitoring of lift progress — this is a skilled operation that requires experience to do well. Reps who describe it as 'drilling holes and pumping in foam' are underselling the craft involved.On patch color matching: be honest about this before work begins. The cement patch will be visually distinguishable from aged concrete. This surprises customers who expect an invisible repair. The honest framing: 'The holes are small — about the size of a dime — and we'll patch them flush with a cement compound. The patches will be slightly different in color from the aged concrete around them, which is normal. Over time they'll weather in and become less visible.' Setting this expectation before work begins prevents complaints after.

#### Slide 7

WHAT TO  EXPECT

What the Customer Should Expect


Before, during, and after — set expectations precisely

Purpose: Managed expectations produce satisfied customers. The moments that most commonly produce callbacks or complaints are all predictable — and preventable with precise pre-job communication.

What Is Normal During the Job

Realistic Results and Limits
▸  Drilling noise: Loud but brief. Each hole takes 1–2 minutes.
▸  Return to service: Typically within 1 hour of completion — one of poly injection's biggest advantages over alternatives.
▸  Warmth on the slab: The foam reaction is exothermic. Customers may feel heat through the concrete. Normal.
▸  Crack visibility: Existing cracks in the slab remain after lifting. The slab is re-leveled, not replaced. Cracks can be filled separately.
▸  Foam visible at cracks: If foam escapes through an existing crack or joint, that actually confirms the void was fully reached. Expected.
▸  Perfect level: The goal is a safe, consistent grade — not necessarily millimeter-perfect flatness. Some slabs with multiple settled panels will have slight transitions even after leveling.
▸  The slab won't move smoothly: Concrete lifts in increments. Some points lift before others. Final leveling is achieved through multiple injections across multiple holes.
▸  Longevity: Properly executed poly injection can last the life of the concrete — 20 to 50 years. Longevity depends on addressing the original cause of settlement.
▸  Visible patch holes: 5/8" cement-patched holes will be visible. They weather in over time but never completely disappear.
▸  Not a guarantee against future settlement: If the root cause is not addressed, re-settlement can occur. Document and communicate the cause clearly.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  WHAT TO EXPECT — BEFORE / DURING / AFTER

### Notes:
WHAT TO EXPECT — SPEAKER NOTESThe two most common sources of customer dissatisfaction after a poly injection job are the visible patch holes and the persistence of existing cracks. Both are completely predictable and entirely preventable through clear pre-job communication.On the patch holes: some customers genuinely expect that the 5/8" holes will be invisible after patching. They will not be. The cement patch compound fills the hole cleanly, but the color and texture will differ from the surrounding aged concrete. This should be disclosed explicitly before the work begins, not explained after the fact when the customer is looking at the finished product and disappointed. The correct language: 'I want to make sure you know what the repair looks like when it's done — we drill holes about the size of a dime, which we patch with a cement compound. The patches will be visible but will weather in over time and become much less noticeable.'On existing cracks: the slab was cracked before the work. It will be cracked after the work. The lift may actually cause slight movement at existing cracks as the slab returns to grade — which is normal but can alarm customers who don't expect it. Identify and photograph all pre-existing cracks during the inspection. Include them in the documentation. Explain that the cracks are pre-existing and that the injection will not eliminate them but can fill them as a separate service if desired.On the 1-hour return to service: this is one of poly injection's strongest competitive advantages and should be stated explicitly. Mudjacking typically requires 24–48 hours before full load. Concrete replacement requires days to weeks. Poly injection allows the customer to drive their car on the surface within an hour of completion. For driveways and garage approaches, this is a significant quality-of-life differentiator.

#### Slide 8

TECH  SPECS

Technical Specifications


Numbers every rep should know before any poly appointment
Analytical customers will ask these questions. Knowing the answers cold is the difference between credibility and deflection — and deflection costs closes.

The Foam

The Process

When Poly Is Right
Type: Two-part polyurethane (isocyanate + polyol)
Hole diameter: 5/8" (≈ size of a dime)
Good candidate: Structurally sound slab, void beneath, soil erosion or settlement cause
Expansion ratio: ~15× original injected volume
Mudjacking comparison: Mudjacking requires 2"–2.5" holes
Good candidate: Interior slabs, garage floors, pool decks, steps, driveways
Density: High-density structural grade (~3 lbs/cu ft)
Lift monitoring: Real-time by technician during injection
Not a candidate: Severely cracked, crumbling, or structurally compromised slabs
Initial cure: 10–15 seconds (begins expanding)
Injection increments: Small doses with cure pauses to prevent over-lift
Not a candidate: Active plumbing leak beneath — fix leak first
90% strength: ~30 minutes post-injection
Hole patching: Cement compound, finished flush
Not a candidate: Settlement due to tree root heave (roots must be addressed)
Return to service: Typically within 1 hour
Typical job time: 2–6 hours depending on scope
Check first: Large voids (>6"–8" deep) may require pre-fill before lift
Water resistance: Closed-cell — impermeable to water
Cure before driving: ~1 hour for light vehicles
Check first: Slabs with rebar: foam works around rebar but confirm with structural review
Longevity: Potentially life of concrete (20–50 yrs)
Root cause: Must be addressed or re-settlement can recur
Always document: Cause of settlement + recommendation to prevent recurrence



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  TECHNICAL SPECIFICATIONS

### Notes:
TECHNICAL SPECIFICATIONS — SPEAKER NOTESThe 'When Poly Is Right' column is the most important one from a professional liability standpoint. Reps need to know not just when to recommend poly injection, but when not to — and be able to explain clearly why.The active plumbing leak exclusion: this is non-negotiable. Injecting foam over an active plumbing leak will lift the slab temporarily, potentially mask the leak from detection, and produce re-settlement as the leak continues to erode soil. Always evaluate the possibility of a plumbing source when the settlement pattern is localized, rapid, or consistent with a linear pipe path. If there is a reasonable suspicion, recommend a plumbing evaluation before scheduling injection.On large voids: when the void beneath a slab is very deep — more than 6 to 8 inches — filling it entirely with foam may require very large volumes of material. In some cases, pre-filling the deeper portions of the void with a compactable material before the lift injection is more practical and cost-effective. Reps encountering suspected large voids should flag this for the operations team before quoting the job.On severely cracked slabs: poly injection cannot repair structural damage in the concrete itself. If a slab has fractured into multiple pieces, has spalling or delamination, or has cracked so severely that it can no longer be safely lifted as a unit, replacement may be the only responsible recommendation. Reps should be honest about this and not oversell injection as a solution when the slab condition doesn't support it.The most frequently asked technical question: 'Will this hold forever?' The honest answer is that properly executed injection on a slab where the root cause has been addressed can last the life of the concrete — potentially 50 years. But if the drainage issue that caused the erosion is not corrected, water will continue to work beneath the slab and may eventually produce new voids. Address the cause. Document it in the proposal.

#### Slide 9

IN  THE  FIELD

How to Talk About Poly Injection


Three emotional drivers — three ways to frame the same repair

Purpose: Technical knowledge only closes jobs when it is connected to what the customer cares about. These three framings map the technical facts to the three most common emotional drivers in a poly injection appointment.

In the field: "What I documented today is a trip hazard that creates real liability exposure — especially if you have guests, visitors, or any regular foot traffic on this surface. The void beneath the slab also means this panel is going to continue dropping over time. The good news is this is exactly what poly injection is designed for, and you'd have the surface back in service today."

SAFETY & LIABILITY
Technical: A differential of 1/2" or more at a joint is a recognized trip hazard. The void beneath the slab makes future cracking and further settlement more likely under load.

In the field: "If you're ever planning to sell, this is going to come up in the inspection report — it always does. And buyers use these items to negotiate price reductions that are almost always larger than the actual repair cost. Having this fixed and documented before you list gives you control over that conversation."

FINANCIAL & EQUITY
Technical: Sunken and cracked flatwork is one of the top items flagged in home inspection reports and is often used by buyers to negotiate price reductions or demand repair credits before closing.

In the field: "I want to be honest with you about what the trajectory looks like if this is left alone. The void beneath this panel is going to grow — the same water that caused it is still moving through the same path every time it rains. What's a manageable repair today becomes a more expensive conversation in 18 to 24 months. I'd rather show you that now than have you find out the hard way."

LOSS AVERSION
Technical: Settlement that is caused by soil erosion will continue if the water source is not corrected. Each wet season deepens the void. As the void grows, the risk of slab failure under load increases, and the cost of repair rises.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  IN THE FIELD — CONNECTING TECHNICAL TO SALES

### Notes:
HOW TO TALK ABOUT IT — SPEAKER NOTESThe three framings on this slide represent the three most common emotional drivers that bring customers to a poly injection appointment. Just as with encapsulation, the technical content does not change — what changes is which aspect of the technical reality is highlighted and how it connects to the customer's specific concern.On the safety and liability framing: this is particularly powerful for customers who have elderly relatives living with them, customers who run any kind of home-based business with clients visiting, and landlords or property managers. The liability dimension is real — a trip and fall on documented uneven concrete creates a straightforward negligence argument. Raising this is not scare tactics; it is relevant professional advice that most customers have not considered.On the financial framing: the inspection report angle is highly effective for customers who mention that they might sell in the next few years. Foundation and concrete issues appear in virtually every home inspection report for homes with flatwork. The characteristic pattern is: buyer's inspector documents the issue, buyer submits a repair credit request, seller is pressured to accept because the issue is already documented. The seller who fixed the issue before listing has full control of the conversation. The seller who didn't is negotiating from a disadvantaged position with a documented problem they can no longer dispute.On the loss aversion framing: the key principle is evidence-based urgency. The rep who says 'this will get worse' without explaining why is applying pressure. The rep who says 'the void beneath this slab is going to grow because the water that caused it is still moving through the same drainage path every time it rains — here's the photo showing where that water enters' is providing genuine information. Same urgency. Very different credibility.

#### Slide 10

Common Customer Questions
Answers every rep should have ready before any poly appointment

"Mudjacking is cheaper — why should I pay more for poly?"
"Will you be able to get the slab perfectly level?"
Acknowledge the cost difference honestly. Then explain the specific advantages: smaller holes (5/8" vs. 2"), lighter material (doesn't add weight to compromised soil), faster return to service (1 hour vs. 24–48 hrs), and superior longevity since the foam doesn't erode. Let the customer decide based on accurate information.
Set expectations clearly before work begins: 'Our goal is a safe, consistent grade — not necessarily millimeter-perfect flatness. Slabs that have settled differentially over years can have subtle transitions even after lifting. We'll get it as close as possible, and the result will be safe and significantly improved.'

"How long will it last?"
"Can you fix the cracks in the slab at the same time?"
Honestly and specifically: 'Properly done poly injection can last the life of the concrete — 20 to 50 years — if the cause of the settlement is addressed. If the water source that caused the erosion isn't corrected, the same process can recur. That's why I'm also going to note what I believe caused this and what you can do about it.'
Yes and no. Lifting the slab will not close existing cracks — they remain. Cracks can be filled separately with a polyurethane or epoxy crack filler after the lift is complete. Some customers want this done; others don't. It's a separate line item that should be presented as an option after discussing the lift.

"What if the foam makes it worse — over-lifts a panel?"
"My neighbor said mudjacking failed on their driveway — should I be worried?"
The process is incremental and monitored in real time, precisely to prevent over-lifting. Small injections, cure pauses, and real-time level monitoring allow the technician to stop at the correct grade. Over-lifting is a risk of uncontrolled injection — our process is controlled. If a slight over-lift occurs, adjacent injection points can compensate.
Validate their concern and use it: 'That's exactly why we use polyurethane instead of mudjacking — the slurry used in mudjacking can wash out over time if the drainage issue isn't corrected, while poly foam is closed-cell and water-resistant. Also, mudjacking material weighs significantly more, which can put additional stress on soil that's already struggling.' Then ask about the neighbor's drainage situation.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  COMMON CUSTOMER QUESTIONS

### Notes:
COMMON QUESTIONS — SPEAKER NOTESReview these before every poly injection appointment until the answers feel completely natural. The goal is not to memorize scripts but to develop genuine fluency with the content so the conversation feels like expertise, not recitation.On the mudjacking cost comparison: this is the question reps encounter most frequently, and it's the one most commonly handled poorly through either dismissal ('mudjacking doesn't work') or defensiveness ('our product is better'). Neither approach works. The correct approach is an accurate, respectful comparison that lets the customer draw their own conclusion. Acknowledge that mudjacking has a lower material cost, then explain specifically why poly costs more and what the additional cost buys. Customers who understand the difference will make an informed decision — and they'll trust the rep more for not oversimplifying.On 'how long will it last': this question is an opportunity, not a trap. Many contractors give vague answers ('it should last a good long time'). A rep who gives a specific, honest answer — 20 to 50 years if the cause is addressed — and then explains what needs to be done to achieve that outcome is demonstrating professional credibility that competitors rarely match. The customer may need to correct a drainage issue or seal an expansion joint to achieve the longest outcome. Telling them that, and documenting it in the proposal, makes Apex the company that gave them the complete picture.On the over-lift concern: reassure with process, not just with promises. 'We monitor the lift in real time and inject in increments' is a specific process description that sounds credible. 'Don't worry, we've done this a thousand times' is not. Lead with the mechanism, not the reassurance.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  REP KNOWLEDGE CHECKLIST

### Notes:
KNOWLEDGE CHECKLIST — SPEAKER NOTESThe most important items on this checklist from a professional responsibility standpoint are the ones in the Process Knowledge column around plumbing leaks and knowing when not to recommend poly injection. These are not just technical knowledge items — they are ethical ones.A rep who recommends poly injection without evaluating the possibility of a plumbing leak beneath the slab is potentially selling a temporary fix that masks a worsening structural problem. A rep who oversells injection for a slab that is too severely damaged to be safely lifted is setting up a job failure. These failure scenarios produce the kind of customer dissatisfaction that generates negative reviews, warranty claims, and loss of referrals.The business case for knowing the limits: a rep who says 'I actually don't think poly injection is the right answer for your situation — here's what I'd recommend instead' is building more long-term trust than the rep who closes every job regardless of suitability. Customers remember the person who was honest with them when they could have sold them something. That honesty generates referrals that are worth far more than the job they didn't close.Team exercise: have each rep describe a scenario where they would not recommend poly injection, explain why, and explain what they would recommend instead. If the rep can do this confidently and specifically, they're ready for poly injection appointments.

#### Slide 12

The slab is
the symptom.
The soil
is the cause.



The rep who understands this — who can explain what happened beneath the concrete, why it happened, and how the repair addresses both the void and the cause — will earn trust that no competitor who just shows up and drills holes ever will.

Next Job Type: Structural Piers

### Notes:
CLOSING SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESThe closing message of this deck — 'the slab is the symptom, the soil is the cause' — is the conceptual frame that separates a rep who understands poly injection from one who just knows how to describe the process.A customer who watches a rep drill holes and inject foam will be impressed by the technology. But a customer who hears the rep explain exactly what void exists beneath the slab, precisely why that void formed, what will happen if the drainage issue that caused it is not corrected, and how the injection restores the load path through the soil — that customer is experiencing expertise. They are not buying a concrete leveling service. They are buying a professional who understands their problem well enough to solve it correctly.This distinction — between selling a service and demonstrating expertise — is the difference between a rep who closes at 30% and one who closes at 60%. Both ran the same appointment in the same amount of time. One treated the repair as the product. The other treated their knowledge as the product, and the repair as the demonstration of that knowledge.Next up is Structural Piers — the highest-stakes job type Apex handles and the one where technical credibility matters most, because the customers are making decisions about the structural integrity of their homes and are understandably cautious, well-researched, and occasionally skeptical.

#### Slide 1

JOB TYPE TRAINING

Piers

Why Foundations Settle  ·  Push vs. Helical  ·  Installation  ·  Tech Specs
