### Notes:
COVER SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESStructural piers are the highest-stakes job type Apex performs. When a customer is considering piers, they are not deciding whether to fix a cosmetic problem or a convenience issue — they are deciding whether to address what may be an active threat to the structural integrity of their home. The conversations that happen in a pier appointment carry more emotional weight than any other job type, and the rep who walks in without genuine technical understanding will be exposed immediately.The customer profile for a pier appointment is almost always one of two types: the frightened homeowner who discovered cracks in their foundation walls and doesn't know how serious they are, or the analytical homeowner who has already done significant research online and wants to evaluate whether what Apex is proposing matches what they've read. Both require a rep who knows the material deeply.This deck follows the same structure as the encapsulation and poly injection decks: problem first, then cause, then what doesn't work, then the correct repair with push vs. helical pier differentiation, then technical specs, then how to talk about it in the field. The goal is the same across all three decks — a rep who can explain their job type with the confidence of someone who has spent years in the crawl spaces and around the foundations, not just someone who read a brochure.

#### Slide 2

What Are Structural Piers?
The permanent solution to foundation settlement — and why it works
“

Structural piers are steel shafts driven deep into the earth beneath a settling foundation, bypassing the unstable near-surface soil and transferring the weight of the home to load-bearing strata below — stabilizing the structure permanently and, in most cases, allowing the foundation to be lifted back toward its original elevation.

The Core Principle: Load Transfer
Why Depth Matters




The failing soil near the surface cannot support the home's weight. Piers bypass that soil entirely, reaching down to a competent load-bearing layer — dense clay, gravel, sand with high bearing capacity, or bedrock. Once the pier is anchored in that layer, the home's weight travels down the steel shaft and into soil that has never moved and is unlikely to move.
Near-surface soils are subject to moisture variation, erosion, shrink-swell cycles, and the influence of tree roots — all of the forces that cause foundation settlement. Below a certain depth (typically 15 to 25 feet in this region, depending on geology), soils become more stable, denser, and less affected by surface conditions. Piers access that stability.

Stabilize and Potentially Lift
A Permanent Engineering Solution




Installing piers does two things: it stops future settlement by transferring load away from the failing soil, and it often allows the foundation to be hydraulically lifted back toward its original grade. 'Toward' is the honest word — the degree of lift achievable depends on how long the settlement has been occurring and what secondary damage exists in the structure above.
Unlike surface repairs that treat symptoms, piers address the structural cause. They are designed to last the life of the building — typically 75 years or more — and are backed by engineering load calculations, not just contractor estimates. A pier system is the only foundation repair method that permanently transfers load to stable soil.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  WHAT ARE STRUCTURAL PIERS

### Notes:
WHAT ARE STRUCTURAL PIERS — SPEAKER NOTESThe concept that reps must communicate clearly on this slide is load transfer. Customers who haven't thought about this often assume the pier is somehow 'fixing the soil' — compacting it, stabilizing it, improving it in place. That is not what a pier does. A pier bypasses the problem soil entirely and finds stable soil below it. The failing soil continues to exist; it simply no longer matters structurally because the home's weight is no longer relying on it.An analogy that resonates with most customers: imagine your house is a table and the soil is the table legs. Right now, one of the legs has rotted — it's shorter than the others and getting shorter. You could try to fix the rotted leg, but it's still going to be weaker than the others. Or you could install a new, longer leg that goes straight to the floor — bypassing the rotted wood entirely. That new leg is made of steel and anchored to the concrete floor below. That's what a pier does. It installs a new structural leg that goes straight to the part of the earth that has never moved.On the 'toward' language for lift: this is an important honesty point. Some contractors overpromise on lift, telling customers their foundation will be restored to its original grade. In reality, the degree of lift achievable depends on how long the settlement has been occurring, how much secondary damage has occurred in the framing and structure above, and whether the foundation can physically be moved without causing additional damage to attached elements. The honest framing — 'we can stabilize it permanently and recover some of the settlement, often significantly — but our first goal is always stopping further movement' — is more credible and more ethical than promising a perfect restoration.

#### Slide 3

THE  PROBLEM

Why Foundations Settle


The causes — and why they are progressive, not stable

Purpose: Settlement is not a single event — it is an ongoing process that typically accelerates over time. Reps who understand the cause can explain urgency honestly and connect the repair to the specific conditions they observe during the inspection.

Weak or Poorly Compacted Fill Soil —

01
Many homes in this region were built on fill soil — material brought in and placed rather than native, undisturbed earth. Fill soil that was not properly compacted at construction continues to compress under the weight of the structure above it, often for the first 10 to 20 years of the home's life. As it compresses, the footings above it settle unevenly.

Expansive Clay Soil — Shrink-Swell Cycles —

02
Clay soils expand dramatically when saturated and contract when dry. In the Gulf South's alternating wet and dry seasons, this cycle repeats annually. Over years, the contraction phase can create voids beneath footings that the re-expansion phase does not fully refill. Each cycle deepens the settlement. Clay soil settlement tends to be gradual, differential, and progressive.

Soil Erosion Beneath the Foundation —

03
Poor drainage, broken underground pipes, or surface water infiltrating the soil beneath the footing can gradually erode the material supporting the foundation. Erosion-driven settlement tends to be more localized and faster than compaction-driven settlement. It is also more likely to produce cracking patterns that appear suddenly rather than developing slowly over years.

Organic Decay in the Soil Profile —

04
In some areas of the Panhandle, near-surface soils contain peat, decayed organic matter, or buried organic fill from clearing and grading during development. This material decomposes over time, losing volume. Foundations built on organic-bearing soils tend to settle progressively and unevenly, often requiring piers at significantly greater spacing than typical mineral soil sites.

Tree Root Desiccation —

05
Large trees adjacent to or beneath the foundation can draw enormous quantities of moisture from the soil through their root systems. In drought conditions, this desiccation causes clay soils to shrink beneath the footing, producing settlement. The settlement may be seasonal — more pronounced in summer, partially recovering in winter — until the cumulative deficit becomes structurally significant.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  THE PROBLEM — WHY FOUNDATIONS SETTLE

### Notes:
WHY FOUNDATIONS SETTLE — SPEAKER NOTESThe critical concept on this slide is that foundation settlement is almost always progressive rather than stable. A customer who says 'it's been like this for a few years and hasn't gotten worse' is almost certainly incorrect — settlement tends to accelerate as the soil beneath the failing section becomes less able to resist the load above it. The crack that has 'been the same size for two years' has almost certainly continued to grow at a rate that isn't perceptible during a brief interval of observation.On fill soil: this is extremely relevant in the Florida Panhandle, where rapid suburban development over the past three decades has produced a significant inventory of homes built on fill material of variable quality. The 10-to-20-year timeline for fill soil compression explains why many settlement calls come from homes that are 12 to 18 years old — the soil is finally reaching a point of significant compaction.On clay soil and shrink-swell: this is the most complex cause to explain because it is cyclical and therefore confusing to homeowners who notice that the crack 'opens up in summer and closes in winter.' The seasonal variation is real — clay soils do partially re-expand when re-wetted. But the net effect over years is progressive settlement because the re-expansion phase never fully compensates for the contraction phase. This is a nuance that reps should understand well because customers who observe the seasonal variation often conclude that the problem is 'self-correcting,' which it is not.On tree root desiccation: this is one of the less obvious causes and one that is genuinely useful for reps to raise when there are large trees within 15 to 20 feet of the settling area. The combination of a large tree, a clay-bearing soil profile, and drought conditions is a classic foundation settlement scenario that reps in the Gulf South should be able to recognize on sight.

#### Slide 4

THE  PROBLEM

Reading the Signs of Settlement


What the evidence tells a trained inspector — and how to explain it

Purpose: Crack patterns, door and window behavior, and floor measurements all tell a specific story about where settlement is occurring and how serious it is. Reps who can read these signs become diagnosticians — not just salespeople.

Crack Patterns & What They Mean

Other Settlement Indicators
▸  Stair-step cracks in brick or block: Classic differential settlement signature. The mortar joints are the weakest path — settlement cracks follow them diagonally.
▸  Doors sticking or swinging open/closed: The door frame has racked — the opening is no longer square. Indicates the foundation has moved differentially at that location.
▸  Diagonal cracks at door/window corners: The foundation is dropping at one corner relative to adjacent areas. Load is redistributing through the structure.
▸  Windows sticking or cracking at corners: Same mechanism as doors. Window frames are rectangular; a settled foundation produces a parallelogram. Glass cracks at the corners under this stress.
▸  Vertical cracks in poured concrete walls: Differential settlement pulling the wall apart. Width and displacement at the crack face indicate severity.
▸  Sloping floors: Measured with a level, a slope greater than 1" per 8 feet is typically considered significant. Greater than 2" per 8 feet warrants serious structural attention.
▸  Horizontal cracks in block walls: Lateral soil pressure — different cause from settlement. Requires evaluation for wall reinforcement, not piers.
▸  Gaps at wall-ceiling or wall-floor junctions: The structure is separating at its joints as the foundation moves. These gaps are most visible in corners.
▸  Cracks wider at top than bottom: Foundation is rotating — one end dropping relative to the other. The wide end indicates the area of greatest movement.
▸  Bowing or leaning walls: More advanced stage — the foundation movement has progressed to the point that structural walls have deflected. Requires immediate professional evaluation.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  READING THE SIGNS OF SETTLEMENT

### Notes:
READING THE SIGNS — SPEAKER NOTESThis slide is the most practically useful one in the deck from a field inspection standpoint. The ability to look at a crack pattern and explain what the foundation has been doing — not just that 'there is a crack' — is what separates an Apex rep from a contractor who points at damage and quotes a number.On stair-step cracking: this is the single most recognizable sign of differential foundation settlement, and it is extremely common in the Panhandle's brick veneer homes. When a rep can say 'that stair-step pattern in the brick tells me that the corner of the foundation below it has dropped relative to the adjacent section — that's differential settlement moving through the path of least resistance in the mortar joints,' the customer hears a professional who understands their home. That understanding is worth enormous trust at the proposal stage.On horizontal cracks in block walls: it is critical that reps understand this distinction. Horizontal cracks in a block foundation wall typically indicate lateral soil pressure pushing against the wall from the outside — not settlement. This is a different structural problem with different repair implications (wall anchors or carbon fiber reinforcement, not piers). A rep who recommends piers for a horizontal cracking problem is recommending the wrong repair and will lose credibility with any engineer who reviews the work.On floor slope measurements: reps should know these thresholds and use them in inspection documentation. A slope of 1" over 8 feet is noticeable but may not yet warrant pier installation if the settlement appears stable. A slope of 2" or more over 8 feet is typically treated as significant and indicative of ongoing movement. Anything greater than 3" over 8 feet warrants urgent attention and should be documented carefully.On bowing walls: this symptom indicates a more advanced structural condition that may involve both settlement and lateral wall movement. Reps who observe significant bowing should document it thoroughly, photograph it, and clearly communicate in the proposal that this finding warrants further structural evaluation.

#### Slide 5

RIGHT  REPAIR

Push Piers vs. Helical Piers


Two technologies — same goal, different mechanisms

Purpose: Understanding when each pier type is appropriate is essential product knowledge. Using the wrong pier type produces inferior results. Reps who can explain the distinction build credibility with customers who have researched their options.

PUSH PIERS  (Resistance / Hydraulic Piers)

HELICAL PIERS  (Screw Piles)
▸  How they work: Steel pipe sections are hydraulically driven into the ground using the weight of the structure itself as resistance. Each section is driven until the pier reaches a competent load-bearing layer — indicated by a dramatic increase in resistance (called 'refusal').
▸  How they work: Steel shafts with welded helical plates are mechanically screwed into the ground using a hydraulic torque motor. Capacity is verified by correlating installation torque to published bearing capacity charts — no structure weight required.
▸  Best for: Heavy structures — homes with poured concrete footings, heavier masonry construction, two-story homes. The structure must be heavy enough to serve as the counterweight that drives the pier.
▸  Best for: Lighter structures, homes where the footing is cracked, new construction, tight access areas where heavy equipment cannot reach. Also appropriate when bedrock is too deep for push pier refusal.
▸  Depth: Can reach 30 to 60+ feet, determined by where refusal is encountered. Driven to bedrock or dense load-bearing stratum.
▸  Depth: Typically 15 to 25 feet in this region, driven to the required torque value in competent soil. Can be installed at angles for special applications.
▸  Key advantage: Field load-tested during installation. When the pier reaches refusal, it has demonstrably achieved its required bearing capacity.
▸  Key advantage: Versatile — works in a wider range of soil conditions and structure types. Can be installed immediately adjacent to the foundation without excavating as much material.
▸  Limitation: Not appropriate for light structures or cracked footings, because the driving force can crack the footing if it is not continuous and sound.
▸  Limitation: Relies on the active soil layer for bearing (not bedrock), which is less ideal in soils with significant shrink-swell behavior. More expensive per pier than push piers in most applications.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  PUSH PIERS VS. HELICAL PIERS

### Notes:
PUSH VS. HELICAL PIERS — SPEAKER NOTESThis is the slide that most differentiates a well-trained Apex rep from the competition. Most homeowners who have done research online have read about both pier types and often have a preformed opinion — usually that 'helical piers are better' because they read that somewhere, or 'push piers go deeper' because they saw that in another article. A rep who can explain both types accurately, without advocacy, and recommend the appropriate one based on the specific inspection findings is operating at a professional level that commands respect.The decision framework in simple terms: push piers use the house as a hammer. The house's weight drives the pier into the ground until the pier finds soil dense enough to stop the hammer. This works extremely well for heavy homes with continuous concrete footings. Helical piers use a motor to screw in rather than the house's weight to push in. This makes them appropriate when the house isn't heavy enough, when the footing is too cracked to take the stress of push pier installation, or when the target bearing stratum is not deep enough to produce clean push pier refusal.A useful analogy: imagine you need to drive a nail through a piece of wood. If you have a heavy hammer, you just pound it in. If the hammer is light, or if the wood is fragile and might crack, you pre-drill a pilot hole and use a screw instead. Push piers are the hammer approach. Helical piers are the screw approach. Both achieve the same result — a secure connection to load-bearing material — through different mechanisms suited to different conditions.On the torque verification for helical piers: this is worth explaining to analytical customers. The installation torque measured during helical pier installation correlates to bearing capacity through well-established engineering relationships. This means the installer knows in real time whether the pier has achieved its required capacity — there is no guesswork. The same load verification that push piers achieve through refusal resistance, helical piers achieve through torque measurement.

#### Slide 6

RIGHT  REPAIR

The Installation Process


Step by step — what the crew does and why it matters

Purpose: A rep who can walk a customer through pier installation before the crew arrives prevents surprises and positions Apex as a professional operation, not just a construction crew.

01
Elevation Mapping

02
Excavation at Pier Locations




Before installation begins, a Zip Level or laser level is used to map foundation elevations around the perimeter of the structure. This documents the existing settlement pattern, identifies the areas of greatest movement, and establishes the target lift grade for the repair.
Small areas are excavated adjacent to the footing at each pier location — typically 2 to 3 feet wide and deep enough to expose the bottom of the footing. This soil is set aside and replaced after installation. The excavation is significantly less disruptive than many customers expect.

03
Bracket Installation

04
Pier Driving or Screwing




A steel pier bracket is installed under the footing at each location. This bracket is the connection point between the foundation and the pier shaft below. Its design, quality, and fit to the specific footing geometry are critical — a poorly fitted bracket is the weakest link in the system.
For push piers: hydraulic cylinders drive the pipe sections through the bracket and into the soil until refusal. For helical piers: the hydraulic torque motor rotates the helical shaft into the ground, adding extension sections until required torque is achieved. Both processes are monitored continuously by the installation technician.

05
Hydraulic Lift

06
Lock-Off and Backfill




Once all piers at a given zone are installed, hydraulic jacks are connected simultaneously and activated in a coordinated lift sequence. Lifting simultaneously prevents one area from rising while creating stress on adjacent sections. The lift is stopped when the target grade is reached or when resistance indicates the structure cannot safely be lifted further.
The piers are locked off at the achieved lift elevation. The excavated soil is compacted back into place and the area is graded. Some disruption to landscaping, concrete flatwork, or structural elements adjacent to the excavation areas is normal and should be discussed before work begins.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  THE INSTALLATION PROCESS — STEP BY STEP

### Notes:
THE INSTALLATION PROCESS — SPEAKER NOTESThe pre-job walkthrough conversation is especially important for pier installations because the process is more visually disruptive than encapsulation or poly injection. Customers who haven't been prepared for excavation activity around their home's foundation, or who haven't been told that some existing landscaping or flatwork may need to be temporarily removed, will be alarmed when the crew shows up and starts digging.On elevation mapping: explain to customers why this step matters. The elevation map is what tells the crew where to concentrate the piers and how much lift to target at each location. Without it, the crew is guessing. With it, the installation is engineered — each pier is placed where it will do the most structural work, and the lift is planned so that the foundation moves as a unit rather than creating new differential settlement.On the simultaneous lift: this is the step that most impresses customers who watch it. When multiple hydraulic jacks are activated simultaneously and a home that has been sinking for years slowly rises back toward its original position, the visual effect is significant. Customers who are present for this moment often describe it as the most reassuring thing they've witnessed — the concrete proof that the system is working. Reps should encourage customers to be present for the lift if at all possible.On the lock-off: explain that the piers are mechanically secured in position at the achieved lift elevation before the jacks are removed. This lock-off is the mechanism that makes the repair permanent — the piers cannot return to their pre-installation position because they are physically secured to the brackets at the achieved grade. The home's weight then permanently rests on the pier system, not on the failing soil.

#### Slide 7

WHAT TO  EXPECT

What the Customer Should Expect


Job site reality, lift limitations, and long-term outcome

Purpose: Pier installation is the most complex and most emotionally significant repair Apex performs. Precise expectation-setting before work begins is the difference between a satisfied customer who refers others and a disappointed one who expected a miracle.

During the Job

Realistic Outcomes and Honest Limits
▸  Job duration: Typically 1 to 3 days depending on number of piers and site conditions. Interior work may require furniture moving.
▸  Primary goal is stabilization: Stopping further settlement is always the first objective. Lift is a bonus — not a guarantee.
▸  Excavation: Soil is excavated at each pier location. Expect some disruption to landscaping, mulch beds, or concrete flatwork adjacent to the foundation.
▸  Lift is often significant: Many foundations return close to original grade. Some recover only partially, depending on how long settlement has been occurring and what secondary damage exists.
▸  Noise: Hydraulic equipment is moderately loud during pier driving. The lift phase is quiet.
▸  Cracks remain visible: Piers stabilize and lift the foundation. They do not repair existing cracks in the wall above. Crack repair is a separate scope of work.
▸  Cracks may shift: As the foundation rises, existing cracks may change appearance — some may partially close, others may appear to open slightly as stress redistributes. This is normal and expected.
▸  The repair is permanent: A properly installed pier system transfers load to stable soil and does not require ongoing maintenance. The steel is galvanized and designed for the life of the structure.
▸  Doors and windows: Sticky doors and windows often improve noticeably after lift. Some may require adjustment — door strikes, window tracks — as the frame geometry changes.
▸  Address adjacent damage: Sticky doors, cracked drywall, and separated trim are common secondary effects of settlement. These typically improve with lift but may need separate remediation.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  WHAT TO EXPECT — JOB REALITY & OUTCOMES

### Notes:
WHAT TO EXPECT — SPEAKER NOTESThe single most important expectation to set in a pier appointment is that stabilization is the primary goal, not full restoration. Customers who have watched their home settle for 10 years sometimes arrive at the sales conversation with the belief that piers will 'fix everything' — that their home will look and function exactly as it did when it was new. That expectation is not achievable in most cases, and a rep who allows it to persist is setting up a disappointed customer.The honest framing: 'Our primary goal is to stop the movement permanently and recover as much elevation as the structure will safely allow. In most cases we achieve significant lift — often enough that doors and windows begin functioning properly again and the crack patterns stabilize. But the cracks that are there will still be visible after we're done — they'll need separate cosmetic repair. What piers give you is confidence that the foundation isn't going to continue moving.'On cracks shifting during lift: this is one of the most alarming things a homeowner can observe if they aren't prepared for it. As the foundation rises, stress redistributes through the structure above it. A crack that was 1/8" wide may temporarily widen before narrowing as the foundation reaches its new position. A crack in a different location may appear for the first time. Prepare customers for this explicitly before work begins. The framing is: 'As the foundation moves back toward its original position, you may notice cracks changing in appearance — some may partially close, some may briefly open wider before settling. This is normal structural behavior as the load redistributes. Our technician will monitor this throughout the lift.'On doors and windows: this is often the most immediate and satisfying result for customers. A door that has been sticking for two years suddenly closes properly after the lift. This moment — which often happens during the lift itself — is one of the most powerful demonstrations of why the repair was warranted, and one of the most compelling referral stories a customer can tell.

#### Slide 8

TECH  SPECS

Technical Specifications


The numbers and standards every rep needs before any pier appointment
Pier appointments attract the most technically sophisticated customers Apex sees. These specs are what they'll ask about. Know them.

Push Pier Specs

Helical Pier Specs

Key Thresholds
Shaft material: High-strength steel pipe (typically 2.875" OD)
Shaft material: High-strength steel shaft (1.5" solid or 2.875" pipe)
Significant floor slope: 1" per 8 feet — warrants evaluation
Installation method: Hydraulic cylinder using structure weight as resistance
Installation method: Hydraulic torque motor — no structure weight needed
Serious floor slope: 2"+ per 8 feet — urgent structural concern
Depth range: 30–60+ feet to refusal
Depth range: 15–25 feet typical in Panhandle geology
Pier spacing (typical): 5–8 feet along footing, varies by load calculation
Load capacity: Verified at refusal — typically 30,000–70,000 lbs per pier
Load capacity: Correlated to torque — typically 25,000–50,000 lbs per pier
Pier bracket: Installed under the footing — must engage continuous footing
Load verification: Field tested — refusal resistance confirms capacity
Load verification: Continuous torque monitoring correlates to bearing capacity
Push pier requirement: Footing must be continuous and intact
Best structure: Heavy: poured concrete footings, 2+ story, masonry
Best structure: Light to medium: any footing condition, tight access
Helical advantage: Works with cracked or discontinuous footings
Galvanization: Galvanized or epoxy-coated for corrosion resistance
Helix configuration: Single or multi-helix; adjusted for soil profile
Lift goal: Return to original grade or practical maximum
Warranty: Typically lifetime transferable structural warranty
Warranty: Typically lifetime transferable structural warranty
Settlement criterion: 1/2" differential over 12 feet: monitor. More: evaluate for piers.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  TECHNICAL SPECIFICATIONS

### Notes:
TECHNICAL SPECIFICATIONS — SPEAKER NOTESThe most frequently asked technical questions in pier appointments, and the answers reps need ready:'How many piers will I need?' This depends on the length of the affected foundation zone and the structural load calculation for the building. Piers are typically spaced 5 to 8 feet along the footing in settlement zones — closer spacing for heavier loads or more severe settlement. The exact number is determined by inspection, not by formula.'How deep do the piers go?' For push piers, depth is determined by where refusal is encountered — the crew doesn't know in advance and doesn't control it directly. The pier goes as deep as the soil requires to find a competent layer. In the Florida Panhandle, this is typically 25 to 50 feet. For helical piers, the target depth is determined by the torque required to achieve the specified bearing capacity, which is typically 15 to 25 feet in regional geology.'Will the repair affect my home's value?' The research is clear on this point: documented foundation repairs with a warranty typically improve a home's sale prospects rather than harming them. An undisclosed, unrepaired foundation problem is a deal-killer. A repaired foundation with documentation and a transferable lifetime warranty is an asset — it tells buyers the issue was identified, properly addressed, and guaranteed by a professional company. Reps should be confident and specific on this point.'Is the warranty transferable?' Yes — virtually all major pier system warranties are transferable to subsequent owners, which is one of the most powerful selling points when customers are considering resale value. The documentation package that accompanies the warranty is itself a selling document in a future real estate transaction.

#### Slide 9

IN  THE  FIELD

How to Talk About Structural Piers


Connecting engineering to what the customer cares about most

Purpose: Pier appointments carry more emotional weight than any other job type. The customer is worried about the most significant investment they own. Reps who acknowledge that emotional reality before presenting evidence will always outperform those who lead with data.

In the field: "I want to be straightforward with you about what these measurements mean. The crack pattern I photographed and the floor slope I measured are consistent with active settlement — meaning this is ongoing, not something that happened and stopped. Left unaddressed, the same conditions that produced the movement you're seeing today will continue producing more movement. The good news is that piers are a permanent solution — once they're in, the movement stops."

FEAR & LOSS AVERSION
Technical: Differential settlement is progressive. The crack patterns and floor measurements documented today represent movement that has already occurred — and movement that will continue under the same soil conditions without intervention.

In the field: "Your foundation is what everything else in this house rests on. The movement I documented today is affecting the load paths through your walls and your roof structure. This isn't just a cosmetic issue with some cracks — the foundation supports everything above it. Stabilizing it is protecting the structural integrity of the whole home."

PROTECTION & SAFETY
Technical: A settling foundation affects the structural integrity of the entire building above it — wall framing, roof loads, and all attached elements transfer through the foundation. Progressive settlement can eventually compromise structural safety.

In the field: "From a real estate standpoint, this is the most impactful category of defect a buyer's inspector can flag. An unrepaired foundation problem will either kill your sale or produce a price reduction that's almost always larger than the repair cost. But here's what most homeowners don't realize: a repaired foundation with a lifetime transferable warranty actually becomes a selling point — it tells every future buyer that this issue was professionally addressed and guaranteed."

FINANCIAL & EQUITY
Technical: Foundation issues are the most significant category of defect in residential real estate transactions. An unrepaired, documented settlement problem will reduce sale price or prevent sale. A repaired foundation with a transferable lifetime warranty is an asset.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  IN THE FIELD — CONNECTING TECHNICAL TO SALES

### Notes:
HOW TO TALK ABOUT PIERS — SPEAKER NOTESThe framing principle that matters most in a pier appointment is this: acknowledge the emotional reality before presenting the technical one. A customer who is sitting across the table from a rep, having just learned that their foundation is actively settling, is experiencing a significant level of stress. The rep who launches immediately into measurements and crack patterns without first acknowledging that this is a meaningful concern will be perceived as transactional — selling rather than advising.The acknowledgment doesn't need to be long or performative. Something as simple as: 'I want to start by saying that I understand this is a significant finding about your home, and I'm going to give you as complete and honest a picture as I can of what I found and what it means.' That sentence alone signals that the rep is operating as an advisor rather than a salesperson, and it changes the emotional tone of everything that follows.On the financial framing: this is the most powerful closing argument for customers who are on the fence about timing. The research on foundation issues in real estate is consistent and unambiguous — undisclosed foundation problems are among the top reasons home sales fall through or produce significant price reductions at the inspection contingency stage. A rep who can state this specifically, and who can then explain that a repaired foundation with documentation and a transferable warranty actually improves the transaction, is giving the customer information that genuinely changes the financial calculus of their decision.On the loss aversion framing: the key standard is always evidence-based urgency. The rep who says 'this will get much worse if you don't act' without supporting that claim with specific measurements and observed crack patterns is applying pressure. The rep who says 'the crack pattern I photographed shows progressive differential settlement — this specific type of cracking indicates ongoing movement, not historical movement that has stopped — and here's the documentation' is providing professional information. Those two statements can have the same urgency. Only one is ethical.

#### Slide 10

Common Customer Questions
The questions Apex reps face in every pier appointment

"Do I really need piers, or is this just a cosmetic issue?"
"Can I just fill the cracks and leave it?"
Acknowledge it's a fair question and then answer with the specific evidence: 'The measurement I took shows [X] inches of differential settlement over [Y] feet. The crack pattern is consistent with active movement rather than historical cracking that has stabilized. Those two things together indicate that this is a structural issue rather than a cosmetic one.' Evidence, not assertion.
Crack filling addresses the symptom, not the cause. The foundation will continue moving and the cracks will reappear — often wider. The honest framing: 'You can fill the cracks, and they'll look better for a period of time. But the same soil conditions that produced the movement will continue producing movement. In 12 to 24 months you'll be having the same conversation but from a more advanced starting point.'

"Will this affect my ability to sell the house?"
"How do I know the piers will actually hold?"
The opposite of what most customers fear: 'An unrepaired foundation problem will come up in the inspection report and either kill the sale or produce a price reduction significantly larger than the repair cost. A repaired foundation with documentation and a transferable lifetime warranty actually becomes a selling point — buyers and their agents recognize that this was professionally addressed and guaranteed.'
Explain the load verification mechanism specific to each pier type: 'For push piers, the pier is driven until the soil physically cannot be overcome — that resistance is how we know it has reached load-bearing depth. For helical piers, we continuously monitor the torque during installation, which correlates to bearing capacity through engineering charts. Both methods give us real-time confirmation that each pier has achieved its required capacity before we move to the next one.'

"What if lifting the foundation causes new damage?"
"I got another quote that was much lower. What's the difference?"
This is a legitimate concern that deserves a direct answer: 'We lift incrementally and monitor continuously — we're not trying to force the foundation back to its original position in one movement. We stop when we reach the practical maximum the structure will safely allow. Some stress redistribution is normal and expected during lift, and we photograph conditions throughout the process to document what changes and what doesn't.'
Ask about the other quote specifically: 'Can you tell me what pier type they proposed and how many? The price difference usually comes down to pier count, pier type, or warranty terms. A quote with fewer piers at closer spacing, or helical piers where push piers are more appropriate, or a limited warranty vs. a transferable lifetime warranty, are not equivalent proposals. Let me walk you through what our scope includes so you can compare the two accurately.'



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  COMMON CUSTOMER QUESTIONS

### Notes:
COMMON QUESTIONS — SPEAKER NOTESThe most consequential question on this slide — and the one most likely to determine whether an appointment closes — is the first one: 'Do I really need piers, or is this just a cosmetic issue?' The customer who asks this question is almost always experiencing a version of loss aversion working in the opposite direction: they are hoping that the evidence doesn't mean what they fear it means. A rep who dismisses this question or answers it vaguely will fail to address the customer's underlying emotional need, which is to be told the truth clearly enough that they can make a confident decision.The correct approach to that question: lead with respect for the question, and then answer it with the specific evidence rather than a general statement. 'That's the right question to ask. Here's what I actually measured and observed — [specific measurements and crack patterns] — and here's what those specific findings indicate.' Customers who receive specific evidence in response to a specific question feel heard and respected. Customers who receive a general statement like 'yes, this is definitely a structural issue' feel sold to.On the lower competitor quote: this question will arise in almost every pier appointment where Apex is not the first or only company to visit. The correct response is never to speak disparagingly about the competitor, and never to justify the price gap with vague claims about quality. Instead, ask for specifics about the competitor's scope and then compare them factually. A quote with fewer piers, no warranty transfer, or helical piers where push piers are more appropriate is not equivalent to the Apex proposal. Let the customer see the difference rather than telling them about it.

Structural Piers — Rep Knowledge Checklist
What every Apex rep must know before any pier appointment

The customer in a pier appointment needs an advisor, not a salesperson. Technical depth is what earns that role.

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  REP KNOWLEDGE CHECKLIST

### Notes:
KNOWLEDGE CHECKLIST — SPEAKER NOTESThe most critical item on the entire checklist for pier appointments is in the Product Knowledge column: 'Horizontal cracks = lateral pressure, not settlement — different repair.' This is the single most important diagnostic distinction in foundation repair, and a rep who misses it — who recommends piers for a horizontally cracked block wall — is recommending the wrong repair for the wrong problem. Lateral wall movement requires wall anchors, carbon fiber reinforcement, or other bracing solutions, not piers. A rep who makes this error in front of a customer who has done their research will lose the appointment and the company's credibility.The coaching approach for this checklist: before a rep's first independent pier appointment, have them complete this checklist in a conversation with a manager or senior rep, explaining each item rather than just checking it. The explanations reveal gaps in understanding that a simple checkbox cannot surface. 'Can you explain the load transfer principle in plain language?' is a very different question from 'do you understand load transfer?'A useful pre-appointment role play: have the manager play a customer who asks 'Do I really need piers, or is this just cosmetic?' and have the rep respond using only the specific inspection evidence — no general statements about the severity of the problem. If the rep cannot construct a specific, evidence-based response to that question, they are not ready for an independent pier appointment.

#### Slide 12

The rep who
earns trust in
a pier appoint-
ment earns it
for life.



The customer in a pier appointment is making the most significant single repair decision of their homeownership. The rep who brings genuine expertise, honest framing, and clear communication walks out with a close — and a customer who will refer everyone they know.

Next Job Type: Crawl Space Drainage

### Notes:
CLOSING SLIDE — SPEAKER NOTESClose this training session with the insight that pier appointments produce the strongest referral outcomes of any job type Apex performs — when they are handled correctly.Here's why. A customer who is frightened about their home's foundation and who walks through the appointment with a rep who explains what is happening, why it is happening, what the repair does, and what the realistic outcomes are, experiences something that is unfortunately rare in the home services industry: a professional who treated them with respect and gave them the complete picture. That experience is memorable. That experience gets shared.Conversely, a pier appointment that is handled with vague reassurances, unrealistic promises about what the lift will accomplish, or inadequate pre-job communication about excavation and disruption produces the kind of customer disappointment that generates online reviews — the negative kind. Pier jobs are high-stakes in both directions.The training challenge for managers: pier appointments should be the last job type a new rep handles independently, not one of the first. The complexity of the diagnostic work, the emotional weight of the customer conversation, and the technical specificity required to handle the push vs. helical pier question confidently all require a foundation of field experience that newer reps have not yet built. Use the other job type decks and actual field exposure before sending a rep into an independent pier appointment.Next up: Crawl Space Drainage — covering interior drainage channels, sump pumps, and the relationship between bulk water management and the encapsulation system.

### Notes:
HOW TO USE THIS DECKThis is a field reference, not a training curriculum. The job type decks and sales process modules are where reps learn the underlying material. This deck is what they use to prepare for specific appointments, rehearse responses before getting in the car, and what managers use to run structured drills in team meetings.Organized by objection category, not job type — most objections appear across all five job types. Each slide has three columns: what the objection really means (the underlying concern), what to say, and what to avoid.For drills: one objection per session, 10 minutes, consistent over time. The 'what to avoid' column is often the most useful — many reps know what they should say but default to the avoid column under pressure.

#### Slide 2

The Objection Mindset
What an objection actually is — and why that reframe changes everything
“

An objection is not a rejection. It is a request for more — more information, more evidence, more reassurance, or more time. The customer who objects is still in the conversation. The customer who says nothing and leaves is the one you lost.

Diagnose Before Responding
Evidence, Not Argument




Most objections have a surface statement and an underlying concern. 'It's too expensive' might mean 'I don't see the value yet' or 'I need my spouse involved.' The response that works depends on which is actually operating. Ask a question before giving an answer.
Arguing with an objection — even correctly — produces defensiveness. A customer who hears 'actually it is serious' feels challenged. A customer who sees the moisture reading you took has their perception updated by evidence. Evidence is always more persuasive than assertion.

Acknowledge First, Always
Inform, Never Pressure




Every response should begin with genuine acknowledgment before any content. 'That's a fair question.' 'I understand why you'd want to compare.' The 'I understand, but' structure erases the acknowledgment. Acknowledgment is not agreement — it signals that you heard the concern seriously.
Pressure creates resistance. A customer who feels pushed will find reasons to say no even when the logic says yes. The goal is to give the customer the accurate, complete information they need to decide confidently — and then let them decide. Reps who close by informing close more and generate better referrals.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  THE OBJECTION MINDSET

### Notes:
MINDSET — SPEAKER NOTESThe reframe in the pull quote is the most important thing in the deck. An objection is a request.On diagnosing first: the rep who hears 'it's too expensive' and immediately launches into value justification is guessing. That guess may be wrong. Ask before responding.On acknowledgment: practice writing the acknowledgment sentence first, before any response content. If it contains the word 'but,' rewrite it. 'I understand, but...' negates the acknowledgment entirely.

#### Slide 3

Price &

CATEGORY  1


Budget

The most common category — and the most commonly mishandled



### Notes:

#### Slide 4

"It's too expensive." / "That's more than I expected."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
Usually not about the number — it's about perceived value. The customer doesn't yet see clearly enough why the repair is worth what it costs. Sometimes it's financial anxiety that has nothing to do with Apex's pricing specifically.
Don't defend the price — expand the value first. Connect the cost to what it prevents: 'I understand it's a significant number. Let me walk through what this addresses and what the alternative progression looks like.' Connect to documented damage, secondary repairs it prevents, or home equity protected. Then ask: 'Is it the total that concerns you, or is it more about timing and cash flow?' That answer tells you what you're actually dealing with.
Immediately discounting, apologizing for the price, or comparing to competitors before establishing value. Discounting before understanding the real concern tells the customer the price was arbitrary — and that pushing back is always worth trying.

"I need to think about it."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
The most ambiguous objection. Can mean: 'I'm not convinced yet,' 'I need my spouse involved,' 'I'm comparing quotes,' or 'I want to say no without conflict.' Treating all of these identically produces failure in most cases.
Ask the diagnostic question first: 'Of course — can you help me understand what part you're still working through? Is it the scope, the timing, or something else?' Spouse conversation: offer to come back together. Comparison: help them compare accurately. Severity doubt: return to the inspection evidence. Never leave without a specific agreed-upon next step.
Saying 'okay, take your time' and leaving without establishing a next action. This produces weeks of non-responsive follow-up on a job that is likely already lost.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  PRICE & BUDGET OBJECTIONS

### Notes:

#### Slide 5

"Can you do it for less?" / "Is there a discount?"

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
Sometimes a genuine budget constraint. More often a test — checking whether the price was arbitrary and whether the rep will cave. How the rep responds tells the customer a great deal about the company's credibility.
Acknowledge, then hold the scope: 'I want to make sure you're getting the best value. Let me explain what each component does and why it's in the proposal, then we can talk about whether financing makes the numbers work better.' Offer Upgrade or GoodLeap as alternatives to reducing scope. If scope reductions are possible, explain the specific trade-off explicitly before making any change.
Reflexively offering a percentage off to end the discomfort of the objection. This communicates the original price was inflated. Any reduction should be tied to a specific, articulable scope change the customer explicitly accepts after understanding the consequence.

"I want to get a couple of other quotes first."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
Reasonable and legitimate — the customer wants to confirm they're not being overcharged. This is a process preference to respect, not an objection to overcome. The rep's job is to ensure the comparison happens on a level playing field.
Encourage it specifically: 'Absolutely — that's the smart move. When comparing, here are the things worth asking each company: What component type are they proposing and why? Does the scope include [critical piece]? Is the warranty transferable? A lower number on a scope missing key components isn't the same repair.' A verbal comparison checklist ensures Apex's complete scope is compared to complete scopes.
Acting defensive or threatened. Offering to 'match any quote' before seeing what's in it. The rep who actively helps a customer compare intelligently almost always wins the comparison, because the comparison reveals scope differences that justify Apex's pricing.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  PRICE & BUDGET OBJECTIONS

### Notes:

#### Slide 6

Urgency &

CATEGORY  2


Timing

The customer isn't saying no — they're asking for a reason to act now



### Notes:

#### Slide 7

"I want to wait and see if it gets worse."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
The customer doesn't see a meaningful cost to waiting. They're weighing the certain cost of acting now against the uncertain cost of inaction. The rep hasn't made the 'don't act' cost sufficiently concrete.
Make the waiting cost specific, not general: 'The moisture reading I took today was 24%. The wood rot threshold is 28%. The conditions producing that reading will continue every wet season — this doesn't self-correct.' Then describe the next stage of damage and its cost. Each job type has a specific mechanism of progression — use it rather than generic urgency statements.
Vague statements like 'these things always get worse.' Both sound like pressure tactics. Effective urgency comes from the documented, specific mechanism of progression for the job type at hand — not from the rep's opinion.

"It's been like this for years — it hasn't fallen down yet."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
The customer is using the absence of catastrophic failure as evidence of safety. This is survivorship bias. Past stability does not prove future stability — the rep needs to explain why without sounding alarmist.
Validate the observation before reframing: 'You're right — the home is standing. What I want to add is what today's measurements tell us about where you are on the progression curve. The soil condition driving the movement hasn't changed. My job is to tell you where you are on that curve and what it looks like as it continues, not to predict when you cross a threshold.'
Agreeing it's probably fine. Downplaying findings to close an easier job. Both create liability for Apex and genuinely fail the customer. Document what you find, explain it accurately, and let the customer decide with the full picture.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  URGENCY & TIMING OBJECTIONS

### Notes:

#### Slide 8

"Maybe next season / after the holidays / in the spring."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
A timing deflection that may or may not represent a genuine constraint. Unless there's a real budget or scheduling reason, 'next season' is often a polite no-in-progress.
Acknowledge first, then ask the diagnostic question: 'I understand — timing matters. Can I ask: is there something specific about the spring timeline, or is it more about wanting more time to think through the decision?' If it's genuine timing, offer a scheduling hold and a specific follow-up date. If it's decision anxiety, identify what needs resolution and address that directly.
Accepting a vague future date and leaving with no clarity on what changes between now and then. 'I'll call you in April' with no agreed next action produces months of ineffective follow-up on a job that may already be lost.

"My finances are tight right now — maybe in a few months."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
The timing objection with a genuine constraint attached. This is the most directly solvable timing objection, because specific financial tools exist for exactly this situation.
Take the constraint seriously, then present the solution: 'I completely understand. We offer two financing options — Upgrade and GoodLeap — that many of our customers use for exactly this situation. Depending on approval, you could have this done now with monthly payments that are often far more manageable than the lump sum. Would it be worth checking while I'm here? Five minutes, no obligation.' Always present the monthly figure, not the total.
Saying 'I understand' and leaving without presenting the financing option. Many customers assume they must pay cash and don't know financing is available. The rep who doesn't offer leaves the customer sitting on a solvable problem.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  URGENCY & TIMING OBJECTIONS

### Notes:

#### Slide 9

Trust &

CATEGORY  3


Credibility

Not about doubting the rep personally — about needing evidence to feel confident



### Notes:

#### Slide 10

"I want a second opinion / I want an engineer to look at it."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
The highest-quality trust objection — the customer takes the issue seriously enough to want independent professional verification. Any resistance communicates that Apex is afraid of independent review.
Encourage it genuinely: 'That's exactly the right instinct, and I fully support it. I'd suggest taking our inspection report and photos to the engineer so they have the same documentation I'm working from. If there's anything specific you want them to evaluate, let me know and I'll give you the technical details to share.' Welcoming scrutiny is itself the most powerful credibility signal available.
Any hint of resistance — minimizing ('you don't really need an engineer for this'), slowing the timeline, or suggesting the engineer will just confirm what Apex said. Any defensiveness at all permanently destroys trust with the analytical customer who asked this question.

"I've never heard of your company." / "How long have you been in business?"

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
A request for proof of credibility and longevity. The customer wants to know they're dealing with a legitimate company that will stand behind its work. A reasonable question that deserves a direct, confident answer.
Answer specifically and confidently — company history, service area, completed jobs, warranty terms — then pivot to the most powerful credibility evidence: 'I have a reference book of before-and-after photos from jobs we've done in [city/neighborhood], with reviews from those customers. Would you like to look through it?' The referral book converts a brand credibility question into social proof from the customer's own community.
Defensive responses ('we may not be a national chain, but...'). Pivoting to the pitch before answering the question. Answer directly, then let the proof do the work.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  TRUST & CREDIBILITY OBJECTIONS

### Notes:

#### Slide 11

"My neighbor / brother / contractor said it wasn't that serious."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
The customer has received conflicting information from a trusted source. This is a social trust conflict. They trust their neighbor. The rep is a stranger asking them to override that trust. Arguing with the neighbor's assessment forces the customer to choose sides.
Don't argue the neighbor — redirect to the specific measurements: 'I can see why their perspective matters. What I can do is share what I specifically measured in your home today, because your conditions may differ from theirs. The reading I got was [X]. The threshold for elevated risk is [Y]. Whether that warrants action is your call — I want the decision based on what I found here, not on a general impression from a different situation.'
Directly contradicting the trusted person or implying they're uninformed. This forces the customer to defend their relationship and they will choose their neighbor every time. Let the evidence create a new reference point without requiring anyone else to be wrong.

"I had another company look at it last year and they said it was fine."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
A prior professional assessment is being presented as conflicting evidence. The customer may be testing whether the rep will hold their position, or may genuinely be confused by competing opinions.
Take it seriously: 'I'd be interested to understand what they looked at — it's possible they evaluated a different aspect, or conditions have changed. What specifically did they assess? Did they take moisture readings / measure crack displacement?' In most cases the prior assessment was more general or addressed a different concern. Use the comparison to demonstrate the specificity of today's findings.
Telling the customer the other company was wrong, incompetent, or lowballing them. Frame the comparison as a question of scope and recency, not as a judgment about the prior contractor.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  TRUST & CREDIBILITY OBJECTIONS

### Notes:

#### Slide 12

Technical

CATEGORY  4


Doubt

The customer who has done research — and needs a rep who has done more



### Notes:

#### Slide 13

"Do I really need all of this, or is it a sales pitch?"

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
A direct challenge to the rep's credibility and the appropriateness of the recommendation. The customer is asking whether the scope is driven by their actual needs or by commission. This objection most directly rewards genuine technical knowledge.
Answer directly and evidence-first: 'That's exactly the right question. Everything in this proposal is tied to something I specifically documented in your home today. [Component A] is here because of [specific finding]. [Component B] is here because of [specific finding]. I can walk you through the documentation for each line item so you can see exactly what drove each recommendation.' The evidence is the answer — not credentials, not company history.
Getting defensive. Leading with credentials before the evidence. Any response that sounds like 'trust me' rather than 'look at what I found' confirms the customer's suspicion that this is a pitch.

"I read online that [alternative approach] works just as well."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
The customer has done research and arrived at a different conclusion. This is a technical credibility challenge — they are asking the rep to engage with their specific research, not dismiss online sources in general.
Engage with the specific thing they read: 'What specifically did you come across? I'd like to understand what you're comparing, because the right answer depends on the specifics.' Then address it accurately — including if the alternative is legitimately valid in some conditions: 'That approach works well when [condition]. In your situation, the specific issue is [finding], which is why [recommended approach] is more appropriate here.'
Dismissing online research as unreliable. Giving a vague answer. Both destroy credibility with the customer who has done homework. Engaging specifically with what they found, accurately and without defensiveness, is what earns trust.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  TECHNICAL DOUBT OBJECTIONS

### Notes:

#### Slide 14

"Will this actually last? I've heard these repairs don't hold."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
The customer has prior experience with, or has heard about, foundation repairs that failed. This is a legitimate concern. The rep's answer needs to be specific about why properly executed work lasts versus what causes failures.
Acknowledge and explain the mechanism: 'That's a completely fair concern. The repairs that fail almost always do so for one of two reasons: the root cause wasn't addressed — so the same conditions produced the same problem again — or the scope was incomplete, leaving gaps in the system. In your proposal, [cause] is addressed by [component]. [Specific component] ensures long-term function by [specific mechanism]. The warranty backs the installation.'
Generic assurances like 'our work is built to last' or 'we've never had a failure.' The customer who has seen failures wants a specific explanation of what makes this different, not a promise. Specificity is the only credible response.

"I don't think it's that serious — I'm not seeing any symptoms."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
The customer's perception of severity is based on visible symptoms, not on measured conditions. Common in encapsulation and drainage appointments where the damage is below the floor or inside the wall.
Connect the invisible evidence to a visible reference point: 'The reason this matters even though you can't see it is [specific mechanism — e.g., 40–50% of your first-floor air comes from that crawl space]. The moisture reading I documented — [X]% — is above the threshold where [specific consequence] becomes active. The absence of visible symptoms means it hasn't yet reached a stage where symptoms have made it upstairs.' Show the photos. Show the meter readings.
Insisting the problem is serious without explaining why the customer can't see it. This sounds like manufactured urgency. The invisible evidence needs to be made concrete and specific to be credible.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  TECHNICAL DOUBT OBJECTIONS

### Notes:

#### Slide 15

Competitor &

CATEGORY  5


Comparison

Compare on scope, not on rhetoric — let the difference speak for itself



### Notes:

#### Slide 16

"Another company quoted me significantly less."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
There is a real price difference, and the customer wants to understand it. They can't yet evaluate whether it reflects a scope difference, a quality difference, or market variation. This is an invitation to make the comparison transparent.
Ask about their scope specifically: 'I'd like to understand what they're including — did their scope include [battery backup / specific component / transferable warranty]? The price difference almost always comes down to scope. Let me walk you through each component in ours and what it does, and then you can compare component by component.' Let the scope comparison do the work, not rhetoric.
Dismissing the competitor's price without understanding their scope. Saying 'you get what you pay for' without explaining specifically what Apex's scope includes. Competing on rhetoric rather than scope content makes Apex look defensive, not confident.

"My neighbor used [Competitor] and was really happy."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
Social proof from a trusted peer is one of the most powerful purchase influences available. This is not primarily a technical comparison — it's a trust comparison. The customer is implicitly asking whether Apex can match or exceed the experience their neighbor had.
Validate the neighbor's experience genuinely: 'That's great to hear — a good experience with any contractor in this space helps customers feel comfortable. What I'd like to do is show you the work we've done in your neighborhood.' Pull out the referral book, flip to work done nearby, and let local social proof match local social proof. Then offer to provide references from customers in similar situations to theirs.
Criticizing the competitor or suggesting the neighbor was lucky. Social proof works both ways — a rep who disrespects the customer's trusted peer loses the social trust the customer was prepared to extend.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  COMPETITOR & COMPARISON OBJECTIONS

### Notes:

#### Slide 17

"I've already had this done before and the problem came back."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
The most frustrated and skeptical customer in the objection set. They have spent money on a repair that didn't hold, and they are evaluating whether this is just another one that will fail. Their trust threshold is high. The opportunity here is enormous if handled correctly.
Acknowledge the frustration first — genuinely: 'I can hear that, and I'm sorry that happened — spending money on a repair that didn't hold is genuinely frustrating.' Then ask what was done: 'Can you tell me what was installed?' In most cases, the previous work was either an incomplete system or addressed the symptom without the cause. Explain the specific gap — not criticizing the prior company — and show how today's proposal closes it.
Jumping immediately to the new proposal without acknowledging the history. Telling the customer the previous company did bad work without understanding what was installed. Minimizing their frustration by moving past it too quickly. The burned customer needs to feel heard before they can hear anything else.

"[Competitor] said they don't recommend [Apex's proposed approach]."

What it really means

What to say

What to avoid
A competitor has directly contradicted the Apex recommendation. The customer is caught between two professional opinions and is looking for a reason to trust one over the other. The rep who responds with the most specific, evidence-based explanation wins.
Engage the disagreement directly: 'I'd like to understand what they recommended instead and why. What specifically did they say they'd do instead?' Then compare on the specific merits for this customer's documented conditions: 'Their approach works well when [condition]. What I found in your home is [specific finding], which is why I recommended [approach] — here's the specific technical reason.' Let the technical comparison be the closer.
Dismissing the competitor's recommendation without engaging with it specifically. Telling the customer the competitor is wrong without explaining why. The customer wants to understand the difference, not watch two companies argue. The rep who explains the difference specifically and calmly wins.



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  COMPETITOR & COMPARISON OBJECTIONS

### Notes:

#### Slide 18

What the objection signals — and which response category applies

Ask what specifically needs more thought → respond to that

Due diligence — reasonable and legitimate
Equip them to compare accurately → scope checklist

"I'll wait and see"
No clear cost to waiting — urgency hasn't been made concrete
Specific mechanism of progression → next damage stage + cost

Survivorship bias — past stability ≠ future stability

APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  PATTERN QUICK REFERENCE

### Notes:
QUICK REFERENCE — HOW TO USEThis is the fast-lookup tool for pre-appointment prep. Before an appointment, scan the left column and identify which objections are most likely for this customer type. Review the response approach column to make sure the strategy is fresh.For manager drills: cover the right two columns, have the rep name the signal and response approach from the objection alone. If they can do this consistently across all ten rows, they're field-ready.

#### Slide 19

Manager-Led Objection Drills
A repeatable 10-minute structure for turning this reference deck into field-ready skills

The 10-Minute Drill Format

What to Watch For in Drills
Pick one objection. Announce it to the team — 'Today we're working on the second-opinion objection.'
▸  The immediate pivot:  Rep hears the objection and goes straight to content without acknowledging. Almost always makes the customer more defensive, not less.

1
Give reps 60 seconds to mentally prepare — no notes, no looking at the deck.
▸  The vague urgency:  Rep says 'this will get worse' without citing a specific mechanism. Sounds like pressure, not evidence. Coach to the specific progression for the job type.

2
One rep delivers the response live while the manager plays the customer. Keep it realistic — the customer doesn't immediately cave.
▸  The defensive posture:  Rep becomes slightly argumentative when challenged on price or recommendation. Coach to curiosity — ask a question instead of defending.

3
Debrief using the three columns: Did they acknowledge first? Did they diagnose before responding? Did they use evidence or assertion? Did they do anything in the 'avoid' column?
▸  The premature discount:  Rep offers a price reduction at the first hint of cost resistance, before understanding the actual objection. Kills margin and credibility simultaneously.

4
Second rep delivers an improved version incorporating the debrief feedback. The comparison is the learning moment.
▸  The abandoned close:  'I need to think about it' gets met with 'okay, take your time' and the rep leaves. No agreed next action. Coach to always leave with a specific next step.

5
30-second summary of the core principle for this objection. Done.
▸  The honest tell:  Rep does all three right — acknowledges, diagnoses, uses evidence — and the customer visibly relaxes and re-engages. Reinforce this moment loudly.

6



APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS  ·  MANAGER-LED OBJECTION DRILLS

### Notes:
DRILL FRAMEWORK — SPEAKER NOTESThe drill format turns a reference deck into a skill-building tool. Reading about objection handling and doing it under simulated pressure are completely different experiences. Most reps who have read this deck will still default to the 'avoid' column behaviors when they feel the social pressure of a live objection. Only practice changes that.The most important element of the debrief is the comparison between the first and second rep's delivery. That comparison makes the improvement concrete rather than abstract.Calendar discipline: 10 minutes at the start of one team meeting per week, one objection per session, means the team covers all 16 objections in four months. After that, rotate back and have reps deliver responses cold — no prep, no notes. The reps who internalize the three principles (acknowledge, diagnose, evidence) will handle novel objections they've never seen before using the same framework.

#### Slide 20

The close isn't
a technique.
It's the natural
result of the
right conversation.



Every objection in this deck has one correct answer: more of the right information, delivered with genuine acknowledgment and backed by specific evidence. The rep who does that consistently doesn't overcome objections. They make them unnecessary.

### Notes:
CLOSING — SPEAKER NOTESThe closing message is the most important reframe in the deck: the close is not a technique at the end of an appointment. It is the accumulated result of everything that came before — the inspection documentation, the technical explanation, the acknowledgment of concerns, the evidence-based responses to objections.A rep who has done all of those things well will find that most appointments close without a formal close. The customer has been given everything they need to make a confident decision.The best investment in closing performance is improving everything before the close, not improving the close itself.

INTERNAL USE ONLY  |  APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS

### Notes:

#### Slide 3

THE MARKET
Why This Region Is Different
The Panhandle and South Alabama produce foundation problems at a higher rate than most of the country because of a specific combination of soil, climate, and housing stock. Understanding the ‘why’ makes you more credible than any competitor.

High Humidity Year-Round
Sandy & Organic Soils




Annual outdoor RH regularly exceeds 70–80%. Crawl spaces without vapor control sustain active mold growth every month, not just seasonally.
Loose sandy soils beneath slabs erode rapidly when water flows. Organic-bearing soils under older homes compact and settle over time as organics decompose.

Crawl Space Dominant Housing
Storm & Surface Water




The largest share of the housing stock here is crawl space construction — the highest-value, highest-frequency job type in our portfolio.
Tropical rainfall events concentrate enormous volumes of water near foundations. Poor lot grading is endemic in this market. Water finds every crawl space.


INTERNAL USE ONLY  |  APEX FOUNDATION SPECIALISTS

Expanding polyurethane foam injected beneath settled concrete to lift and stabilize slabs and driveways.
Push or helical piers driven to load-bearing soil to stop foundation settlement and, where possible, restore elevation.
Interior perimeter drain channel, sump pump, and discharge system to manage bulk water in crawl spaces.
Interior drainage for finished and unfinished basements. Includes wall gap detail and sump system.

#### Slide 8
